201 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Merrill v. Department of Motor Vehicles


L. A. No. 29627


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 907; 458 P.2d 33; 80 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 296



September 3, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Robert W. Kenny, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Department of Motor Vehicles to annul its decision refusing an application for a motor vehicle dealer's license.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment granting writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the California Department of Motor Vehicles (Department), sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which granted a writ of mandamus commanding it to set aside its decision that denied an application for a motor vehicle dealer's license that plaintiff, a membership discount house, filed.


OVERVIEW: The membership discount house offered new automobiles for sale to its members at reduced rates. The Department notified the house that it would need a motor vehicle dealer's license to sell cars. After the Department denied the application for a license, the house filed a Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1094.5 petition for a writ of mandate to review the denial. The trial court granted the petition. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment granting the writ of mandate directing the Department to set aside its denial of the license. The court held that the Department's grounds for denial of the license were legally insufficient because the discount house was a bona fide dealer within the meaning of the California Vehicle Code. The court found that the discount house was a bona fide dealer because the legislature had set forth an extremely broad definition of the term "dealer" in the vehicle code and the dominant concern of the scheme was to protect the public from irresponsible and unscrupulous persons in the sale of automobiles. The court found that the discount house was not lacking in the qualities of honesty and reliability with which the statute concerned itself.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that granted a writ of mandate in favor of the membership discount house and directed the Department to set aside its denial of the application for a motor vehicle dealer's license.

 

202 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Spencer


Crim. No. 13249


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 933; 458 P.2d 43; 80 Cal. Rptr. 99; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 298



September 3, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County. J. Kelly Steele, Judge.

Prosecution for manslaughter.



DISPOSITION: Attempted appeal from verdict dismissed; judgment reversed. Judgment of conviction of voluntary manslaughter reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Kern County (California) convicting her of voluntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of voluntary manslaughter after the trial court determined that she killed her lesbian lover. On appeal, the court found that the evidence was clearly sufficient to sustain the trial court's conclusion that defendant killed the victim upon a sudden quarrel or heat of passion. However, the court found that the trial court erred in giving a witness's prior inconsistent statement substantive use. The state failed to demonstrate that the error was harmless to defendant beyond a reasonable doubt. The statement concerned the conduct of defendant on the night of the killing. The witness was the only person who gave testimony as to the events immediately preceding the killing. The possibility that the statement contributed to defendant's conviction compelled the court to hold the error prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The order convicting defendant of voluntary manslaughter was reversed.

 

203 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


Crim. No. 10801


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 614; 456 P.2d 633; 79 Cal. Rptr. 65; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 276



July 7, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petitions of the Appellant and the Respondent for a Rehearing were Denied September 17, 1969. Peek, J., * Sat in Place of Mosk, J., who Deemed Himself Disqualified. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the Opinion that the Respondent's Petition Should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder with the penalty fixed at death affirmed as to guilt and reversed as to penalty.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which, upon a jury verdict, convicted defendant of first degree murder. The jury returned the death penalty. Defendant contended that his statements were improperly admitted into evidence and that he was denied a speedy trial. Defendant also asserted the death penalty was improperly imposed because certain veniremen were wrongfully excluded.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and the jury returned the death penalty. Defendant contended that his statements to the police officers were improperly admitted into evidence because he received no constitutional warning. Defendant also argued that he was denied a speedy trial. In addition, defendant asserted that the exclusion of prospective jurors was contrary to standards required in death penalty cases. The court affirmed the defendant's conviction for first degree murder because, although the warnings that were given were arguably technically insufficient, the introduction of the statements was harmless error, where the statements did not constitute a confession and their introduction was not prejudicial. The court also determined that, in the absence of actual prejudice, defendant cannot complain that he was denied his constitutional right to a speedy trial. The court, however, reversed the imposition of the death penalty because the questions put to two of the jurors excused for cause and the jurors' responses presented reasonable doubt just what their positions were with respect to imposing the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree murder, finding that the admission of defendant's statements to police officers was harmless error, even if the warnings were technically insufficient, and that defendant's right to a speedy trial was not violated where defendant was not prejudiced. The court, however, reversed the imposition of the death penalty because veniremen voicing objections to the death penalty were excluded.

 

204 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pike


Crim. No. 12378


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 595; 455 P.2d 776; 78 Cal. Rptr. 672; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 275



July 2, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment opposing death penalty affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the imposition of the death sentence by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for his conviction for murder in the first degree and robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's case was remanded for retrial in habeas corpus proceedings with respect to the penalty phase only. The jury entered a death sentence. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that it was not reversible error to allow challenge for cause to jurors who had general objections to or expressed conscientious or religious objections to the death penalty because the voir dire conducted demonstrated that the challenged jurors unequivocally stated they could not vote for the death penalty under any circumstances. The court found that defendant could not argue that he unfairly testified on his own behalf because he thought his incriminating statements to police would be introduced. The court finally held that defendant could not argue that admission of witness testimony in the form of deposition denied him his right to confrontation when his counsel previously stipulated to such admission. The court noted that defendant could have sought to exclude the testimony on the grounds that the witnesses were not unavailable under Cal. Evid. Code §§ 1200, 1291, 240, or requested a continuance until the sick witnesses could testify under Cal. Penal Code § 1050.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence for his conviction for murder in the first degree and robbery.

 

205 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Purdy & Fitzpatrick v. State


L. A. Nos. 29602, 29603


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 566; 456 P.2d 645; 79 Cal. Rptr. 77; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 273; 2 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 415; 38 A.L.R.3d 1194; 60 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P52,119; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9987



July 1, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of San Diego County. George A. Lazar, Judge.

Actions to declare Lab. Code, § 1850, unconstitutional or otherwise invalid and to recover certain penalties paid for the alleged violations of the section.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgments for plaintiffs affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of California sought review of summary judgments from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in favor of plaintiffs, contractors on public works, in their actions to declare Cal. Lab. Code § 1850 unconstitutional or otherwise invalid and to recover penalties paid for the alleged violations of the section.


OVERVIEW: These cases involved a challenge to Cal. Lab. Code § 1850, which basically prohibited the employment of aliens on public works. The court held that § 1850 and related sections could not stand. The discrimination involved denied arbitrarily to certain persons, merely because of their status as aliens, the right to pursue an otherwise lawful occupation. The classification within the statutory scheme operated irrationally without reference to any legitimate state interest except that of favoring United States citizens over citizens of other countries. This latter objective did not reflect such a compelling state interest that it would permit the court to sustain this kind of discrimination. Because § 1850 offended the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, that section was declared to be void. To the extent that it conflicted with its present holding, the court overruled its decision in City of Pasadena v. Charleville, 215 Cal. 384, 10 P.2d 745 (1932), and specifically rejected the language that "the right of any legislative body in this state to prohibit the employment of alien Chinese on public works may not be questioned."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgments for plaintiffs, contractors on public works, insofar as they voided sections of the California Labor Code for encroaching upon the congressional scheme for immigration and naturalization and offending the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, and affirmed an award of damages for penalties assessed under the sections against the Division of Highways of the State Department of Public Works.

 

206 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mabry


Crim. No. 11510


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 430; 455 P.2d 759; 78 Cal. Rptr. 655; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 266



June 26, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 20, 1969. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. William M. Gallagher, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder with the penalty fixed at death affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) convicting him of first degree murder of a police officer and imposing the death penalty. The automatic appeal was taken pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from the conviction for first degree murder of a police officer and the imposition of the death sentence. The court first found that the trial court properly instructed the jury regarding the first degree murder charge because the evidence clearly pointed to commission of the homicide in the perpetration of another felony. The court also concluded that defendant was not harmed by the trial court's corrective set of jury instructions. Nor was the marital privilege violated by the admission of witness testimony. The privilege could not be asserted because defendant's marriage to the witness was void. The court further held that tape recorded statements defendant made to undercover police were admissible, even though no Miranda warnings were given, because they were not made during custodial interrogation. Finally, the court ruled that there was sufficient evidence to uphold the trial court's exclusion of 13 members of the jury venire because their statements made it clear that they would vote against the imposition of capital punishment without regard to evidence, and that their death penalty views prevented them from impartially deciding defendant's guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of first degree murder of a police officer and imposing the death penalty.

 

207 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vaughn


Crim. No. 12425


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 406; 455 P.2d 122; 78 Cal. Rptr. 186; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 264



June 20, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. William M. Gallagher, Judge.

Prosecution of inmate serving a life term for assault with a deadly weapon on a correctional officer.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and affirmed in part. Judgment of conviction fixing the penalty at death reversed as to penalty; in all other respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's conviction for assault with a deadly weapon on a correctional officer and death sentence in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) was automatically appealed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to assault with a deadly weapon on a correctional officer. The court affirmed and found that the trial court excused for cause three prospective jurors because of their opposition to the death penalty. The court further found that two of the excused prospective jurors had made equivocal and ambiguous statements regarding their ability to impose the death penalty and that it was not unmistakably clear that these jurors would have automatically voted against the imposition of capital punishment without regard to any evidence that might have been developed at the trial. The court held that these jurors were improperly excused. The court further held that the trial court's instructions adequately informed the jury of the consequences of a verdict of life imprisonment, that to subject defendant to the death penalty for an assault that did not result in the death of the victim did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment upon him, that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in granting defendant's request that he be allowed to represent himself, and that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in failing to offer defendant a new attorney.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for assault with a deadly weapon on a correctional officer and reversed the trial court's imposition of the death penalty.

In re Allen


Crim. No. 12718


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 388; 455 P.2d 143; 78 Cal. Rptr. 207; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 262



June 19, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to attack as invalid one of the terms and conditions specified in a court order granting probation.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a habeas corpus proceeding to attack as invalid one of the terms and conditions specified in a court order granting probation.


OVERVIEW: The petitioner's sentence was suspended and she was placed on probation following her plea of guilty of possession of a restricted dangerous drug without prescription, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §11910. By application for habeas corpus, petitioner attacked as invalid one of the terms and conditions specified by the court in its order granting probation. The condition of probation under attack was the requirement that petitioner reimburse the county for a court-appointed counsel through the probation department. The court concluded that the imposition of the condition under attack constituted an impediment to the free exercise of a right guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. VI and, as with respect to other impediments or forms of compulsion against the exercise of such rights, may not be permitted by the courts.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ and directed the trial court to modify its order to eliminate the condition that petitioner shall reimburse the county for court-appointed counsel. In all other respects, the court denied the writ.

 

209 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Orr v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S. F. No. 22651


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 220; 454 P.2d 712; 77 Cal. Rptr. 816; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 247



May 29, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied June 25, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition by the Department of Motor Vehicles to restrain the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco from taking any further action to restore driving privileges and licenses to uninsured motorists and to declare certain Vehicle Code sections unconstitutional.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied; alternative writ discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, Department of Motor Vehicles, its director, and associated entities, sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) from taking any further action to restore driving privileges and licenses to uninsured motorists and to declare certain vehicle code sections unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The real parties in interest in an action against the department were uninsured drivers of vehicles which were involved in accidents. The department sent a notice to each driver to either post security as required by Cal. Veh. Code § 16020 or risk suspension of driving license under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 16080 and 16100. Each driver failed to post the security requested, and the department ordered that the driver's license of each be suspended. Based upon a petition for prohibition and mandate by the drivers, the department filed a petition for a writ of prohibition to restrain the court from taking further action. The court denied the writ, holding that before ordering suspension of the license of a driver involved in an accident the department should have determined whether there was a reasonable possibility that a judgment may be recovered against the driver, and so should have considered culpability. The court determined that an order suspending the license was subject to court review and that no undue burden would appear in requiring the department to consider the question of culpability in determining the reasonable possibility of a judgment for damages against the driver.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition of the department, its director, and associated entities, for a writ of prohibition.

 

210 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Berutko


Crim. No. 12695


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 84; 453 P.2d 721; 77 Cal. Rptr. 217; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 236



May 7, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 4, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Vernon P. Spencer, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of heroin and possession of heroin for sale.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for possession of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500 and for possession of heroin for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.5. The judgment was rendered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), and defendant's sentence was enhanced because the superior court found that defendant had been previously convicted of possession of heroin.


OVERVIEW: An informant disclosed to a police officer that defendant was selling heroin at a specified address. No search or arrest warrant was obtained, and a police officer placed defendant's apartment under surveillance. During the surveillance, the police officer observed defendant looking out of his window several times, and the police officer then move closer to the apartment and was able to see what appeared to be heroin through a small opening in a window. The officer obtained a key to the apartment from the apartment manager and entered the apartment unannounced and seized heroin in the apartment. The lower court convicted defendant of possession of heroin and of possession of heroin for sale, and the court reversed. The court noted that a case dealing with the validity of unannounced entries had been decided after defendant's trial and that the record was unclear as to the reasons for the unannounced entry. Therefore, a reversal was required to clarify the record. However, the court held that there was probable cause for defendant's arrest prior to the police officer's entry because the police officer had the right to use a public vantage point to peer through defendant's window.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions for possession of heroin and of possession of heroin for sale.

 

211 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Marks


Crim. No. 12822


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 31; 453 P.2d 441; 77 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 231



April 30, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure the release of petitioner confined in the California Rehabilitation Center under an order of commitment as a narcotics addict.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from the California Rehabilitation Center (CRC) where he was confined under an order of commitment as a narcotics addict pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051, after the Narcotic Addict Evaluation Authority (NAEA) suspended his outpatient status and ordered his return to the CRC because of his refusal to submit to drug testing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a convicted drug addict, challenged the NAEA's suspension of his outpatient status and the order returning him to the CRC on the grounds that he refused to submit to drug testing. It was unquestioned that petitioner's refusal was motivated by his adoption of the principles of Synanon, a treatment facility, that prohibited drug testing. Petitioner claimed that the NAEA acted without having received any reports that he engaged in misconduct on release, and that it was within the NAEA's discretion to allow other types of monitoring. The court denied petitioner's request for writ of habeas corpus. The court held that the NAEA had construed Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3152 to mean that outpatient drug testing was mandatory, and that any reference to the NAEA's discretion in § 3152 should be read to pertain to an addict's return to the CRC. The court found that a report was made to the NAEA, and that § 3152 did not require that the reports reveal any particular misdeed by an outpatient, but rather § 3152 was satisfied if the reports conveyed information from which the NAEA could reasonably conclude that return to the CRC was in the best interests of the addict and society.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause and denied petitioner's request for writ of habeas corpus that sought his release from the CRC drug treatment facility based on his refusal to submit to mandatory drug testing.

 

212 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Galvan v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S. F. No. 22642


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 851; 452 P.2d 930; 76 Cal. Rptr. 642; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 372



April 23, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco to grant an injunction restraining a city and county from enforcing a firearms control ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner resident brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) to grant an injunction restraining respondents city and county from enforcing a firearms control ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner resident brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent Superior Court to grant an injunction restraining city and county from enforcing a firearms control ordinance. Petitioner alleged that the ordinance, which required registration of all firearms within the city, was unconstitutional because, inter alia, it conflicted directly with state laws that regulated firearms and violated the federal right to bear arms. The court denied the writ of mandate because the ordinance was a valid exercise of local police power which was not in direct conflict with state laws or impliedly preempted by state laws concerning weapons, and it violated no provision of the United States or state Constitutions. The court held that the penal code prohibited licenses or permits, but it did not prohibit registration requirements, and the city registration requirement did not conflict with the permit and licensing provision. The statutes could not reasonably be said to show a general scheme for the regulation of the subject of gun registration, and there was no basis for a conclusion that there was a legislative intent to make the subject of gun registration immune from local regulation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of mandate because the city ordinance did not violate any provision of the state or United States constitutions, and was a valid exercise of police power that did not conflict with any state law. The court held that there was no legislative intent to make gun registration immune from regulation from local government.

 

213 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hill


Crim. No. 11316


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 678; 452 P.2d 329; 76 Cal. Rptr. 225; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 362



April 9, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Mark Brandler, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder with the penalty fixed at death affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of murder in the first degree and fixed the penalty at death. Defendant received an automatic appeal pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §1239(b) from a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which rendered judgment on the jury verdict, denied defendant's motion for a new trial, and denied defendant's motion to vacate the order denying a new trial based on newly discovered evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contacted the police to inform them that he was in the victim's home on the afternoon of her murder. At the end of his statement the police considered him a suspect and read him his rights. Defendant contended inter alia that his counsel was ineffective, that the lower court abused its discretion in denying his request to personally argue to the jury, and that his statements to the police were inadmissible. The court held that defendant failed to show that the alleged omissions of counsel indicating lack of preparation and incompetence resulted in the withdrawal of a crucial defense from the case. The court found that counsel's withdrawal of a question was a permissible trial tactic, that the omitted jury instruction did not withdraw a possible defense, and that counsel failed to call a witness because she was not material to his defense. The court held that absent a showing that justice would be served had defendant's request to argue before the jury been granted, the lower court did not abuse its discretion. The court also held that defendant's statements to the police prior to receiving the his Miranda warnings were admissible because he was not yet in custody.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction of first degree murder with the penalty fixed at death.

 

214 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. Leslie Salt Co.


S. F. Nos. 22616, 22617


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 627; 451 P.2d 406; 75 Cal. Rptr. 766; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 358



March 20, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 17, 1969, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Peters, J., Mosk, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of San Mateo County. James T. O'Keefe, Judge.

Actions by taxpayers attacking the manner in which municipal improvements and services were financed by a municipal improvement district.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of dismissal after demurrers to complaint sustained without leave to amend affirmed; motion to take evidence denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of an order from the Court of Appeal, First Appellate District, Division Four (California), which affirmed a trial court judgment dismissing his action attacking the manner in which respondents, company and individual, caused municipal improvements and services to be financed by the district to which the taxpayer belonged.


OVERVIEW: A special improvement district was established by a special act of the California Legislature. The funding for the district came from the taxpayer's district. The taxpayer filed an action challenging the constitutionality of the district. The taxpayer's amended complaint was dismissed and his request for further amendments was denied. The taxpayer appealed the decision, and it was affirmed by the court of appeals. The taxpayer filed another challenge that the court took under consideration. The court held that the taxpayer was precluded from contesting the validity of the proceedings leading to the formation of the district because this ability was reserved to the attorney general of the state. Once the district's existence was established, either de facto or de jure, it followed that the taxpayer, as a private person, was not able to contest the validity of the proceedings that led to its formation. Therefore, the court held that the taxpayer failed to state a claim, and his request to amend was properly denied because there was no reasonable probability that the defect could have been cured by amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding the dismissal of the taxpayer's action was affirmed, and the taxpayer's motion to take evidence was denied.

 

215 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Howard


Crim. No. 12408


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 618; 451 P.2d 401; 75 Cal. Rptr. 761; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 357



March 17, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of the Stockton Judicial District of San Joaquin County. Chris Papas, Judge.

Prosecution for diversion of construction funds received for the purpose of paying laborers and materialmen.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Municipal Court of the Stockton Judicial District of San Joaquin County (California) for diversion of construction funds received for the purpose of paying laborers and materialmen in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 484b.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 484b for failing to use construction funds for the payment of laborers and materialmen on a construction project. On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of § 494b and the sufficiency of the evidence against him. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, concluding that § 484b was not so indefinite as to violate the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. A reasonable construction of § 484b was that a reduction in equity or in security required a showing of a valid lien right by one who furnished services or materials to the job. Thus, it was sufficiently certain what conduct was prohibited. Defendant's conduct became criminal once the diversion of funds caused harm to the person providing them. Nor did Cal. Penal Code § 484b conflict with Cal. Const. art. I, § 15's prohibition against imprisonment for debts. The diversion of funds dealt unfairly with and prejudiced the rights of homeowners, which fell within the fraud exception to Cal. Const. art. I, § 15. Finally, sufficient evidence supported defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's conviction of defendant for diversion of construction funds.

 

216 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Varnum


Crim. No. 12041


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 480; 450 P.2d 553; 75 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 349



February 25, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. William B. Keene, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with intent to commit robbery, robbery, first degree murder and kidnaping for the purpose of robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him, on a jury verdict, of the offenses of assault with intent to commit robbery, first-degree murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and robbery, and that sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on charges of assault with intent to commit robbery, first-degree murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and robbery. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder conviction. On appeal, defendant alleged that evidence of other offenses not specifically charged in the information was improperly admitted, that the prosecutor committed prejudicial misconduct in arguing the deterrent effect of the death penalty, and that he was denied his right to an impartial jury due to the exclusion of a prospective juror for cause. The court affirmed, holding that other offenses not charged in the information were properly admitted in the penalty phase of the trial because those offenses were established by sufficiently corroborated evidence, that the jury was promptly and fully admonished as to effectively cure any harmful results of the prosecutor's remarks, and that the superior court correctly refused to give a Morse instruction because such charge would have confused the jury. Also, because one of the witnesses made it unmistakably clear that she would automatically vote against the death penalty, there was no error in excusing that juror for cause.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the superior court's judgment that convicted defendant of the offenses of assault with intent to commit robbery, first-degree murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and robbery, and that imposed a sentence of death.

 

217 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Dennis M.


Sac. No. 7819


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 444; 450 P.2d 296; 75 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 346



February 20, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Robert W. Cole, Judge.

Action in juvenile court to try a minor on charges of involuntary manslaughter and theft, to declare him a ward of the court and to commit him to the Youth Authority.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment declaring minor to be ward of the court and committing him to Youth Authority affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile challenged an order from the Superior Court of Sacramento County, California, which declared him to be a ward of the court and committed him to the youth authority after finding him guilty of involuntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile, after having been released from a juvenile offenders facility, stole a car and a .22 caliber pistol. While with his girlfriend, he carelessly pointed the pistol at her as he hugged her. The pistol went off and killed the girlfriend. The juvenile thought that the pistol was unloaded. At trial, the trial court admitted the juvenile's confession into evidence despite the juvenile's protests that he never voluntarily waived his right to silence because no parent, attorney, or interested adult was present when he consented to confess. The trial court applied a preponderance of the evidence standard of proof and found the juvenile guilty of involuntary manslaughter. On review, the court held that the preponderance of the evidence standard of proof set forth in Cal. Health & Safety Code § 701 was not "clearly, positively, and unmistakably" constitutional. The court held that the juvenile's conduct displayed a reckless disregard of human life and amply supported a finding of involuntary manslaughter. The court concluded that in viewing the entire, total circumstances, with the juvenile's prior experience with police, the juvenile effectively waived his right to silence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the trial court declaring the juvenile to be a ward of the court and finding him guilty of involuntary manslaughter.

 

218 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Curtis


Crim. No. 12665


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 347; 450 P.2d 33; 74 Cal. Rptr. 713; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 338



February 13, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 12, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County. Robert P. Sullivan, Judge.

Prosecution for battery upon a police officer.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which convicted defendant of battery upon a police officer in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 834a and 243, challenging the construction and constitutionality of the statutes as applied to his allegedly unlawful arrest.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for battery upon a police officer, challenging the construction and constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code §§ 834a and 243 as applied to his allegedly unlawful arrest. Defendant was arrested when an officer, investigating a report of a prowler, accosted him based on a cursory description of the suspected prowler. After the officer told defendant he was under arrest, a violent struggle ensued. Defendant contended that his arrest was unlawful for lack of probable cause, and therefore his resistance was justified. The court affirmed that, while a person could not use force to resist arrest, lawful or unlawful, reasonable force was allowed in defense against excessive force. Where an arrest was proven to be unlawful, a defendant could be convicted only of a misdemeanor. The court therefore reversed defendant's conviction because the jury was not so instructed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction because the jury was not properly instructed that, if the arrest was proven to be unlawful, defendant could be convicted only of a misdemeanor for resisting the arrest.

 

219 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Pierpont Inn, Inc. v. State


L. A. No. 29583


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 282; 449 P.2d 737; 74 Cal. Rptr. 521; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 333



February 6, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County. W. Preston Butcher * and Louis C. Drapeau, + Judges.


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


+ Retired Justice of the Court of Appeal sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

Action in inverse condemnation.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a decision the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of respondent property owner in an action for inverse condemnation.


OVERVIEW: The property owner was the owner of a hotel consisting of a main building and guest cottages. The property owner's property was divided by a road which run north-south over the property. The state constructed a freeway on property owner's property. The trial court entered judgment in favor of property owner in an action for inverse condemnation. The state filed a motion contending that the property owner's claim was not timely filed and the action should have been barred. The court found that the state's contentions were not meritorious or supported by evidence and affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that when the state, with the full knowledge of property owner's asserted rights in the premises, determined to proceed without exercising its power of eminent domain in the manner prescribed by law, it could not have been hard to complain that the rules applicable to ordinary condemnation proceedings were not applied to the instant action which it forced upon property owner.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for property owner.

 

220 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Durham


Crim. No. 11317


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 171; 449 P.2d 198; 74 Cal. Rptr. 262; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 324



January 21, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Appellant Robinson for a Rehearing was Denied February 19, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying motion for a new trial. Mark Brandler, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgments of conviction imposing death penalty as to one defendant and life imprisonment as to one defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a shooter and an accomplice, appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sentenced defendant accomplice to life imprisonment and defendant shooter to death based on a jury's verdict finding defendants guilty of murder in the first degree. Defendant accomplice's motion for new trial was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a shooter and an accomplice, were pulled over for a traffic stop by city police. Defendant shooter shot and killed a police officer during the stop. At trial, evidence of defendants' pre-shooting crime spree was admitted. Defendants were convicted of murder in the first degree. Defendant accomplice was sentenced to life imprisonment. Defendant shooter was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed, ruling that sufficient evidence supported both convictions and sentences. As to defendant accomplice, the court ruled that respondent state properly utilized conspiracy principles, but was not required to prove a conspiracy to convict on its sole theory of aiding and abetting. As to defendant shooter, the court ruled that the probative value of his parole status and criminal activities outweighed any prejudicial effect. The court ruled that he received effective assistance of counsel and presented no evidence to the contrary. The court ruled that the probative value of his sodomy conviction in the penalty phase outweighed its prejudicial effects, that jury instructions were proper, and that certain jurors were properly excluded for cause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments convicting defendants. The court dismissed the attempted appeal of the denial of a motion for new trial as nonappealable.

 

221 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Risenhoover


Crim. No. 10708


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 39; 447 P.2d 925; 73 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 217



December 23, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County. Denver C. Peckinpah, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder with penalty fixed at death reversed as to penalty; in all other respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and death sentence for murder in the first-degree from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California). Defendant contended that the trial court erred in instructing the jury regarding the felony-murder rule under Cal. Penal Code § 189, that there was insufficient evidence to support the verdict, and that certain veniremen were improperly excused for cause based on their opposition to the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly killed the victim during an attempt to steal the victim's car. A jury found defendant sane and guilty of first degree murder and sentenced defendant to death. The trial court denied defendant's motion for a new trial and for reduction of the degree of the crime and modification of punishment. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction, but reversed defendant's sentence. The court held that (1) the jury was properly instructed regarding the felony-murder rule and the state's theory that the murder was premeditated because there was substantial evidence, including psychiatric testimony, that defendant was capable of forming and did form the intent to rob, that defendant killed the victim during an attempted robbery, and that the killing was a wilful, deliberate, and premeditated murder; (2) although some prospective jurors read an article regarding the trial, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's motion for a change of venue; and (3) certain veniremen were improperly excused for cause based on their opposition to the death penalty. Thus, the court set aside defendant's sentence and remanded for a new trial on the penalty issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder, but reversed defendant's death sentence. The court remanded to the trial court for a new trial limited to the issue of penalty.

 

222 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re McKinney


Crim. No. 12698


Supreme Court of California


70 Cal. 2d 8; 447 P.2d 972; 73 Cal. Rptr. 580; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 215



December 20, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner witness sought a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from custody after the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) found him in misdemeanor criminal contempt in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 166(6) and sentenced him to six months imprisonment for the violation.


OVERVIEW: A witness was found in criminal contempt of court in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 166(6) when he refused to answer questions as a defense witness at a trial for the murder of a police officer. The witness then filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging his imprisonment for the duration of the trial based on the contempt finding. The court held that (1) the lower court had inherent power to punish contempts of court, (2) the legislature could place reasonable limitations on that inherent power, (3) the lower court had had no specific jurisdiction to hear and convict the witness of the misdemeanor of contempt under the statute employed, and (4) because the writ had to be granted on jurisdictional grounds, there was no reason to rule on whether due process had been denied or that the witness' constitutional right not to incriminate himself had been violated.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of habeas corpus.

 

223 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Moore


Crim. No. 12428


Supreme Court of California


69 Cal. 2d 674; 446 P.2d 800; 72 Cal. Rptr. 800; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 269



November 20, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County committing defendant to a state institution for narcotic addiction. Robert Clifton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that committed him to a state institution for narcotic addiction, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3100 et seq., after a finding that he was in imminent danger of becoming addicted to narcotics.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that certain evidence was the result of an illegal search and should have been excluded. The court agreed and reversed the order of commitment. The court held that the exclusionary rule was applicable to narcotics addiction proceedings explaining that while such a proceeding may be labelled civil, there was a close identity to the aims and objectives of criminal law enforcement, and that to hold unconstitutionally obtained evidence admissible in the proceedings would furnish an incentive to violate the Fourth and Fourteenth Amendments. The court concluded that the officers were not justified in detaining and questioning defendant merely because the area was one where narcotic transactions had taken place in the past and defendant appeared nervous at seeing the officers' car. As such, the court concluded that the evidence obtained when in the course of the questioning defendant permitted the officers to inspect his arm was not admissible. The court then deemed the testimony of the physician that examined defendant after his arrest inadmissible as "fruit of the poisonous tree."


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of commitment.

 

224 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Anderson


Crim. Nos. 11572, 11573


Supreme Court of California


69 Cal. 2d 613; 447 P.2d 117; 73 Cal. Rptr. 21; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 268



November 18, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On December 11, 1968, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied December 18, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDINGS in habeas corpus to have petitioners' death sentences declared invalid.



DISPOSITION: Writs granted as to petitioners' penalty trials and petitioners remanded to the custody of trial courts for new trials on the issue of penalty only.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought habeas corpus relief to have their death sentences declared invalid, on the grounds that it was improper to excuse for cause veniremen conscientiously opposed to the death penalty were unconstitutional because they gave the jury unguided discretion to impose death or life imprisonment, the death penalty constituted cruel and unusual punishment, and petitioners were denied their right to counsel.


OVERVIEW: The court determined that petitioners' failure to object to the exclusion of the prospective jurors in question did not bar them from claiming error as a result of an intervening change in law. The United States Supreme Court ruled that a sentence of death could not be carried out if the jury that imposed or recommended it was chosen by excluding veniremen for cause simply because they voiced general objections to the death penalty or expressed conscientious or religious scruples against its infliction. The court determined that the broad language of the Supreme Court's opinion established without doubt that in no case could respondents be put to death where a venireman was excused for cause solely on the ground he was conscientiously opposed to the death penalty. Reversal was automatically required if a venireman was improperly excused for cause on the basis of his opposition to the death penalty. The court upheld the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code §§ 190 and 190.1 and the constitutionality of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial courts' judgments insofar as they related to the penalties imposed and remanded for new trials limited to the issue of penalties only.

 

225 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Giannini


Crim. No. 11446


Supreme Court of California


69 Cal. 2d 563; 446 P.2d 535; 72 Cal. Rptr. 655; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 266



November 14, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 11, 1968. McComb, J., Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a topless dancer and a nightclub manager, sought habeas corpus relief from criminal convictions affirmed by a department of the superior court (California).


OVERVIEW: A topless dancer and a nightclub manager were convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code §§ 314(1) and 647(a), willfully and lewdly exposing their person in a public place or in any place where there are present other persons to be offended or annoyed thereby, and disorderly conduct. Petitioners' convictions were affirmed and they filed a writ of habeas corpus. The court granted the writ and held that a dance performed before an audience for entertainment could not be held to violate the statutory prohibitions of indecent exposure and lewd or dissolute conduct in the absence of proof that the dance, tested in the context of contemporary community standards, appealed to the prurient interest of the audience and affronted standards of decency generally accepted in the community. The court found that the dance was protected by U.S. Const. amend. I until it was shown to be obscene, which required a showing that the dance affronted current community standards. The court found that the prosecution offered no proof of contemporary community standards, the community being the State of California. The court set aside the convictions based on this lack of proof.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioners' writ of habeas corpus and set aside their convictions.

In re Harris


Crim. No. 12308


Supreme Court of California


69 Cal. 2d 486; 446 P.2d 148; 72 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 258



October 30, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from constructive custody of bail under civil arrest.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner civil arrestee proceeded in the court on habeas corpus to secure his release from constructive custody of bail under a civil arrest order of the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California). The civil arrestee questioned the constitutionality of the statutes that authorized arrest and imprisonment on an ex parte application of plaintiff creditor in a civil action.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner agreed to buy a truck tractor from the creditor but defaulted in his payments. The creditor brought an action against him for recovery of the truck or damages, invoking the remedy of claim and delivery. The sheriff could not find the civil arrestee or the truck and returned the complaint and claim and delivery process unserved. The creditor then invoked the remedy of mesne civil arrest and bail under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. §§ 478- 504. The sheriff arrested the civil arrestee. He spent five weeks in jail with no opportunity to appear in the lower court to seek release from detention. He was finally able to obtain a surety bond and was then placed in the constructive custody of bail. The civil arrestee challenged the procedures that did not require that he be brought into court after his arrest or that he be notified that he has the right to release on bail, the right to apply for reduction of bail, the right to appear in court to challenge the legality of the arrest, and the right to appear by counsel. The civil arrestee contended that the procedure did not meet the standards of due process of law. The court agreed and granted the writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: Finding a lack of due process in the statutory scheme for mesne civil arrest, the court granted the writ of habeas corpus and discharged the civil arrestee from the constructive custody of bail.

 

227 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Huntley v. Public Utilities Com.


S. F. No. 22574


Supreme Court of California


69 Cal. 2d 67; 442 P.2d 685; 69 Cal. Rptr. 605; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 228



July 19, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review a decision of the Public Utilities Commission modifying and approving tariff schedules requiring recorded message subscribers to include in the recording the name of the individual or organization responsible for the message and the address at which the service is rendered unless such address is published in a current directory listing.



DISPOSITION: Decision annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, civil liberties union and subscriber, sought review of respondent Public Utilities Commission's decision, which modified and approved tariff schedules that required all subscribers to include their name and address when transmitting recorded messages.


OVERVIEW: A telephone company instituted tariff schedules that required subscribers who transmitted recorded messages to include their name and address unless the information was listed in a current directory listing. Petitioners brought an action challenging the schedules as violative of the First Amendment. The court annulled the Public Utilities Commission's approval of the tariff schedules. The court found that the First Amendment protected the right to be anonymous and that any impairment on that right must be justified by a compelling state interest. The court determined that even erroneous statements made by the recorded messages were entitled to constitutional protection. The tariff was not justified by the telephone company's fears that callers would assume that it sponsored the message. The telephone company was unable to provide a public utility function that necessitated identification by its subscribers. Finally, the court held that 47 U.S.C.S. § 317(a)(2), which required identification of persons that furnished political material to broadcasting stations, was not analogous to the tariff schedules.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the decision of the Public Utilities Commission, which approved a tariff schedule for the telephone company that required the subscribers to include their name and address in recorded messages.

 

228 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Marshall


Crim. No. 11947


Supreme Court of California


69 Cal. 2d 51; 442 P.2d 665; 69 Cal. Rptr. 585; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 227



July 16, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 14, 1968, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. McComb, J., Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. William S. Lee, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of marijuana.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted him of possession of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: Officers picked a lock and broke into defendant's apartment after an informant told them defendant had given him a bag of marijuana. While the officers waited for defendant and his roommate to return, they discovered a brown bag containing several cellophane bags of marijuana. Defendant was arrested without a warrant and was subsequently convicted. On appeal, defendant claimed that the trial court erroneously admitted the cache of marijuana that was illegally obtained into evidence. The court held that: (1) the officers lawfully entered into the apartment based on their reasonable belief that defendant was in it and they complied with the statutory "knock and announce" requirements; (2) the seized contraband was not in plain view and the seizure of it was not justified without a warrant; (3) the prosecution failed to sustain its burden of proving that the seizure was lawfully made in the course of a search for the suspects and the search was not substantially contemporaneous with the arrest; and (4) the marijuana was unconstitutionally seized and erroneously admitted into evidence, which contributed to defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of possession of marijuana was reversed.

Burton v. Municipal Court of Los Angeles Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 29524


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 684; 441 P.2d 281; 68 Cal. Rptr. 721; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 197



June 6, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of the Real Party in Interest for a Rehearing was Denied July 3, 1968. McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Municipal Court of the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County from proceeding with the trial of a theater manager and his assistant charged with exhibiting motion picture films to the public for compensation without the proper permit.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner theater managers were charged with exhibiting motion picture films to the public for compensation without a permit. They filed demurrers to the complaint on constitutional grounds. Respondent Municipal Court of Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), overruled the demurrers. The theater managers sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the lower court from proceeding with their trial.


OVERVIEW: The theater managers were charged with violating Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code §103.109, exhibiting motion picture films to the public for compensation without a permit. They filed demurrers to the complaint on the grounds that the ordinance violated their rights of free speech and press. The lower court overruled the demurrers and the theater managers pleaded not guilty to the charges. They sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the lower court from proceeding with the trial. The court held that (1) the theater managers had standing to challenge the ordinance without first applying for a license and having the application rejected, (2) it was constitutionally permissible for the municipality to impose reasonable regulations upon the theater, however, (3) the ordinance was overly broad; it did not provide precise standards capable of objective measurement and thus vested the city officials with unlimited discretionary power to grant or refuse permits and thereby effectively impose a censorship or previous restraint upon the theater managers' constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition as prayed by the theater managers proscribing the lower court from trying them for violation of an ordinance requiring them to be licensed to exhibit motion pictures.

 

232 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davis


Crim. No. 11822


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 481; 439 P.2d 651; 67 Cal. Rptr. 547; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 181



April 22, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County. Howard S. Goldin, Judge.

Prosecution for violation of Pen. Code, § 404.6.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an appeal of his conviction pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 404.6, which prohibited conduct with the intent to cause a riot, in favor of the state in the Municipal Court of the Los Angeles County Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for conduct with intent to urge a riot, contending the Cal. Penal Code § 404.6 was unconstitutional. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court rejected the argument that the statute exempted labor organizations in violation of equal protection, explaining that a common sense construction of the statute subjected both union and nonunion persons alike to criminal punishment, and the second paragraph merely declared the legislature's intent. The court observed that, when possible, statutes were to be given a reasonable interpretation that rendered them valid and operative. The court found that the statute was not impermissibly broad because it provided adequate warning of what constituted a penal offense. The court explained that the word "urge" was of common usage and the proscribed urging occurred at the point where the speaker uttered the words to urge violent or forcible acts. The court found that the statute's inclusion of the phrase "clear and present danger" was not vague because the courts had long used such language to distinguish between permissible limitations on speech and those that ran afoul of constitutional guarantees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for urging a riot.

 

233 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sesslin


Crim. No. 11519


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 418; 439 P.2d 321; 67 Cal. Rptr. 409; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 174



April 10, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 9, 1968. McComb, J., Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph L. Call, Judge.

Prosecution for forgery and grand theft.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction of forgery reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant on one court of forgery after defendant was arrested based on a warrant that did not provide specific facts and had provided handwriting exemplars after the arrest.


OVERVIEW: Police obtained an arrest warrant based solely on "information and belief" and without any underlying facts upon which the magistrate could independently find probable cause against defendant for forgery and grand theft. The police arrested defendant and, after advising defendant of his rights, including the right to not give a handwriting exemplar, obtained handwriting exemplars from defendant. The trial court convicted defendant of one count of forgery. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the search warrant was invalid under U.S. Const. amend. IV and Cal. Penal Code §§ 806, 813, and 952 because it did not provide specific facts of the alleged crime. As such, the court held that the handwriting exemplars were inadmissible as fruit of the poisonous tree.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's conviction of defendant for forgery where the arrest warrant was not supported by specific facts, and so was illegal, and ruled that handwriting exemplars obtained by the state after the arrest were inadmissible as fruit of the poisonous tree.

 

234 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rosales


Crim. No. 11533


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 299; 437 P.2d 489; 66 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 164



February 28, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 28, 1968. McComb, J., Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph L. Call, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of heroin.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of possession of heroin. The prosecution was based on the discovery of heroin on defendant's person at the time of his arrest. Defendant contended that the heroin was obtained by an unconstitutional search and seizure by the arresting officers.


OVERVIEW: The arresting officers had received a telephone call that defendant and another individual were arranging sales of heroin. One officer got in touch with defendant's parole officer and both suspect's had failed to report. Defendant's parole was suspended, and an all points bulletin was issued for his arrest. The officers then went to defendant's house to arrest him for the parole violation. The officers quickly entered the house and accosted defendant. Just before they arrested defendant they passed a girl and told her they were police officers but did not announce their purpose before entry into the house. The court held that the officers' failure to explain their purpose and demand admittance as required by Cal. Penal Code § 844 vitiated the arrest. The court reasoned that the conviction required reversal as the officers' identification to the girl did not constitute substantial compliance with § 844. The court found that the prosecution did not discharge its burden of establishing the legality of the entry, and the fact that defendant was a parole violator did not excuse noncompliance with § 844.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was reversed.

 

235 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Department of Public Works v. Superior Court of Merced County


S. F. No. 22510


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 206; 436 P.2d 342; 65 Cal. Rptr. 342; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 156



February 1, 1968 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of the Real Parties in Interest for a Rehearing was Denied February 28, 1968. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Merced County to proceed with the condemnation of three instead of two parcels of real property owned by the real parties in interest.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Department of Public Works, brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Merced County (California) to proceed with the trial of the complaint against respondent landowners, or in the alternative for a writ of prohibition forbidding the court from proceeding with the order, which dismissed the complaint in part.


OVERVIEW: The landowners challenged the condemnation of three instead of two parcels of land on the ground that taking property for such a purely economic purpose violated the Cal. Const. art. I, § 14, because the taking was not for a "public use." The court held that the entire parcel could probably be condemned for little more than the cost of taking the part needed for the highway and paying damages for the remainder. The court held that it was sound economy for the state to take the entire parcel to minimize ultimate costs and under the circumstances, excess condemnation was constitutional. The court held that when the property was not needed for the physical construction of the public improvement, the question of public use turned on a determination of whether the taking was justified to avoid excessive severance or consequential damages. The court held that if it was determined that the excess condemnation was not justified, the court must find that it was not for a public use.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate, which ordered the trial court to proceed with the trial of the case under the original complaint in accordance with the views expressed by the court. The court held that the trial court must refuse to condemn the property if it found that the taking was not justified to avoid excessive severance or consequential damages.

 

236 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Berry


Crim. No. 11117


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 137; 436 P.2d 273; 65 Cal. Rptr. 273; 1968 Cal. LEXIS 151; 67 L.R.R.M. 2657



January 24, 1968 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted ordering petitioners discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner picketers were charged with criminal contempt for the willful disobedience of a temporary restraining order issued by a superior court (California) after they participated in picketing against the county. The picketers sought review of the superior court's denial of their application for a writ of habeas corpus, in which they had alleged that they were illegally restrained of their liberty because the order was void and unenforceable.


OVERVIEW: The temporary restraining order was issued to prevent striking and mass picketing against the county by the social workers union and related individuals. The picketers were private citizens in no way affiliated with the union, county, or welfare department when they picketed before a county building. The court held that (1) the order was unconstitutionally overbroad in that it unnecessarily restricted the exercise of First Amendment rights as peaceful picketing was an activity subject to absolute constitutional protection in the absence of a valid state interest justifying limitation or restriction, which the county had not shown; (2) the order was too vague and uncertain to satisfy the requirements of notice and fair trial which were inherent in the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment as the description of the group to which the proscription applied was indefinite; (3) the doctrine of severability of the order was inapplicable; and (4) the order was therefore void in its entirety because an order constitutionally void on its face was issued in excess of the superior court's jurisdiction and could not sustain a contempt judgment based on its violation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the picketers' application for a writ of habeas corpus and ordered their discharge.

Wirta v. Alameda-Contra Costa Transit Dist.


S. F. No. 22418


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 51; 434 P.2d 982; 64 Cal. Rptr. 430; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 201



December 21, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Appellant District for a Rehearing was Denied January 17, 1968. Schauer, J., * Sat in Place of Tobriner, J., who did not Participate therein. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Schauer, J., * were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County granting a preliminary injunction to restrain defendants from refusing to accept an advertisement. Cecil Mosbacher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant transit companies challenged an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) granting a preliminary injunction to restrain defendants from refusing to accept an advertisement espousing world peace through education.


OVERVIEW: Defendant transit companies refused to accept and place advertising from plaintiff peace organization on defendants' buses. Defendants' policy restricted advertising to the sale of goods and services and political ads during election time. Plaintiffs filed an action, seeking an injunction, to restrain defendants from refusing to accept the advertising on the ground that defendants' refusal was unconstitutional. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction as prayed for. The trial court found that defendants' conduct abridged plaintiffs' rights of free speech and deprived them of equal protection.On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's granting of the preliminary injunction. The court held that defendants, having opened a forum for the expression of ideas by providing facilities for advertisements on its buses, could not, for reasons of administrative convenience, decline to accept advertisements expressing opinions and beliefs within the ambit of U.S. Const. amend. I protection The court further noted that the fact that plaintiffs' message was a paid advertisement did not detract from the protection afforded it under the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which granted the preliminary injunction. The court held that defendant transit companies could not decline to accept plaintiff peace group's advertising, because plaintiff's message was protected by the First Amendment of the U.S. Constitution.

Vogel v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 29515


Supreme Court of California


68 Cal. 2d 18; 434 P.2d 961; 64 Cal. Rptr. 409; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 200



December 21, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Robert W. Kenny, Judge.

Action by taxpayer to enjoin county from expending public funds for administering or enforcing portion of oath required of public employees.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Summary judgment granting injunction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, County of Los Angeles, sought a review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff taxpayer and an injunction to enjoin the county from expending funds for administering or enforcing the unconstitutional portion of the oath required of public employees.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer brought an action in the trial court to enjoin the county from spending public funds for administering or enforcing the second paragraph of the oath required of public employees by the state constitution, Cal. Const. art. XX, § 3. The trial court held that the second paragraph was invalid and granted the injunction. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. The court determined that the oath forbade membership in organizations that advocated the overthrow of the state or federal government and that this language was unconstitutionally overbroad because the oath could only constitutionally forbid membership when the member oathtaker held the specific intent to further the group's unlawful aims. The court rejected the county's suggestion that the court read that limitation into the oath because there was no ambiguity in the language that would allow the court, in effect, to rewrite the oath.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's grant of summary judgment, enjoining the county from spending public money to administer the unconstitutional part of the oath to public employees. The court determined that the oath's second paragraph, proscribing membership in any group that advocated overthrowing the government, was unconstitutionally broad because the proscription can only extend to a specific intent to further the group's unlawful aims.
