In re Wells


Crim. No. 11555


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 873; 434 P.2d 613; 64 Cal. Rptr. 317; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 272



December 13, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied; order to show cause discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued an order to show cause to consider petitioner inmate's claim that his underlying conviction for sexual misconduct with a five-year-old girl in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288 was open to collateral attack upon his petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: An order to show cause was issued to consider the inmate's claim that his underlying conviction for the violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288 was open to collateral attack upon his petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The inmate alleged that at the preliminary examination preceding of his initial conviction, he was not apprised of his right to counsel pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 859. The court discharged the order to show cause and denied the writ of habeas corpus. The inmate's petition lacked merit because he did not move to set aside the information, and he thereby waived his statutory right to question the legality of his commitment. The court further held that even had the inmate moved to set aside the information, his failure to pursue the issue on appeal would have barred consideration in the collateral proceeding. Moreover, the court held that although the inmate should have been certified to the superior court for hearing and examination on whether he was a mentally disordered sex offender following his second conviction, the failure to observe correct statutory procedures in postconviction proceedings did not render an underlying judgment of conviction unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause, and the writ of habeas corpus was denied.

 

240 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Hoffman


Crim. No. 11026


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 845; 434 P.2d 353; 64 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 269



December 11, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner war protesters who were convicted of violating a city ordinance, Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 421.11.1, restricting the right to be in a railroad station, sought a writ of habeas corpus on the ground that the ordinance unconstitutionally abridged their right to free speech.


OVERVIEW: The war protesters, who were leafleting a train station, were found guilty of violating § 421.11.1, on the ground that they were in the station without any business with a carrier and hence were loitering within the meaning of the ordinance. Petitioners contended that the ordinance unconstitutionally abridged their right of free speech under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court granted a writ of mandamus and ordered that petitioners be discharged from custody. The court ruled that the railway station was like a public street or park and that noise and commotion were characteristics of the normal operation of the station. The court found that the railroads sought neither privacy within nor exclusive possession of the station, as such, they could not invoke the law of trespass against petitioners to protect those interests. The court held that petitioners' activities did not interfere with the use of the station. As such, because of the overbreadth of coverage of the ordinance, the language prohibiting presence in a terminal longer than reasonably necessary to conduct business with a carrier was unconstitutional and petitioners' convictions could not stand.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of mandamus and ordered that petitioner war protesters be discharged from custody. The court held that petitioners' activities did not interfere with the use of the station and concluded that a city ordinance's language prohibiting presence in a terminal longer than reasonably necessary to conduct business with a carrier was unconstitutional and petitioners' convictions could not stand.

Eye Dog Foundation v. State Board of Guide Dogs for the Blind


L.A. No. 28495


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 536; 432 P.2d 717; 63 Cal. Rptr. 21; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 240



October 27, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Arthur K. Marshall, Judge.

Action seeking declaratory relief as to constitutionality of statutes concerning guide dogs for the blind and for an injunction prohibiting the enforcement of the statutes and interference with the operation of the school engaged in the training of seeing eye dogs.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. That portion of judgment declaring Business and Professions Code section 7210.5 unconstitutional and enjoining defendants from enforcing or threatening to enforce it for a 120-day period reversed; in all other respects judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff guide dog corporation and defendant California State Board of Guide Dogs for the Blind both appealed portions of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 7200-7217 were constitutional, except for § 7210.5, in plaintiff's action seeking a declaratory judgment that they were unconstitutional and seeking an injunction to prohibit defendants from enforcing them.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff guide dog corporation sought a declaratory judgment that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 7200-7217 were unconstitutional on their face and as applied to plaintiff, and sought an injunction prohibiting defendant California State Board of Guide Dogs for the Blind from enforcing said statutes and interfering with plaintiff's operation of its business or school. The trial court held that the statutes, except for § 7210.5, which was partially invalidated, were constitutional. Both plaintiff and defendant appealed. Upon review of an opinion prepared by the trial court, the court reversed the portion of the judgment that declared § 7210.05 unconstitutional and affirmed the judgment in all other respects. The court held that the statutes were not unconstitutional because they were reasonable regulatory enactments, and that when a state saw fit to regulate upon a matter that was within its police power, its authority over the subject was plenary and reviewable by the courts only to the extent of determining whether the regulation was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court held that the entire chapter on the subject of guide dogs for the blind in the Business and Professions Code was constitutional on its face and as applied to plaintiff guide dog corporation by defendant California State Board of Guide Dogs for the Blind. The court held that it had nothing to do with the wisdom of laws or regulations, and that the legislative power was to be upheld unless it infringed constitutional guarantees.

Silver v. Reagan


Sac. Nos. 7814, 7815


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 452; 432 P.2d 26; 62 Cal. Rptr. 424; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 230



October 6, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to enforce rights of citizens, taxpayers, and voters to equality of voting power in the election of members to the House of Representatives of the United States Congress.



DISPOSITION: Writ withheld to permit Legislature to reapportion congressional districts; jurisdiction retained to grant further relief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, citizens, taxpayers, and voters, sought a writ of mandate against respondents, governor, state officials, and the legislature, to enforce their rights and the rights of others to equality of voting power in the election of members to the House of Representatives of the United States Congress pursuant to U.S. Const. art. I, § 2 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: In 1961, the California legislature divided the state into 38 congressional districts pursuant to Cal. Const. art. IV, § 27, which required congressional districts to consist of either whole counties, whole assembly districts, or combinations thereof. As a result, many of the districts departed substantially from the ideal populations of one thirty-eighth of the state's total population. The court had denied a previous request for a writ of mandate for reapportionment due to time constraints that would have come into play had reapportionment been ordered in time for the 1966 elections. However, the legislature failed to enact a measure for reapportionment by the close of its regular 1967 session. Respondents asserted that practical difficulties prevented them from altering voting districts in accord with the 1960 census. The court did not grant a writ of mandate but asked the legislature to adopt a reapportionment plan and held that if it failed to do so by December 7, 1967, the court would adopt a plan of its own. In reaching this decision, the court noted that the difficulties resulting from reapportioning did not justify perpetuating an unconstitutional apportionment.


OUTCOME: The court did not grant a writ of mandate but ordered the legislature to adopt a reapportionment plan and reserved the option, in the alternative, of adopting a plan of its own.

People v. Lara


Crim. No. 10061


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 365; 432 P.2d 202; 62 Cal. Rptr. 586; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 226



September 29, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS, one automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Beach Vasey, Judge.

Prosecution for first degree murder and kidnaping for the purpose of robbery with bodily harm.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction imposing sentence of life imprisonment as to one defendant and the death penalty on the murder charge as to the other defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed judgments from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting them of first degree murder and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery with the victim suffering bodily harm.


OVERVIEW: The defendant shooter contended that the shotgun admitted into evidence was obtained via an illegal search and seizure. The defendant accomplice contended that there was no evidence of the corpus delicti of the crime of kidnapping for the purpose of robbery. Both defendants contended that they did not knowingly and intelligently waive their constitutional rights to counsel and to remain silent. However, rejecting these contentions, the court affirmed the defendants' convictions. The challenged search was incident to the arrest of the defendant shooter, such that the search was constitutional if the arrest was lawful. The requisite probable cause for the arrest was established. Also, the requisite corpus delicti was established. Finally, although the defendant accomplice possessed the mental age of a minor, the age of a juvenile did not rule out the possibility of an intelligent waiver of Fifth and Sixth Amendment rights. It was a matter of proof in each instance. In this case, the evidence demonstrated that the defendants had knowingly and intelligently waived their rights to counsel and to remain silent.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions were affirmed.

 

244 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Farley v. Healey


S. F. No. 22567


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 325; 431 P.2d 650; 62 Cal. Rptr. 26; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 396



September 18, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel an acting registrar of voters and a county clerk to determine the sufficiency of signatures to a petition to place an initiative measure on the ballot and, if determined to be sufficient, to place the proposed initiative on the ballot.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondents, an acting registrar of voters and a county clerk, to determine the sufficiency of signatures to a petition to place an initiative measure on the ballot and, if determined to be sufficient, to place the proposed initiative on the ballot. The superior court (California) denied a writ of mandate and there was insufficient time before a ballot deadline for an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The acting registrar of voters, on the city attorney's advice, refused to determine the sufficiency of the signatures on a petition to place a referendum issue on the ballot to urge a ceasefire and troop withdrawal in Vietnam. The court allowed the voters to proceed under its direct jurisdiction. The court held that under City and County of San Francisco, Cal., Charter § 180, the duty of the acting registrar of voters was limited to the ministerial function of ascertaining whether the procedural requirements for submitting an initiative measure had been met and that it was not his function to determine whether a proposed initiative would be valid if enacted or whether a proposed declaration of policy was one to which the initiative might apply. The court held that the initiative provision, Charter § 179, was to be construed broadly and did not expressly restrict the type of declaration that might be submitted but instead referred to "any" declaration. The court held that this included declarations of policies on state and national matters whether or not the board of supervisors could carry those policies into effect.


OUTCOME: The court issued a preemptory writ of mandate that directed the acting registrar of voters and county clerk to determine the sufficiency of the signatures to the petition and, if determined to be sufficient, to place the proposed initiative on the ballot.

Driscoll v. City of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 28479


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 297; 431 P.2d 245; 61 Cal. Rptr. 661; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 220



September 13, 1967  
September 13, 1967


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Leonard A. Diether, Judge.

Action by widows of police and fire department members for a declaration of past and future pension rights and benefits.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. That portion of judgment granting limited recovery affirmed as to plaintiffs Driscoll, Beard and Powell, and reversed as to plaintiffs Slaten and Galbreth (La Niece); that portion of judgment denying recovery to plaintiff Hand reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant widows contested a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which limited recovery to unpaid pension benefits accruing within six months prior to the filing of the declaratory judgment action seeking a determination of appellants' past and future pension rights arising from retired former members of the city police and fire departments. Appellee city appealed the entire judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant widows sought review from adverse portions of the trial court's judgment limiting their recovery to unpaid pension benefits accruing within six months prior to the filing and denying recovery of other unpaid pension benefits accruing prior to six-month period. Appellee city sought review of the entire judgment. Appellants brought an action to obtain a monthly death benefit pension. The trial court concluded that appellee was estopped to assert the statute of limitations but not the claims provisions and that appellants were eligible for benefits. On appeal, the court found that appellee was not estopped to assert the bar of its claims provision. The court failed to find that the conduct intended to induce action, the ignorance of the true state of facts on the part of the parties asserting estoppel, and, particularly, the reliance by the parties upon such conduct to their injury. The court held that the proffered evidence was relevant to the issue of the reasonableness of appellee's conduct under all the circumstances and was, therefore, admissible. The court affirmed as to some appellants and affirmed as to others.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment granting limited recovery to appellant widows and reversed as to appellants who were not entitled because the court failed to find the conduct intended to induce action, the ignorance of the true state of facts on the part of the parties asserting estoppel, and, particularly, the reliance by said parties upon such conduct to their injury.

People v. Coffey


Crim. No. 10930


Supreme Court of California


67 Cal. 2d 204; 430 P.2d 15; 60 Cal. Rptr. 457; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 214



July 28, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 21, 1967. Traynor, C. J., and Peters, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. William L. Murray, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with intent to commit murder, for assault with a deadly weapon upon police officers, and for being armed with a concealed weapon.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted him by jury of assault with intent to commit murder, being armed at the time with a pistol capable of being concealed upon the person without having a license to carry the pistol, and assault with a deadly weapon upon the person of a police officer. The information also charged him with having a prior felony conviction.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney made a motion before sentencing to strike the allegations of the deadly weapons section, Cal. Penal Code § 12022. The court found that the trial court's abortive attempt to reflect in the information and judgment the ambiguous motion had resulted in a judgment and record so confused and uncertain as to render meaningful analysis impossible. The court concluded that the state of the record, together with the fact that the trial court's action represented a purported exercise of discretionary powers as to sentencing, precluded present modification or correction by the court. The court held that the allegations made by defendant in support of his motion to strike the prior conviction were sufficient to justify a hearing for the purpose of determining whether, in the proceedings leading to that conviction, he was accorded his right to counsel in accordance with the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments. The court concluded that if the prior conviction was determined to be invalid, then there was a reasonable possibility that erroneous impeachment of defendant's testimonial credibility by the prior conviction materially influenced the jury in arriving at its verdicts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the cause for further proceedings under the directions and in conformity with the views expressed in its opinion.

Flack v. Municipal Court for Anaheim-Fullerton Judicial Dist.


L. A. No. 28782


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 981; 429 P.2d 192; 59 Cal. Rptr. 872; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 360



July 3, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 26, 1967. McComb, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Stephen K. Tamura, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Municipal Court for the Anaheim-Fullerton Judicial District of Orange County to return an allegedly obscene film seized incident to an arrest.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment discharging the alternative writ of mandate and denying the peremptory writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner arrestees sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied the arrestees mandamus relief in their action brought to compel respondent municipal court to return an allegedly obscene film seized during an arrest. The arrestees claimed the First Amendment had been violated because the seizure occurred without a warrant and without a prior judicial determination of obscenity.


OVERVIEW: The police officers watched the arrestees' film from the audience and determined the film was an exhibition of obscene matter. Without obtaining a search warrant or a prior judicial determination of obscenity, the police officers initiated an arrest and seized the film. After the arrestees were charged with exhibiting obscene matter, the arrestees filed a motion for return of the film. When the municipal court denied the motion, the arrestees filed a complaint for a writ of mandate, praying that the municipal court be ordered to direct the films return. Thereafter, the superior court denied the writ, and upon review, the court reversed. After finding that a writ of mandate would lie to compel the return of property illegally seized without a warrant, the court held the film's seizure had been invalid under the First and Fourteenth Amendments because a search warrant was required prior to any search for or seizure of allegedly obscene material, even if the search was contemporaneous with an arrest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment denying the arrestees mandamus relief in the arrestees' action brought to compel the municipal court to return an allegedly obscene film seized during the arrest.

O'Donnell v. Mullaney


L. A. No. 29409


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 994; 429 P.2d 160; 59 Cal. Rptr. 840; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 361



July 3, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Robert P. Kneeland, Judge.

Action for damages for wrongful death resulting from an automobile accident on a private road.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, decedent's mother and stepfather, challenged a jury verdict from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) in favor of defendants, driver and others, on plaintiffs' wrongful death action resulting from an automobile accident on a private road.


OVERVIEW: The decedent accepted a social invitation from defendant driver to ride in his car. The driver failed to negotiate a sharp curve on a private road and an accident resulting in the decedent's death occurred. Plaintiffs brought a wrongful death action. The trial court refused to give instructions on negligence, ruled that the California Guest Law, Cal. Veh. Code § 17158 applied, and instructed the jury that plaintiffs could recover only upon a finding of willful misconduct. The jury returned a verdict for defendants and this appeal ensued. The court found the guest law was inapplicable to an accident on a private roadway, as distinguished from a public highway. There was no rational basis to construe the guest law, as urged by defendants, to mean that it was to be applied when part of the ride was on a public highway, but the negligent injury was inflicted on private property. Since the California Guest Law was inapplicable to the driver's negligence, which occurred on a private road, improper instructions were delivered to the jury. That error required reversal of the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which had found in favor of defendants on plaintiffs' wrongful death action.

People v. Massie


Crim. No. 9506


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 899; 428 P.2d 869; 59 Cal. Rptr. 733; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 354



June 21, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Appellant Massie for a Rehearing was Denied July 19, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS, one automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. H. Burton Noble, Judge.

Prosecution for first degree murder, attempted murder and robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed as to one defendant and reversed as to the other. Judgment of conviction affirmed as to defendant on automatic appeal, reversed as to other defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were jointly convicted by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of first degree murder, attempted murder, and robbery. Defendant one was sentenced to death for the murder, defendant two to life imprisonment for the murder, and each defendant was sentenced to the terms prescribed by law on the other counts. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant one contended that his convictions and death sentence should be reversed because they rested upon confessions obtained in violation of his constitutional rights and that under Cal. Penal Code § 654 the trial court erred in sentencing him. Defendant two contended that the trial court erred in denying his motion for a new trial. The court held that the trier of fact properly found that defendant one gave a voluntary confession. The court held further that the record did not lack support for the conclusion that, although fully informed of his rights, defendant one waived his rights and confessed voluntarily. In addition, the court held that defendant one's commission of a series of robberies did not constitute "a single course of conduct," and, therefore, § 654 did not apply. In regard to defendant two, the court held that it could not conclude that the identifications of defendant two were so impeached, inherently improbable, or physically impossible as to render them unworthy of belief. However, the court held that the trial court erred in failing to order a separate trial and that this error prejudiced defendant two.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments as to defendant one and reversed the judgments against defendant two.

County of Sacramento v. Hickman


Sac. No. 7803


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 841; 428 P.2d 593; 59 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 347



June 15, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel a county assessor to comply with Rev. & Tax. Code, § 401, requiring assessment of all taxable property at an announced fraction of its full cash value.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, county, county board of supervisors, and several school districts, brought an original proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent county assessor to comply with Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 401, which required the assessment of all taxable property at an announced fraction of its full cash value.


OVERVIEW: In an original proceeding in mandamus, petitioners, county, county board of supervisors, and several school districts, sought to compel respondent county assessor to comply with Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 401, which required the assessment of all taxable property at an announced fraction of its full cash value. The court held that § 401 was not unconstitutional because Cal. Const. art. XI, § 12 allowed assessment at a uniform fraction of full cash value provided that full cash value remained the standard or basis of each assessment, and the legislature had the constitutional power to prescribe that fraction for statewide application. The peremptory writ was granted.


OUTCOME: The court granted the peremptory writ in an original mandamus proceeding brought by petitioners, county, county board of supervisors, and several school districts, seeking to compel respondent county assessor to proceed in accordance with a statute requiring assessment at a ratio less than full cash value because the statute was not in conflict with the state constitution.

 

251 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Jehl v. Southern Pacific Co.


L. A. No. 29342


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 821; 427 P.2d 988; 59 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 345



June 2, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County granting a new trial limited to damages in an action for damages for personal injuries. Martin Katz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed unless the trial court in its discretion orders an additur to be accepted or rejected by defendant within the time prescribed by the court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent employee a new trial limited to damages in an action for personal injuries sustained in the railroad yard.


OVERVIEW: The employee was injured while working and successfully moved for a new trial on the issue of damages. The employer sought review. The court affirmed the judgment unless the trial court in its discretion ordered an additur after its receipt of the court's remittitur. The court rejected the employer's contention that the jury should have been returned for further deliberations. Additur described an order by which a plaintiff's motion for a new trial on the ground of inadequate damages was granted unless a defendant consented to a specified increase of the award within a prescribed time. The court held that a defendant should not be denied the advantages of additur, when required to submit to remittitur. The court held that additur would have no effect on the activities of railroads and their employees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment unless the trial court in its discretion ordered an additur after its receipt of the court's remittitur. The court held that additur did not affect activities of railroads and had no substantial effect on the outcome of litigation between railroads and their employees.

People v. Varnum


Crim. No. 10190


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 808; 427 P.2d 772; 59 Cal. Rptr. 108; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 344



May 31, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 28, 1967. White, J., * Sat in Place of Mosk, J., who Deemed Himself Disqualified. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. John G. Barnes, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with intent to commit robbery, first degree robbery, kidnaping with bodily harm for the purpose of robbery, and first degree murder.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and affirmed in part. Judgment of conviction imposing death penalty on murder charge and fixing penalty for kidnaping at life imprisonment reversed insofar as it relates to penalty; affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was granted an automatic appeal under Cal. Penal Code, § 1239 (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found him guilty of assault with intent to commit robbery, first degree robbery, kidnapping with bodily harm for the purpose of robbery, and first-degree murder and which sentenced him to life imprisonment and death for the murder charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of assault with intent to commit robbery, first degree robbery, kidnapping with bodily harm for the purpose of robbery, and first-degree murder. He was sentenced to life imprisonment without possibility of parole on the kidnapping charge and sentenced to death for the murder charge. On an automatic appeal, under Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b), the court reversed the judgment only as to the penalty. The police found the murder weapon by questioning an accomplice, whose husband, also an accomplice, had informed her of the location of the weapon. Neither had been advised of their constitutional rights prior to the police questioning. The court found that although the wife was not prosecuted for the offense, she was entitled to be advised of her rights before being subjected to police questioning. The information elicited not only implicated defendant but also implicated both accomplices. The court found that the evidence could not be used against them. The court further found that it was substantial error to admit the accomplices' testimony, and it was probable that a result more favorable to defendant would have been reached in the absence of the error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court as to defendant's penalty only for his conviction of robbery, kidnapping and, murder.

People v. Modesto


Crim. No. 10267


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 695; 427 P.2d 788; 59 Cal. Rptr. 124; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 335



May 23, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 21, 1967. White, J., * Sat in Place of Mosk, J., who Deemed Himself Disqualified. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County. John Neblett, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing death penalty affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder and sentenced him to death. The court had reversed defendant 's two prior convictions for the same offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted, inter alia, that the prosecution improperly suggested that, if he were found not guilty by reason of insanity, he would be "set free," that the prosecution erroneously commented on his failure to take the stand, and that the prosecutor's improper references to defendant's first two trials infected the guilt and penalty trials with reversible error. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court reasoned that the prosecutor's statement that if defendant were found insane at the time he committed the offense, he would be "turned loose," was obvious misconduct. However, the court found that it could not hold that the misconduct constituted reversible error because defendant rendered the matter moot by waiving his right to a sanity trial. The court concluded that the prosecution's comments on defendant's silence were improper but did not require a new trial under the test of harmless constitutional error. The court reasoned that a timely objection would have prevented the prosecutor's most harmful comments about the previous trials from ever reaching the jury and that under such circumstances an objection could not be made for the first time on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death.

People v. United Nat'l Life Ins. Co.

Supreme Court of California

Motions for summary judgment were filed by plaintiff and by defendants. The trial court granted defendants' motions and denied plaintiff's motions upon the ground that California could not constitutionally regulate the type of insurance business being conducted by defendants. Plaintiff appealed. Finding that defendants were subject to the provisions of Cal. Ins. Code § 700 and that such statute comported with the applicable criteria of federal due process and was free of conflict with any federal law or regulation, this court reversed the trial court's judgments.


OUTCOME: The judgments, which granted defendants' motions and denied plaintiff's motions for summary judgment on the ground that California could not constitutionally regulate the type of insurance business being conducted by defendants, were reversed.

 

255 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pennington


Crim. No. 9339


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 508; 426 P.2d 942; 58 Cal. Rptr. 374; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 321



April 27, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 24, 1967. Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Imperial County. George R. Kirk, Judge.

Prosecution for murder, felony child stealing, lewd and lascivious conduct with a child under 14 years of age, and of unlawfully furnishing drugs to a minor.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An automatic appeal was taken under Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Imperial County (California) after a jury trial, on verdicts finding defendant guilty of offenses including first degree murder, finding that he was sane at the time of the crime, and imposing the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: An appeal as of right was taken from a superior court judgment after a jury found defendant guilty of crimes, including first degree murder, and imposed the death penalty. During the trial, defendant interrupted the proceedings with comments and curses and was ordered gagged. Defendant's counsel moved the court to suspend the trial pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1368 for a hearing on present sanity, offering an affidavit, then testimony, from a licensed psychologist, concluding that defendant was insane. The trial court denied the motion, and declined to reconsider after a psychiatrist, relying instead on the opinions of four court-appointed psychiatrists later offered similar testimony. The court reversed, holding that due process required a full hearing when a substantial doubt was raised regarding defendant's present sanity. The court found the psychologist's opinion met this standard as a matter of law, regardless of any opinion to the contrary, and that the taking of his testimony did not constitute the "hearing" required under § 1368. The court found the failure to conduct a hearing under these circumstances was per se prejudicial and reversible error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court judgment convicting defendant of offenses, including first degree murder, and finding him sane at the time of the crime, and imposing the death penalty. The court held that defendant was improperly denied a hearing to determine if he was sufficiently competent and sane to stand trial.

 

256 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Charles


Crim. No. 9657


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 330; 425 P.2d 545; 57 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 306



April 4, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 4, 1967. McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Adolph Alexander, Judge.

Prosecution for armed robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment of conviction affirmed as to one defendant and reversed as to the other.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two defendants were charged with armed robbery, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 211, 211a. After a joint bench trial, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, California, found both defendants guilty as charged. Defendants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with armed robbery of a liquor store. The court found that criminal procedure rules created by a November 12, 1965 decision applied because defendants were on appeal after that date even though their cases were tried earlier. In affirming one conviction, the court held that the first defendant offered to confess twice. The second time, police simply said, "Go ahead," and defendant gave a full confession. The court held that although it was error for the court to hear both defendants' confessions during the trial, defendant's conviction was not prejudiced because it stood on his properly admissible and amply corroborated confession alone. In reversing the second defendant's conviction, the court held that his confession came from interrogations designed to elicit incriminating statements. The court found that the second defendant initially denied the crime, but after what police called a "short conversation," defendant finally confessed. The court found no record of the so-called "short conversation" which overcame the second defendant's reluctance to confess and held that the prosecution failed to meet its burden that the confession was obtained properly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's armed robbery conviction as to one defendant and reversed the conviction as to the other defendant on the ground that the confession was elicited by unconstitutional methods.

 

257 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Parrish v. Civil Service Com.


S.F. No. 22429


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 260; 425 P.2d 223; 57 Cal. Rptr. 623; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 301



March 27, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Wayne P. Burke, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a county civil service commission to reinstate a social worker discharged for insubordination.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff social worker appealed an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted judgment to defendant county employer in the social worker's complaint seeking reinstatement with back pay because his termination was illegal, in that he was fired for his insubordination in refusing to participate in a welfare raid that was violative of a welfare recipient's constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The employer initiated unannounced early-morning searches of the homes of county welfare recipients for the purpose of detecting the presence of "unauthorized males." When directed to participate in the operation, the social worker submitted a letter stating that he could not participate because the searches were illegal. He was discharged for insubordination. The court held that the warrantless searches by the employer transgressed constitutional limitations because the evidence sought would have afforded a basis for criminal prosecution. There was no probable cause to search the targeted residences, and no effective voluntary consent for the searches was possible upon consideration of the relationship of the employer, a welfare provider, to the recipients. The employer also failed to demonstrate that the scope of the raids was closely related to the achievement of a legitimate end. The court held there were alternate means of detecting welfare fraud that were less subversive of the recipients' constitutional rights. The court held that in light of the social worker's knowledge of the projected operation, he had adequate grounds for declining to participate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment and ordered judgment entered for the social worker, granting him reinstatement to his job with the employer and backpay.

 

258 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Chichernea


L. A. No. 28392


Supreme Court of California


66 Cal. 2d 83; 424 P.2d 687; 57 Cal. Rptr. 135; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 285



March 8, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Beach Vasey, Judge.

Action to determine heirship rights of nonresident aliens.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for objector reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner surviving relatives, all residents of Romania, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which was in favor of objector state as to the surviving relatives' action to determine heirship rights of nonresident aliens. That court had refused to enforce the decedent's will and accordingly permitted the state to escheat the estate to itself.


OVERVIEW: The state had sought to escheat the decedent's estate on the grounds that the law of Romania applicable at the date of death did not accord to United States citizens a reciprocal right to take property upon the same terms and conditions as its nationals, a requirement imposed by Cal. Prob. Code § 259. On appeal, the court held that the surviving relatives, in fact, had established the reciprocity required by § 259 and that the trial judge erred in deciding that it could not honor the decedent's wishes. The court accordingly reversed, noting that Romania, much like the Soviet Union, did allow United States citizens to inherit economically significant property on terms of full equality with its own citizens and residents. The court further found that although Romania law imposed temporary restrictions upon the right of United States citizens to remove inheritance proceeds from Romania, such restrictions were not fatal to the reciprocity required by § 259. Thus, as the statutes interposed no barrier to right of Californians to leave their estates to citizens and residents of Romania, the trial judge had erred in denying that right.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the state and remanded the matter.

Rosenfield v. Malcolm


S. F. No. 22352


Supreme Court of California


65 Cal. 2d 559; 421 P.2d 697; 55 Cal. Rptr. 505; 1967 Cal. LEXIS 365; 54 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P9035; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9760



January 4, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Homer W. Buckley, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a county health officer to reinstate a dismissed employee with back pay.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer was sustained, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff doctor appealed from a judgment of dismissal entered by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), pursuant to an order of the trial court sustaining defendant County Health Officer's demurrer to the doctor's petition for a writ of mandate to compel his reinstatement with back pay.


OVERVIEW: A doctor was "provisionally appointed" to a post by a County Health Officer. He was discharged from the post for the express reason that he refused to resign from membership in an organization that the officer deemed incompatible with occupancy of the post. The doctor appealed the judgment of dismissal entered pursuant to the trial court's order sustaining the officer's demurrer to his petition for a writ of mandate to compel his reinstatement with back pay. The court reversed. The court rejected the officer's contention that he could dismiss the doctor without regard to his constitutional rights. While the court noted that the officer had broad discretion to determine which provisional employees he might have retained, the court also noted that it had the power and duty to review the dismissal of a provisional or probationary employee in circumstances that suggested a constitutional violation. Additionally, the court rejected the officer's argument that the doctor had not exhausted his administrative remedies. The court noted that the section of the county charter invoked by the officer did not even authorize the taking of an appeal. Thus, adjudication of the case was not barred.


OUTCOME: The judgment of dismissal entered pursuant to the order of the trial court was reversed, and the case was remanded.

Bagley v. Washington Township Hospital Dist.


S. F. No. 21831


Supreme Court of California


65 Cal. 2d 499; 421 P.2d 409; 55 Cal. Rptr. 401; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 218



December 20, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Lyle E. Cook, Judge.

Action by a public employee for reinstatement to her position, back wages, and punitive damages, and to enjoin her employer from representing to employees that their political activity was unlawful.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer was sustained to plaintiffs complaint reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered a judgment of dismissal pursuant to its order that sustained respondent hospital district's demurrer in a case whereby a public employee sought reinstatement following an alleged improper termination.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee challenged the judgment of dismissal entered by the lower court that sustained respondent hospital district's demurrer. Appellant was a public employee and sought reinstatement to her position, back wages, punitive damages, and sought to enjoin respondent from representing to employees that their political activity was unlawful. The court reversed the lower court judgment and remanded for further proceedings because the restraints respondent placed upon the political activities of its employees advanced no compelling public interest and exacted a waiver of constitutional rights without compensating public benefit. The court held that only "compelling" public interests could justify a governmental entity in demanding a waiver of constitutional rights as a condition of public employment. The court concluded that respondent did not attempt to demonstrate that the political restrictions related to the general purposes of civil service legislation and found the restrictions attempted too wide a prohibition upon public employees' constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court that sustained respondent hospital district's demurrer and remanded for further proceedings because the restraints respondent placed upon the political activities of its employees advanced no compelling public interest and was an improper waiver of constitutional rights.

Hallinan v. Committee of Bar Examiners, State Bar


S. F. No. 22295


Supreme Court of California


65 Cal. 2d 447; 421 P.2d 76; 55 Cal. Rptr. 228; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 214



December 15, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review a decision of the Committee of Bar Examiners refusing to certify an applicant for admission to the practice of law.



DISPOSITION: Order issued requiring committee to certify applicant as qualified to practice law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant appealed a decision of respondent California Committee of Bar Examiners in which committee refused to certify applicant for admission to the practice of law.


OVERVIEW: Committee refused to certify applicant for admission to the practice of law on the ground that he was not of good moral character. Applicant had been involved in a number of civil disobedience incidents and he had also been involved in other physical altercations with others where he had either defended himself or another person. The court held that not every intentional violation of the law was not grounds for excluding an individual from membership in the legal profession and that the question of whether an individual was fit to practice law turned upon whether he had committed or was likely to commit acts of moral turpitude. The court held that the nature of the acts with which applicant had been involved were not acts of moral turpitude and that they bore no direct relationship with his fitness to practice law. Committee's conclusion that applicant was unfit was not justified by the record. Committee was ordered to certify applicant as qualified to practice law.


OUTCOME: The court ordered committee to certify applicant to the court as one qualified to be admitted to practice law.

Wilke & Holzheiser, Inc. v. Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control


S. F. No. 22212


Supreme Court of California


65 Cal. 2d 349; 420 P.2d 735; 55 Cal. Rptr. 23; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 208



December 1, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 28, 1966. McComb, J., and Peters, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Byron Arnold, Judge.

Proceedings in mandamus to review certain administrative proceedings of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control and of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board suspending and revoking liquor licenses.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writs affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff liquor retailer appealed judgments of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied writs of mandate seeking review of decisions of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control suspending plaintiff's licenses for selling distilled spirits in violation of the mandatory retail price maintenance provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code, §§ 24749- 24757.


OVERVIEW: The California Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control suspended or revoked the licenses of the liquor retailer's stores for selling distilled spirits in violation of the mandatory retail price maintenance provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act (ABC Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code, §§24749- 24757. On appeal from the denial of writs of mandate, the court affirmed, holding that the price maintenance provisions were not unconstitutional. The promotion of temperance in the consumption of alcoholic beverages and of orderly conditions in their marketing constituted proper legislative objectives. The legislation was reasonably related to that legitimate objective. The ABC Act did not unlawfully delegate legislative power by enabling each producer and wholesaler to set the price below which a retailer could not sell its product. Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755.1 did not preclude the imposition of the penalties of license suspension and revocation rendered against the retailer as that statute, enacted after the licenses were suspended, applied only prospectively.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying writs of mandate seeking review of the suspension of the retailer's licenses was affirmed on the grounds that the price maintenance provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act did not transgress constitutional requirements.

Prosecution for first degree murder.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which following a jury penalty trial imposed the death penalty on defendant for two murders in the first degree.

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which imposed the death penalty on defendant for murder in the first degree of two individuals.

Sokol v. Public Utilities Commission


S. F. No. 22187


Supreme Court of California


65 Cal. 2d 247; 418 P.2d 265; 53 Cal. Rptr. 673; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 194



September 29, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Intervener's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 26, 1966.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review a decision of the Public Utilities Commission reaffirming its rule requiring a communications utility to summarily discontinue service to a subscriber when advised by any law enforcement agency that the service is being used for unlawful purposes.



DISPOSITION: Decision annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner operator of a club requested review by the court of an order from respondent California Public Utilities Commission in decision 69510 that reaffirmed the commission's decision number 41415 obligating the real party in interest telephone company to immediately terminate service when the law enforcement agency advised the company that the service was being used by the operator for an unlawful purpose.


OVERVIEW: After the law enforcement agency notified the company that the operator of the club which made predictions of the fastest racehorses was using the club's telephone service for the unlawful purposes of illegal bookmaking activity, the company disconnected that service. The operator brought a complaint requesting that the commission repeal its decision 41415 as providing an unconstitutional rule and that the commission should modify its decision 63178, which held that the evidence was insufficient to prove that the operator used the telephone facilities for an illegal purpose. The court annulled the commission's decision 69510 because its rule of decision 41415 involved a constitutional infirmity, although its defect could be cured by a proper amendment of the rule. The court found no rule of universal application on an individual's right to present his views at a hearing before the institution of action affecting his substantial rights. The court recognized the agency's interest in immediate disconnections. The court, however, found that decision 41415 would adversely effect legal businesses and deprive telephone customers of state and federal constitutional rights to free speech.


OUTCOME: The court reviewed the order of the commission in decision 69510 and annulled the commission's decision number 41415, which required the company to immediately terminate service after the law enforcement agency informed the company that the operator was using the service for the unlawful purpose of predicting the fastest racehorses for illegal bookmaking. The court held that decision 41415 failed to meet certain constitutional requirements.

In re Perez


Crim. No. 10031


Supreme Court of California


65 Cal. 2d 224; 418 P.2d 6; 53 Cal. Rptr. 414; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 191



September 21, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Petitioner discharged from the custody of prison authorities and committed to the custody of the sheriff of Los Angeles County for arraignment and pronouncement of judgment; in all other respects, petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging his convictions in the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) for lewd acts against children, oral copulation, and kidnapping, while confined in state prison as a sexual psychopath for another crime.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner pled guilty to statutory rape and was committed as a sexual psychopath for an indeterminate period. While in state prison, the prisoner signed a document authorizing the trial court to impose sentence in his absence on his convictions in another county. The state claimed that the prisoner's petition was untimely. The court denied the prisoner's petition, but directed that the prisoner be arraigned for pronouncement of judgment. The court held that the prisoner's delay in applying for the writ of habeas corpus was sufficiently explained, that the prisoner's confinement in prison was a commitment as a sexual psychopath for another offense, and that Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2a applied. The court held that a waiver of the right to counsel was not implied from the prisoner's execution of a document that waived only the right to personal presence and did not mention the right to counsel, that the prisoner was not afforded the assistance of counsel when the trial court pronounced sentence, and that the prisoner was entitled to be arraigned in person and while represented by counsel.


OUTCOME: The court denied the prisoner's petition for a writ of habeas corpus, but discharged the prisoner from the custody of the state prison officials and committed prisoner to the custody of the county sheriff. The court ordered that the prisoner be arraigned for pronouncement of judgment.

In re Watkins

PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus or coram nobis to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted with directions.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus following his conviction for issuing checks with insufficient funds in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 476a.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus regarding his conviction for violating Cal. Penal Code § 476a by issuing checks with insufficient funds. The court partially granted that writ. The court reasoned that defendant was improperly convicted of two felony violations of § 476a because each of the checks, which totaled more than $ 50, for which he was convicted were for less than $ 50. 267 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Klor


Crim. No. 9124


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 816; 415 P.2d 791; 51 Cal. Rptr. 903; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 313



July 7, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from custody after the Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) affirmed his conviction of violating Cal. Penal Code § 311.2, which proscribed the distribution and exhibition of obscene matter.


OVERVIEW: Police officers entered petitioner's home under the authority of an arrest warrant for a parking violation, but advised petitioner that they had received complaints that he produced lewd films. Petitioner showed the officers where he kept the films and allowed the officers to take two reels, but told the officers that the films were not edited or ready for distribution. Petitioner was charged with violating § 311.2 and the trial court instructed the jury that it should convict petitioner if it found the materials obscene and found that petitioner had either prepared the material or possessed it with intent to distribute. The court granted the writ and held that the jury instruction was improper. The court found that the intent of the statute was not to punish the mere preparation or possession of obscene material, but only to punish for the dissemination of the material, because the word "prepares" was to be read in conjunction with the phrase "with intent to distribute." Because the evidence was strong that the jury actually convicted petitioner of only preparing obscene films, petitioner met his burden of proving that he was convicted under an invalid portion of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's writ and discharged him from custody.

 

268 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Shell Oil Co. v. State Board of Equalization


Sac. No. 7576


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 713; 414 P.2d 820; 51 Cal. Rptr. 524; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 304



June 6, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Shell Oil Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization, 386 U.S. 211, 17 L. Ed. 2d 870, 87 S. Ct. 973, 1967 U.S. LEXIS 2144 (1967)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Stanley W. Reckers, Judge.

Action for refund of state retail sales taxes collected on sales of fuel oil to ships engaged in interstate and foreign commerce.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied the taxpayer a refund of state retail sales taxes collected on sales of fuel oil to ships engaged in interstate and foreign commerce, in the taxpayer's action against respondent state board of equalization. The trial court upheld the tax as constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer sold and delivered bunker fuel oil to ships of foreign registry engaged in foreign commerce and to ships of domestic registry engaged in foreign and interstate commerce. The taxpayer sought a refund of retail sales taxes collected on these transactions pursuant to the California Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6001 et seq., as prohibited by the import-export and commerce clauses of the United States Constitution. The trial court upheld the tax as constitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court concluded that the sales tax on bunker fuel oil was a constitutional tax levied upon a subject related to, but nevertheless distinct and separable from, articles of export, and it did not violate the import-export clause of the federal constitution. The state did not violate the commerce clause by its general imposition of a non-discriminatory sales tax upon the fuel purchases made locally for the purposes of consumption in ships engaged in interstate commerce. The tax was similar to the state's permissible taxation of fuel sold to airplanes used in interstate commerce. The taxpayer failed to demonstrate that the sales were exposed to multiple tax burdens.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the imposition of state retail sales taxes on sales of fuel oil to ship engaged in interstate and foreign commerce was not prohibited under the import-export and commerce clauses of the United States Constitution.

 

269 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Otsuka v. Hite


L. A. No. 28537


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 596; 414 P.2d 412; 51 Cal. Rptr. 284; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 290



May 24, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Burnett Wolfson, Judge.

Action to compel a county registrar of voters to register as electors persons who had been convicted of violating the Selective Service Act.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment denying the relief sought reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voters sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld the decision of defendant registrar of voters refusing to register plaintiffs as voters under Cal. Elec. Code § 350.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff voters brought an action against defendant registrar of voters, pursuant to defendant's refusal to register plaintiffs as voters pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 350. The trial court affirmed defendant's decision, holding that because plaintiffs had violated the Selective Service Act, former 50 U.S.C.S. app. § 311, plaintiffs' convictions rendered them ineligible to be voters under Cal. Const. art. II, § 1. On appeal, the court reversed the denial of plaintiffs' registrations, ruling that their violation of the Selective Service Act did not brand them as morally corrupt and dishonest men convicted of an "infamous crime" under § 1. The court held that plaintiffs' crime was not "infamous" and they were not a threat to the integrity of the elective process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of defendant registrar of voters, which refused to register plaintiff voters, ruling that plaintiffs' violation of the Selective Service Act did not brand them as morally corrupt and dishonest men convicted of an infamous crime within the meaning of the state constitiution, so as to deprive them of the right to vote..

 

270 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Prendergast v. Snyder


L. A. No. 28422


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 877; 413 P.2d 847; 50 Cal. Rptr. 903; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 324



May 10, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 8, 1966. White, J., * Sat in Place of Mosk, J. McComb, J., and White, J., * were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Martin Katz, Judge.

Action to enjoin defendant from evicting plaintiffs from rented premises by reason of plaintiff husband's race and cross-action seeking a declaration that termination of the tenancy was not invalid.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiffs entered on defendant's motion for summary judgment on the cross-complaint, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landlord appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of plaintiff tenants, a husband and wife, on his motion for summary judgment on his cross-complaint to the tenants' action to enjoin him from evicting them from rented premises by reason of the tenant husband's race.


OVERVIEW: The tenants brought an action against the landlord seeking to enjoin him from evicting them from their rented premises by reason of the husband's race. The landlord brought a cross-action seeking a declaration that the termination of the tenancy was not invalid. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the tenants upon the landlord's motion for summary judgment on the cross-action, and he appealed. The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that Cal. Const. art. I, § 26, upon which the landlord relied for the declaration of his rights, was, in its entirety, an unconstitutional infringement of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The Fourteenth Amendment, through the equal protection clause, proscribed discrimination based on race where directly practiced by a state and also if practiced by private persons where to some significant extent the state in any of its manifestations had been found to have become involved in it. The prohibited involvement occurred when a state court enforced the racial discriminatory act of a private individual relating to occupancy of residential real property in cases where affirmative relief was sought in aid or furtherance of the discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.
