Redevelopment Agency of Fresno v. Buckman


S. F. No. 22017


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 886; 413 P.2d 856; 50 Cal. Rptr. 912; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 326



May 10, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the chairman of a municipal redevelopment agency to enter into and execute on behalf of the agency a contract with the federal government for a loan and grant.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner municipal redevelopment agency (agency) brought a mandamus action to compel respondent chairman of the agency to enter into and execute on behalf of the agency a contract with the federal government for a loan and grant for the redevelopment of a blighted area.


OVERVIEW: In order to receive the loan and grant, the federal government required the agency to comply with Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 (Title VI), 78 Stat. 252, and federal regulations pertaining to anti-discrimination on the basis of race, color, or national origin. The chairman contended that the anti-discrimination provisions of Title VI were inconsistent with Cal. Const. art. I, § 26, which prohibited the agency from denying the right of any redeveloper within the project to sell, lease, or rent real property to anyone. The agency contended that the chairman's refusal to execute the contract resulted in irreparable injury and sought a declaration that Cal. Const. art. I, § 26 did not apply to redevelopment programs. The court denied the the agency's petition for a writ of mandate, but held that Cal. Const. art. I, § 26 was unconstitutional in its entirety.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the agency's petition for a writ of mandamus to compel the chairman to enter into a redevelopment contract with the federal government.

 

272 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mulkey v. Reitman


L.A. No. 28360


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 529; 413 P.2d 825; 50 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 340



May 10, 1966, Decided.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 8, 1966, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. White, J., * Sat in Place of Mosk, J. McComb, J., and White, J., * were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Raymond Thompson, Judge. Action for general and statutory damages for refusing to rent an apartment to plaintiffs on the ground of race in violation of the Unruh Civil Rights Act.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Summary judgment for defendants (treated as a judgment on the pleadings) reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff renters appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of respondents, a manager and owner of an apartment building, in plaintiffs' action for relief under the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51- 52.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff renters sought to rent an unoccupied apartment from respondents, a manager and owner of an apartment building. Plaintiffs were minorities and respondents refused to rent to them. Plaintiffs filed an action under the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51- 52. The trial court granted respondents' motion for judgment on the pleadings. The court reversed on appeal, concluding that Cal. Const. art. I, § 26 did not conform to U.S. Const. amend. XIV and was therefore void. The court determined that Cal. Const. art. I, § 26 provided a purported constitutional right to privately discriminate on grounds that were unavailable under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found that state action was sufficiently involved because the state took action through its legislature to change its laws to permit private discriminatory practices that were previously restricted. Cal. Const. art. I, § 26 was void in its entirety because the right to discriminate was fully integrated, and thus the severability clause did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of respondents, a manager and owner of an apartment building, holding that a state constitutional provision purporting to grant a right to privately discriminate against plaintiff renters was a violation of the Fourteenth Amendment.

 

273 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reynoso


Crim. No. 9656


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 432; 412 P.2d 812; 50 Cal. Rptr. 468; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 269



April 15, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. John W. Kerrigan, Judge.

Prosecution for illegal possession of heroin.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of a drug-related offense and committed to a state rehabilitation center. While there, defendant was charged with, and pleaded guilty to, possessing heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. A writ of habeas corpus was issued. After subsequent proceedings, defendant was sentenced to prison. The District Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District (California), affirmed the judgment, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to violating § 11500. Defendant was again committed to the center after the trial court suspended the proceedings. After his second commitment, defendant successfully obtained a writ of habeas corpus. Defendant was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings; it sentenced defendant to prison. Defendant appealed after the appellate court affirmed. He asserted that the sentence constituted double punishment for the same offense in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 654, that he had served his sentence through his prior confinement at the center, and that that he was denied equal protection. In affirming, the court modified but adopted the appellate court's opinion. Defendant's commitment for treatment at the center was not a penal sentence. He did not suffer double punishment when he was subsequently sentenced to prison for heroin possession, and his time spent in the center did not constitute time served on a prison sentence. No equal protection violation occurred. Cal. Penal Code § 2900.1 merely offered an incentive for addicts to complete their treatment; defendant was treated similarly to all addicts who rejected the offered help.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.

 

274 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Patterson


Crim. No. 9224


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 357; 411 P.2d 897; 49 Cal. Rptr. 801; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 260



March 17, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted as to the transfer of petitioner to the custody of an authorized representative in another state; in all other respects writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus. The prisoner requested the court to order his unconditional release from custody on the ground that the trial courts that convicted him did not have jurisdiction over his person. Alternatively, he asserted the right to be transferred to the custody of the State of Texas in order to serve his California sentences and a prior Texas sentence concurrently.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was convicted of robbery in Texas and sentenced to a term of five years. After he was released on parole, the prisoner came to California in violation of his parole. While in California, the prisoner was convicted, on two separate occasions, of two counts of second degree burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459. The judgment provided that the sentences imposed were to be served concurrently with each other and any prior uncompleted sentences. The prisoner then filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus with the court, requesting his unconditional release from custody or to be transferred to the custody of the State of Texas in order to serve his California sentences and a prior Texas sentence concurrently. The court concluded that the prisoner's convictions were valid, but granted the writ of habeas corpus for the sole purpose of transferring the prisoner to the custody of Texas. The court held that the California trial courts had jurisdiction over the prisoner, as the prisoner entered California of his own volition; and that Cal. Penal Code § 669 required the transfer of the prisoner, as Texas would not have credited time served in California.


OUTCOME: The court granted the prisoner's writ of habeas corpus for the sole purpose of transferring petitioner to the custody of an authorized representative of the state of Texas, in order that he may serve any incomplete sentences previously imposed by that state. In all other respects, the court denied the prisoner's application for a writ of habeas corpus.

 

275 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Weaver v. Jordan


Sac. No. 7682


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 235; 411 P.2d 289; 49 Cal. Rptr. 537; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 253; 7 Rad. Reg. 2d (P & F) 2166



March 2, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Irving H. Perluss, Judge.

Declaratory relief action attacking as unconstitutional an initiative measure adopted by the electorate entitled the Free Television Act which undertakes to ban the business of home subscription television.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Summary judgment declaring act unconstitutional and declaring it to be the duty of the Secretary of State to accept for filing articles of incorporation of a corporation formed for the purpose of engaging in home subscription television affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff incorporators filed a declaratory judgment action challenging the Free Television Act (Act) after defendant Secretary of State refused to file their articles of incorporation for the formation of a corporation to engage in the home subscription television business. The Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) held that the Act violated U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV as well as Cal. Const. art. I, § 9. The secretary appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the effective date of the Free Television Act (Act), which banned the business of home subscription television in the state of California, the secretary refused to file the incorporators' articles of incorporation on the grounds that the corporation proposed to engage in an unlawful business. The trial court held that the Act abridged the free speech guarantees of the California and U.S. Constitutions. The court held that communication by television fell within First Amendment protection. The court further held that the Act amounted to total censorship, in advance, because it banned transmission when a charge was made to home viewers and that restriction of such an unlimited potential scope could be imposed only to avoid a clear and present danger of a substantive evil. However, even if true, the Act's statements that development of the subscription television business would have an adverse effect upon existing television stations, would tend to deprive viewers of their freedom of choice, or would tend to create a monopoly, did not demonstrate any clear and present danger of a substantive evil. Any suggested evil was, furthermore, well discounted by improbability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment finding that the Free Television Act was unconstitutional.

 

276 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bakersfield v. Miller


L. A. No. 28224


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 93; 410 P.2d 393; 48 Cal. Rptr. 889; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 237



February 4, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 2, 1966.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County. John Locke, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

Action to have certain portions of a hotel declared a public nuisance as defined by a city ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hotel owner challenged the power of defendant city to determine that an existing building, which failed to meet current standards of safety enacted in a municipal building code that was enacted since the construction of the building, was a public nuisance. The Superior Court of Kern County (California) found that the hotel was a public nuisance. The hotel owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The hotel owner was given notice by the city that his hotel did not conform to the city building code. The hotel owner claimed that he was financially unable to accomplish the required repairs and modifications. Over the course of several years, the city made repeated requests of the hotel owner that he comply with the city's ordinance, but the hotel owner failed to take any action with the exception of improving a fire escape. The hotel owner did not deny that the ordinance had been violated but contended that the city exceeded its legislative powers by declaring as a matter of law that the violations constituted a public nuisance. The court held that the city had the power to define by statute the standards by which courts were to judge whether a nuisance existed. The court rejected the hotel owner's contention that state housing requirements had preempted the field of regulation. Finally, the court found no constitutional violation occurred by applying the standards of fire safety incorporated in the code to buildings constructed prior to the code's enactment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the hotel was a nuisance.

In re Carlson


Crim. No. 9236


Supreme Court of California


64 Cal. 2d 70; 410 P.2d 379; 48 Cal. Rptr. 875; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 234



February 2, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied March 2, 1966. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition with the court for a writ of habeas corpus, which sought his release from custody from the prison sentence that he was serving for a conviction of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500, and his sentence was enhanced due to his prior conviction for a violation of Cal. Heath & Safety Code § 11721. Subsequently, after the inmate had served greater than the maximum sentence that he could have served had his sentence not been enhanced, the inmate filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus that sought his release from custody. The court denied the inmate's petition. The court held that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721 was unconstitutional only to the extent that it purported to constitute the status of narcotics addiction as a crime. The court also held that although § 11721 did not contain a severability clause, the unconstitutional portion of the statute was severable from the remainder of the statute. The court found that there was limited information from the record regarding the previous conviction under § 11721. The court ruled that there was a presumption of regularity to the judgment, which placed the burden upon the inmate to prove that his conviction was not based on the constitutional portion of the statute. The court concluded that the inmate failed to satisfy his burden.


OUTCOME: The court denied the inmate's habeas corpus petition.

 

278 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Watson


L.A. No. 28441


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 829; 409 P.2d 481; 48 Cal. Rptr. 481; 1966 Cal. LEXIS 332



January 11, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 9, 1966. Traynor, C. J., and Peters, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Assessor of Los Angeles County to advise school boards within the county in writing of the estimated total assessed valuation of all taxable property in their respective districts for the fiscal year 1965-66.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied but the assessor is directed to supply such information in response to future requests.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school districts sought, in a proceeding in mandate, to compel respondent assessor to advise the county school boards in writing of the estimated total assessed valuation of all taxable property in their respective districts for the fiscal year. The authority the districts cited was Cal. Educ. Code § 20811, which the assessor claimed was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The assessor maintained that because § 20811 limited its operation to school districts in counties with a population exceeding 4 million, it constituted special legislation in violation of the 10th, 28th, and 33rd clauses of Cal. Const. art. IV, § 25. The court directed the assessor to provide the requested information in the future, holding that: 1) although the time for performance by the assessor of the duties prescribed by § 20811 had long since expired for the fiscal year, the basic issues presented were not moot because the obligation to provide the information recurred annually; 2) the court could not say, as it would be required to do in order to hold § 20811 unconstitutional, that no state of facts could reasonably be conceived to justify the classification made; 3) the fact that the assessor might not be able to comply with § 20811 in one comparatively minor respect did not compel invalidation of the section in its entirety; and 4) because the section required the assessor to provide only an estimate of assessed valuation, it did not seem unreasonable to assume that a meaningful estimate of the succeeding year's values could be made by the middle of May.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ as moot with regard to the fiscal year, but directed the assessor to comply with the statute by supplying the information in response to future requests by the districts.

 

279 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Griffin


Crim. No. 8752


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 757; 408 P.2d 959; 48 Cal. Rptr. 183; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 234



December 23, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, prison inmate, sought a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from custody following his conviction of sales of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531 and a prior felony conviction under the same section.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, prison inmate, brought a petition for habeas corpus. He had been convicted of sales of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531 and was also convicted under the same section in a prior felony conviction. The court questioned whether, if prior to finality of a judgment of conviction, the statute under which petitioner was charged and convicted was amended so as to decrease the punishment, the petitioner was entitled to the benefits of the amendatory statute. The court held that he was, if that were the only point involved in the instant case. But it was not the only point involved. Here, the amendatory statute only in a limited sense ameliorated the punishment. In a very real and practical sense it increased the penalty. Since the amendment, considered as a whole, increased the penalty it could not be applied to petitioner without violating the constitutional inhibition against ex post facto legislation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 3. The court held that, as applied to petitioner, when considered realistically and as a whole, the amendatory statute increased the punishment, and so could not be made applicable to petitioner.


OUTCOME: The order to show cause was discharged and the writ of habeas corpus was denied.

 

280 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Estrada


Crim. No. 8524


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 740; 408 P.2d 948; 48 Cal. Rptr. 172; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 232



December 23, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 19, 1966. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Schauer, J., * were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate requested a writ of habeas corpus to secure release from custody, asserting that the amended version of the criminal statute should have been applied to his punishment because the amendment occurred after the prohibited act was committed but before final judgment was entered.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was convicted of escape without force or violence in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4530. At the time of his escape, Cal. Penal Code § 3044 provided that no person convicted of an escape could be paroled until he had served at least two calendar years from and after the date of his return to the prison after such conviction. Several months after the act of escape was committed, these two sections were amended to reduce the penalties formerly provided. The court held that since the amendatory statute lessening punishment became effective prior to the date the judgment of conviction became final, it, and not the old statute in effect when the prohibited act was committed, should have been applied. The court stated that when the legislature amended a statute, which lessened the punishment, it had determined that its former penalty was too severe and that a lighter punishment was proper. The court ordered that the Adult Authority fix petitioner's sentence as provided in the amended statute and determine his eligibility for parole accordingly, but denied petitioner's writ because he was not entitled to release.


OUTCOME: The court denied a writ of habeas corpus and discharged the order to show cause, but remanded with directions to fix petitioner inmate's sentence to comply with the amended version of the statute, as well as to determine petitioner's eligibility for parole. Petitioner was not entitled to release.

 

281 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cockrell


Crim. No. 9015


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 659; 408 P.2d 116; 47 Cal. Rptr. 788; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 225



December 9, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 26, 1966. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Evelle J. Younger, Judge.

Prosecution for illegal sale of marijuana, possession of marijuana for sale and conspiracy to sell marijuana.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, husband and wife, appealed the judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of the illegal sale of marijuana, possession of marijuana for sale, and conspiracy to sell marijuana. They contended that the evidence was obtained by an unlawful search of their home, that the evidence was insufficient to support the conviction, and that statements by the wife were inadmissible.


OVERVIEW: An undercover agent bought marijuana from a third party who received it from the husband and wife's house. Officers went to the house and knocked on the door, identified themselves, and requested admittance, then forced the door open when no one responded. The wife was inside the premises and agreed to allow the officers to look around. They found marijuana and the money used in the undercover operation. When the officers located the husband, marijuana was found in his vehicle. The husband and wife appealed their convictions for the illegal sale and possession of marijuana and conspiracy. The court affirmed and held that the search of the house was substantially contemporaneous with the wife's lawful arrest. The officers had probable cause to force the door open after they complied with Cal. Penal Code § 844. The search of the car was lawful as well because the husband consented to it. Evidence was sufficient to show an agreement between the husband, the wife, and the third party to sell marijuana and overt acts in furtherance of that agreement. The admission of the wife's attempted exculpatory statements after the search of her house did not result in a miscarriage of justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions of the husband and wife for the illegal sale of marijuana, possession of marijuana for sale, and conspiracy to sell marijuana.

 

282 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thayer


Crim. No. 9272


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 635; 408 P.2d 108; 47 Cal. Rptr. 780; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 222



December 6, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 5, 1966.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from orders denying new trials. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.

Prosecution for submitting and for conspiring to submit fraudulent claims for payment for medical services allegedly rendered to welfare patients.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed; appeals from orders dismissed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of judgments from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of having made fraudulent claims for payment for medical services rendered to welfare patients pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 72. Defendants claimed that their billing records, seized under a warrant pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1524, were obtained unconstitutionally.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with having made fraudulent claims for payment for medical services rendered to welfare patients. The prosecution introduced defendants' billing records that had been seized under warrant. Defendants claimed that the introduction of the records violated their rights pursuant to U.S. Const. amends. IV, V, XIV, because they were merely evidence of crime and not contraband, instruments of crime, or fruits of crime. Defendants were convicted and sought review. The court affirmed the judgments and held that: (1) the seizure by warrant was specifically authorized by Cal. Penal Code § 1524, which specifically rejected the "mere evidence" rule; (2) the records were the instruments of crime; and (3) business records were not constitutionally protected from properly authorized searches and seizures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments that convicted defendants of having made fraudulent claims for payment for medical services rendered to welfare patients, and the denial of their motions for new trial.

 

283 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Grubb


Crim. No. 8166


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 614; 408 P.2d 100; 47 Cal. Rptr. 772; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 219



December 2, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 12, 1966. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Schauer, J., * were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County. Edward Henderson, Judge.

Prosecution for unlawful possession of weapon of a kind commonly known as a billy.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed solely on the constitutional ground announced in People v. Dorado, 62 Cal.2d 338 [42 Cal.Rptr. 169, 398 P.2d 361].


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Ventura County convicted defendant of possession of a weapon commonly known as a billy, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12020. Defendant appealed his conviction.


OVERVIEW: The court determined that statements that defendant made to police officers about his possession and use of the billy club should not have been admitted, because defendant was not informed of his right to counsel. Because defendant's description of the object placed it precisely into the statutory design, such statements were pointedly prejudicial. The court rejected defendant's argument that officers did not discover the billy by means of an illegal entry because the court determined that warrantless entry into defendant's automobile was sustained by extraordinary and exceptional circumstances. Furthermore, the court determined that Cal. Penal Code § 12020 was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction.

 

284 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Tracy v. Contractors' State License Board


Sac. No. 7685


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 598; 407 P.2d 865; 47 Cal. Rptr. 561; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 216



November 23, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Elvin F. Sheehy, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Contractors' State License Board to reinstate a contractor's license.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment granting writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Contractors' State License Board appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) granting plaintiff contractor's action for mandamus and ordering defendant to reinstate plaintiff's contractor's license.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contractor filed for bankruptcy. Defendant license board suspended plaintiff's license pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7113.5 until plaintiff paid claims for employee wages and gave a bond pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7071.5. Plaintiff sought an order to reinstate plaintiff's license. Plaintiff's request was granted and defendant appealed. The court reversed and held that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7113.5 was not unconstitutional and that defendant did not abuse its discretion when it imposed the penalty on plaintiff. Section 7113.5 constituted a proper exercise of the state's police power because plaintiff's bankruptcy related to his license. The penalty was designed to promote the health, safety, and welfare of the public. There was no abuse of discretion in imposing the penalty because it applied to a selected group of creditors who were injured by plaintiff's operation, and there was high public interest in protecting them and making them whole. The penalties required plaintiff to pay pension, health, welfare, and vacation funds that were due to plaintiff's employees at the time plaintiff filed bankruptcy. The payments were preferred claims in bankruptcy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the granting of plaintiff's action for mandamus and held that the penalties imposed on plaintiff under a state statute were not unconstitutional and defendant did not abuse its discretion when it imposed the penalties on plaintiff.

 

285 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aranda


Crim. No. 9078


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 518; 407 P.2d 265; 47 Cal. Rptr. 353; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 206



November 12, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph A. Wapner, Judge.

Prosecution for robbery.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgments of conviction of first degree robbery reversed as to one defendant, and reversed solely on the constitutional ground announced in People v. Dorado, 62 Cal.2d 338 [42 Cal.Rptr. 169, 398 P.2d 361] as to second defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of robbery in the first degree in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211a.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of robbery in the first degree of a jewelry store. Fingerprints matched one defendant known to an officer. The officer offered defendant a ride, and defendant led the officers to the other defendant's apartment where both defendants were arrested. Three shells were found in the apartment, but the gun used in the robbery was never found. The first defendant allegedly confessed, but the confession was not recorded. On appeal, the court held that where nothing in the record indicated that defendant had been advised of his rights or that he had waived them, the confession was inadmissible. Because it was reasonably probable that a result more favorable to the non-confessing defendant would have been reached if the confession had been excluded, the error resulted in a miscarriage of justice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions for robbery in the first degree.

 

286 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Spencer


Crim. No. 8119


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 400; 406 P.2d 33; 46 Cal. Rptr. 753; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 194



October 1, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to review judgment of death imposed on conviction of murder and armed robbery. LeRoy Dawson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted as to penalty trial; judgment reversed as to penalty only. Petitioner remanded to custody of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County for a new penalty trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a writ of habeas corpus to review the judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which imposed the death penalty on the inmate's conviction of murder and armed robbery. The inmate argued that his constitutional rights were violated by admission into evidence of statements he made before he was advised of his right to counsel.


OVERVIEW: The court held that when the inmate had been arrested and the police had undertaken a process of interrogation that lent itself to eliciting incriminating statements, the accusatory or critical stage had been reached and the inmate was entitled to counsel. The court held that because the inmate was questioned during the accusatory stage, when he had not been advised of his rights to counsel and to remain silent, the statements should not have been admitted into his trial. The court held that because a court appointed psychiatrist questioned the inmate during the critical stage without the presence of counsel, admission of the psychiatrist's testimony that the inmate admitted guilt was a violation of the inmate's constitutional rights. However, the court held that neither of these violations was so prejudicial as to require reversal. The court granted the writ of habeas corpus to the inmate regarding the penalty phase of the trial and reversed the judgment of the death penalty because the trial court instructed the jury that it could consider that a prisoner serving a life sentence could be paroled.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court as to the death penalty.

 

287 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mathis


Crim. No. 8143


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 416; 406 P.2d 65; 46 Cal. Rptr. 785; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 195



October 1, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 10, 1965. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from judgments of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Robert E. Cassin, Judge.

Prosecution for murder and robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction imposing death penalty for first degree murder affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) convicted defendant of murder in the first degree and robbery in the first degree, and fixed the penalty at death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder in the first degree and robbery in the first degree and was sentenced to death after he and a friend robbed and killed a man they believed had a great deal of money in his possession. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed. The court held that (1) admission of color photographs of the victim was not erroneous because the probative value of the evidence outweighed the inflammatory effect it may have had, (2) admission of defendant's wife's testimony at trial was not contrary to the spousal privilege at Cal. Penal Code § 1322 because the testimony was taken with her consent during the penalty phase and related to prior acts of violence directed at her, and (3) defendant's prior recorded statement given to police without assistance of counsel was not erroneously played in court because defendant took the stand during trial and testified to substantially the same admissions.


OUTCOME: The judgment that convicted defendant of murder in the first degree and robbery in the first degree and that sentenced him to death was affirmed.

 

288 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Silver v. Brown


Sac. Nos. 7679, 7681


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 270; 405 P.2d 132; 46 Cal. Rptr. 308; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 183



September 1, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petitions of Respondent Governor and Certain Interveners for a Rehearing were Denied September 29, 1965.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDINGS in mandamus to compel reapportionment of the state Assembly and Senate.



DISPOSITION: Writ not issued, but reapportionment ordered and jurisdiction retained to review any reapportionment legislation enacted by December 9, 1965, and to order proposed reapportionment plans into effect if necessary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens filed an action in mandamus to compel respondent state to reapportion the California state legislature so as to bring the legislature into conformance with the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: After the legislature was reapportioned in accordance with Cal. Elec. Code §§ 30100, 30200, 30201, the United States Supreme Court held that the apportionment of state legislatures was subject to judicial challenge on equal protection grounds. The citizens filed an action to compel the state Reapportionment Commission to reapportion the state senate; the court denied mandamus. The Supreme Court then held that each house of a bicameral state legislature had to be apportioned on a population basis. At the time, actions were pending in federal district court regarding reapportionment of the legislature. The court took jurisdiction of the cases; the Supreme Court then determined that the state senate was malapportioned, and the court held that departures in the assembly were large enough to deny equal protection. No explanation existed for giving the City and County of San Francisco five instead of four assemblymen. The court in ordering reapportionment adhered to existing county and assembly district boundaries and refrained from drawing any new lines dividing counties or districts. The court's plan was effective only if the legislature failed to enact valid plans.


OUTCOME: The court did not issue writs of mandate in the citizens' action for reapportionment of the state legislature but retained jurisdiction to review any reapportionment legislation that the legislature enacted and to order the court's proposed plans into effect if necessary.

 

289 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Los Angeles v. Industrial Acci. Com.


L. A. Nos. 28254, 28255


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 255; 404 P.2d 809; 46 Cal. Rptr. 105; 30 Cal. Comp. Cases 278; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 180



August 27, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review two awards of the Industrial Accident Commission for workmen's compensation benefits to recipients of city pensions.



DISPOSITION: Awards annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated case, petitioner employer, a city, sought review of two awards of the Industrial Accident Commission (California), which awarded workmen's compensation benefits to respondent pension recipients (recipients), who were a former employee and a widow. The commission held that an estoppel barred the employer's reliance upon Cal. Lab. Code §§ 5405 and/or 5406 and refused to allow the employer any credit for the pensions paid.


OVERVIEW: One of the recipients, a former employee, suffered an injury while on duty as a city fireman for the employer. The employer paid the former employee his full salary for the year following the injury; after that it paid him a disability pension. The statute of limitations, Cal. Lab. Code, § 5405 would have barred the former employee's claim unless the employer was estopped to assert the statute. Another recipient, the widow of a deceased fireman, received a widow's pension from the employer. Cal. Lab. Code § 5406 would have barred the widow's claim unless the employer was estopped to assert the statute of limitations. City officials, who were assumed to have been authorized agents of the employer, told the recipients that because they received pensions, their filing of claims for workmen's compensation benefits would be useless. The court held that the awards of workmen's compensation benefits to the recipients were annulled. The mere fact that the employer gave erroneous advice did not in itself estop it from asserting the statute of limitations. Under the circumstances of the case, the advice given to the recipients was not unreasonable at the time that it was given.


OUTCOME: The court held that the awards of workmen's compensation benefits to the recipients were annulled.

 

290 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Franklin Life Ins. Co. v. State Board of Equalization


S. F. No. 21577


Supreme Court of California


63 Cal. 2d 222; 404 P.2d 477; 45 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 177



August 19, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 15, 1965. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Raymond J. O'Connor, Judge.

Action by an out-of-state insurer to recover certain retaliatory taxes assessed against it and paid under protest.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court that denied plaintiff's claim to recover retaliatory taxes assessed by defendant under Cal. Ins. Code § 685.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, an Illinois insurance company doing business in California, was assessed a retaliatory tax and paid the tax under protest. Illinois would have taxed a California insurer that did business in Illinois at a greater rate than an Illinois insurer and the tax assessed against plaintiff by defendant was lower than what the Illinois tax on plaintiff would have been. Cal. Ins. Code §§ 685- 685.3 imposed retaliatory taxes upon foreign insurers whenever their home states imposed taxes on similar California insurers higher than California assessed those states' insurers. Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 14 4/5, subd. (e), allowed plaintiff to deduct from the gross premium tax the amount of real property taxed paid in California and was not violated by §§ 685-685.3. Hypothesizing an unconstitutional application of the statute did not save plaintiff from its actual constitutional operation. The statute was properly applied to a company of the discriminating state and the restricted coverage did not show that the future use of the statute would become uncertain, nor would such limited coverage violate the statute's legislative design. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Lower court's judgment that retaliatory tax did not violated the California Constitution was affirmed since its constitutionality could not be attacked by hypothetical scenarios; the tax was properly applied to an insurer of a discriminating state and there was no evidence that the future application of the tax would have become uncertain.

 

291 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clark


Crim. Nos. 7610, 7615, 7616, 7617


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 870; 402 P.2d 856; 44 Cal. Rptr. 784; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 304



June 17, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 14, 1965, and the Judgment was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein. McComb, J., and Bray, J., + were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


+ Retired Presiding Justice of the District Court of Appeal sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judical Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS (one automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b)) from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph A. Wapner, Judge.

Prosecution for murder and robbery.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgments of conviction of first degree murder and first degree robbery reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendants of first degree murder and first degree robbery.


OVERVIEW: Two of the defendants were convicted as accomplices of the third defendant. The accomplices claimed that they were not aware of the principal's actions in the store where the robbery and murder took place as they waited outside in their car. The court found that the trial court erred in admitting the extrajudicial statements of the defendants made during police interrogation while the defendants were under arrest because none of the defendants were warned of their right to counsel or their right to remain silent. Furthermore, where the statements of the principal defendant amounted to a confession and where the statements of the accomplices were the only evidence offered to prove their guilt, admission of the statements was prejudicial and required reversal. The court also found that the two accomplices were deprived of their right to a speedy trial because the prosecution's desire to try all three defendants together in order to strengthen the case against the accomplices, which necessitated extradition of the principal defendant, was not good cause for the delay. Violation of these defendants' speedy trial rights required dismissal of the prosecutions against them.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions, dismissed the prosecutions against the two defendants tried as accomplices, and remanded the principal defendant's case for a new trial.

People v. O'Neil


Crim. No. 7958


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 748; 401 P.2d 928; 44 Cal. Rptr. 320; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 292; 17 A.L.R.3d 806



May 21, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Gerald C. Thomas, Judge.

Prosecution for driving a vehicle while addicted to the use of a narcotic.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of driving while addicted to a narcotic drug in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23105.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appeals from a judgment convicting him of driving while addicted to a narcotic drug. The court dealt with the definition of the term "addicted" in § 23105, and found that the trial court erred in its formulation of the term. The court reversed and remanded the cause for a determination of whether the defendant was "addicted" to the use of narcotics. The court concluded that the trial judge used the improper "habitual use" test in finding defendant addicted to the use of narcotics. The court ordered the trial court to determination of whether defendant exhibited the three characteristics of the addiction process: (1) emotional dependence on the drug, (2) an increased tolerance to its effects, and (3) physical dependence manifested by withdrawal symptoms upon sudden termination of drug intake. The court believed that when an individual reached the point that his body reacted physically to the termination of drug administration, he had become "addicted" within the meaning and purpose of § 23105.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded defendant's conviction of driving while addicted to a narcotic drug.

Addison v. Addison


L.A. No. 27167


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 558; 399 P.2d 897; 43 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 274; 14 A.L.R.3d 391



March 15, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Defendant and Appellant for a Rehearing was Denied April 14, 1965, and the Opinion and Judgment were Modified to Read as Printed Above. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein. McComb, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from portions of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Bernard S. Jefferson, Judge.

Action for divorce.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment affirmed insofar as it decrees divorce and custody of the minor child and reversed in all other respects with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife, who was granted an interlocutory decree of divorce, challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that the only community property was the household furniture and furnishings. Appellee husband challenged his obligation to pay, without recoupment, the current income tax obligations.


OVERVIEW: This case concerned the operation and constitutionality of California's quasi-community property scheme. The court reviewed the trial court's interlocutory decree of divorce and the record on appeal and held that: 1.) Because the divorce was granted on the ground of adultery, and it was the property of the adulterous spouse that was being divided as the court deemed just, no valid constitutional objection could be raised; 2.) Changes in the community property system which affect "vested interests" could not constitutionally be applied retroactively but must be limited to prospective application; 3.) It could not be successfully argued that the quasi-community property legislation was unconstitutional because of a violation of the due process clause of the federal Constitution; and 4.) The state's inherent sovereign power included the so called "police power" right to interfere with vested property rights whenever reasonably necessary to the protection of the health, safety, morals, and general well being of the people.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment insofar as it decreed divorce and custody of the minor child. The court reversed all findings regarding the division of the property of the parties and ordered the wife to recover costs on both appeals.

People v. Modesto


Crim. No. 7877


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 436; 398 P.2d 753; 42 Cal. Rptr. 417; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 262



February 11, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County. John G. Gabbert, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted him of two counts of first degree murder, determined that he was sane at the time of the crimes, and imposed the death penalty. Defendant asserted numerous challenges to the conviction, including the admissibility of statements made during a custodial interrogation and the constitutionality of the state's comment rule.


OVERVIEW: A defendant was charged with first degree murder for the killing of two girls with a sledgehammer. At trial, the defendant raised an insanity defense. Upon a jury verdict, the trial court convicted the defendant of two counts of first degree murder, determined that he was sane at the time of the crimes, and imposed the death penalty. On an automatic appeal pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1249(b), the court reversed and remanded for a new trial. Specifically, the court held that the trial court erred in admitting damaging statements made by the defendant during a custodial interrogation before he was formally charged, but after he was denied an opportunity to speak with his lawyer. In particular, the court found that all of the Escobedo conditions had been met and that it was immaterial that the defendant had spoken with his lawyer several hours before the interrogation. Finally, the court rejected the defendant's argument that the comment rule on a defendant's silence at trial under Cal. Const. art. I, § 13 and Cal. Penal Code § 1323 violated the federal privilege against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the defendant's conviction for first degree murder and remanded for a new trial. In particular, the court held that damaging statements made by the defendant during a custodial interrogation, after he was denied an opportunity to speak with his lawyer, were improperly admitted. However, the court rejected the defendant's argument that the state's comment rule on a defendant's silence at trial was unconstitutional.

In re Lopez


Crim. No. 7794


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 368; 398 P.2d 380; 42 Cal. Rptr. 188; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 254



January 29, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied February 24, 1965. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to review judgments of death imposed upon convictions of murder and affirmed on appeal. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted as to penalty trials; judgments reversed as to penalty and in all other respects affirmed. Petitioners remanded to custody of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County for new penalty trials.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and co-defendant petitioned for habeas corpus review of judgments of death imposed upon their convictions for murder, raising the issue of whether defendant was entitled to a new trial because of the admission at trial of his statements allegedly obtained in violation of his right to counsel as delineated cases decided after defendant's case was final.


OVERVIEW: At a joint trial, defendant and a co-defendant were convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. The court granted defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Although the right to counsel as established in judicial opinions rendered after the final determination of defendant's case did not apply retroactively on collateral attack. The court explained that because the justification of the requirement of the right to counsel at the accusatory stage was the prospective purpose of benefiting the overall system of criminal administration by drying up the sources of coercion, no purpose would be served by applying the judicial decisions retroactively. The court, however, concluded that defendant and co-defendant were entitled to new penalty phase hearings because the instruction as to the possible release on parole invited speculative argument to the jury.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ granted as to penalty trials, reversed the judgments as to penalty, but affirmed the judgments in all other respects.

 

296 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dorado


Crim. No. 7468


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 338; 398 P.2d 361; 42 Cal. Rptr. 169; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 253



January 29, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County. Thomas F. Keating, Judge.

Prosecution for malicious assault with a deadly weapon by a life-term inmate of a state prison, resulting in death of the victim.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which found defendant guilty of malicious assault with a deadly weapon by a life-term inmate of a state prison, resulting in death of the victim, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4500, and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found defendant guilty of malicious assault with a deadly weapon by a life-term inmate of a state prison, resulting in death of the victim, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4500, and sentenced defendant to death. On appeal, the court stated that an accused could intelligently and knowingly waive his right to counsel either at a pre-trial stage or at the trial. However, a waiver could not be effected unless it was intelligently and knowingly given. Here, defendant had the constitutional protection of an absolute right to remain silent; the prosecution could not introduce into evidence defendant's confession unless that right of silence were waived. The court concluded that defendant's confession could not be properly introduced into evidence because the authorities had not effectively informed defendant of his right to counsel or of his absolute right to remain silent, and no evidence established that he had waived these rights. The court reversed the trial court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court.

In re Cruz


Crim. No. 8063


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 307; 398 P.2d 412; 42 Cal. Rptr. 220; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 250



January 27, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody after commitment as a narcotics addict to a narcotics rehabilitation center.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate brought an original habeas corpus proceeding to secure his release from custody after commitment as a narcotics addict to a narcotics rehabilitation center. A show cause order was issued upon this application.


OVERVIEW: The inmate claimed that his commitment was invalid because his waiver was not obtained in compliance with Cal. Penal Code § 6450. The inmate also claimed that his transfer from a treatment center to a regular penal institution constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court rejected these arguments, discharged the order to show cause, and denied the petition for habeas corpus. The court held that the inmate voluntarily waived his right to a hearing and that his commitment was valid. The court found that the inmate was first fully informed of the nature and purpose of the proceeding and his rights, an attorney was appointed to represent the inmate, and that he was advised regarding the proposed waiver. The court also found that medical examinations verified the inmate's addicted condition and that the inmate was questioned individually regarding the waiver and treatment. The court held that the requisite procedures were followed and that the waiver was valid. The court also upheld the inmate's transfer as a proper exercise of authority. The court also held that the transfer was warranted by the inmate's failure to cooperate in the treatment and rehabilitation programs.


OUTCOME: The order to show cause was discharged and the petition for habeas corpus was denied.

 

298 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Victor


Sac. No. 7600


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 280; 398 P.2d 391; 42 Cal. Rptr. 199; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 249



January 27, 1965 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County committing defendant to a narcotics treatment facility as one in imminent danger of narcotics addiction. Thomas B. Quinn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order by which the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California) committed him to a narcotics treatment facility as one in imminent danger of narcotics addiction pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 6500 et seq. Defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutional and that there was no jurisdiction to hear the commitment proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of misdemeanor possession of narcotic paraphernalia pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11555 and was ordered given a partially suspended sentence. Later, the lower court committed him to further custody for placement in the narcotic rehabilitation program as a person in imminent danger of becoming addicted to narcotics, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 6500 et seq. Defendant then appealed. The court found that (1) the presence of an element of degree in the definition of a person in imminent danger of becoming addicted to narcotics did not render the statutory language insufficiently certain for due process, (2) while it was insufficient for an individual to have merely begun to experiment with drugs, it was not necessary that defendant actually be addicted in order for the statutory provisions at issue to apply to him, and (3) Cal. Penal Code § 6500 et seq. did not give jurisdiction to entertain commitment proceedings where the statutory provisions for such commitment were not satisfied, insofar as commitment after conviction was not statutorily permitted and there was no express authority for commitment under the circumstances at issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment.

 

299 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Waltreus


Crim. No. 7932


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 218; 397 P.2d 1001; 42 Cal. Rptr. 9; 1965 Cal. LEXIS 241



January 15, 1965 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied February 24, 1965. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody. Lewis Drucker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner, after exhausting his appeal of right, filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus in the court alleging that his conviction was obtained by perjured testimony knowingly used by the State.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner argued that several of the State's witnesses committed perjury with the knowledge of the State. The prisoner argued that the contradictory testimony presented at trial was sufficient to establish that the witnesses had perjured themselves and that the State knew of the perjury. The court disagreed with the prisoner's arguments and pointed out that he had failed to show that the alleged perjured testimony could have been proven by testimony that existed independent of the contradictory testimony at trial. The prisoner also failed to demonstrate that he did not have an opportunity at trial to present the alleged truth of the matter related to the perjured testimony. Finally, the prisoner raised three additional issues pertaining to the sufficiency of the evidence, the suppression of evidence favorable to the prisoner, and failure to appoint trial counsel, which the court denied because these issues had already been decided by the prisoner's first appeal.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the show cause order and denied the prisoner's petition.

In re Norwalk Call


L. A. No. 27516


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 185; 397 P.2d 426; 41 Cal. Rptr. 666; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 172



December 24, 1964 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 20, 1965. Traynor, C. J., Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County declaring a newspaper to be one of general circulation. F. Ray Bennett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contestant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared respondent newspaper to be one of general circulation.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper sought a decree declaring it to be a newspaper of general circulation in the city. The contestant filed an answer opposing the decree. The trial court ruled in favor of the newspaper. On review, the court affirmed. For many years prior to the enactment of legislation defining a newspaper of general circulation, the newspaper conducted its operations in the township, then unincorporated, in a manner entitling it to be a newspaper of general circulation, and it continued to operate in such a manner. The newspaper continued to be qualified as a newspaper of general circulation after the definition was changed because Cal. Gov't Code § 6006 provided an exemption. Thereafter, a proviso was added to § 6006 which resulted in the newspaper losing its status. However, the proviso was eliminated two years later. The newspaper should not be prohibited from relying on an exemption for the sole reason that during some intervening period of time in the past it did not meet another requirement added by the legislature and subsequently eliminated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the newspaper in its action that had sought a declaration that it was a newspaper of general circulation.

Paul v. Milk Depots, Inc.

Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 129; 396 P.2d 924; 41 Cal. Rptr. 468; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 166

302 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Hubbard


Crim. No. 7824


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 119; 396 P.2d 809; 41 Cal. Rptr. 393; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 165



November 30, 1964 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied December 23, 1964. Burke, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody. A. Curtis Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were arrested for violating Long Beach, Cal., Mun. Code § 4140.7, because they had been playing, for money, a game of chance played with cards. A petition for a writ of habeas corpus was filed to test the validity of the ordinance. Defendants argued that the phrase "game of chance" as used in the ordinance was so vague as to render the ordinance invalid and that the ordinance was void due to preemption.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested for violations of Long Beach, Cal., Mun. Code § 4140.7, in that they had been playing for money, within the city limits, a game of chance played with cards, known as panguingui. Assuming that panguingui was a game of chance, it was one of the games prohibited by the ordinance, but was not a "banking or percentage game," as that phrase was used in Cal. Penal Code § 330. Defendants attacked the ordinance on the dual grounds that: (1) the phrase "game of chance" was so vague as to render the ordinance invalid, and (2) it was unconstitutional and void because the state Legislature preempted the field of gambling. However, as the ordinance set forth in clear terms what type of game was prohibited, and under what circumstances, it was not void for uncertainty or vagueness. By limiting the general statutes to regulation or prohibition of specifically enumerated activities, the Legislature had not intended to prevent local authority from legislating on those subjects in regard to which the former were silent. Therefore, the state had not occupied the entire gaming field to the extent that the general laws conflicted with an ordinance that regulated games of chance.


OUTCOME: The order to show cause was discharged, and the petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied. The phrase "game of chance" as used in the ordinance was not so vague as to have rendered the ordinance invalid, as the ordinance set forth in clear terms what type of game was prohibited, and under what circumstances. The ordinance was not void due to preemption, as the State Legislature had not intended to occupy the entire field of gaming.

 

303 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kevelin v. Jordan


Sac. No. 7626


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 82; 396 P.2d 585; 41 Cal. Rptr. 169; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 159



November 18, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to direct the Secretary of State not to file in his office a statement of the vote on an allegedly unconstitutional initiative measure submitted to the voters and passed at a general election.



DISPOSITION: Petition denied without prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of mandamus to direct respondent, the California Secretary of State, not to file in his office a statement of the vote on an allegedly unconstitutional initiative measure submitted to the voters and passed at a general election.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners sought to prevent a filing of a statement of the vote on Proposition 15. Petitioners argued that the title of that proposition was defective and that it violated various provisions of the United States Constitution and the California Constitution. Upon review, the court denied the petition. The court held that Cal. Const. art. IV, § 1, and Cal. Elec. Code § 18756 imposed a clear duty on the Secretary of State to file a declaration or statement of the vote on measures submitted to the people. The court held that those two provisions did not empower the Secretary to refuse to do so with respect to any particular measure on the ground that the measure was invalid. The court held that, regardless of whether the initiative was unconstitutional that it would be an intolerable interference with the people's reserved legislative power to prevent the official recordation of their vote on the initiative.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition without prejudice.

 

304 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Higgins v. Santa Monica


L. A. No. 27989


Supreme Court of California


62 Cal. 2d 24; 396 P.2d 41; 41 Cal. Rptr. 9; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 151; 21 Oil & Gas Rep. 522



November 5, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Leon T. David, Judge.

Action to determine validity of a municipal initiative ordinance prohibiting drilling or prospecting for oil, gas and other hydrocarbon substances on tidelands and submerged lands granted by the state to the city.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment declaring ordinance constitutional affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individual challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment for appellee city in the individual's action to determine the validity of a municipal initiative ordinance prohibiting drilling or prospecting for oil, gas, and other hydrocarbon substances on tidelands and submerged lands granted by the state to the city.


OVERVIEW: The court reasoned that no valid objection to the constitutionality of a statute under the due process clause could properly arise if it was reasonably related to promoting the public health, safety, comfort, and welfare, and if the means adopted to accomplish that promotion were reasonably appropriate to the purpose. The court concluded that such a statute was presumed to have been constitutional; and it must have been deemed to have been enacted on the basis of any state of facts supporting it that reasonably could have been conceived. Accordingly, the court reasoned that the burden was on the party challenging the statute to show that the legislative choice was palpably excluded from any consideration whatever by the legislative body; that it was palpably arbitrary and beyond rational doubt erroneous; that, in short, there was no rational basis whatever for the statute. The court affirmed the judgment upholding the constitutionality, holding that the individual did not make any such showing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, against the individual, declaring the city's ordinance prohibiting drilling or prospecting for oil, gas, and other hydrocarbon substances on tidelands and submerged lands constitutional.

People v. Sharer


Crim. No. 8025


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 869; 395 P.2d 899; 40 Cal. Rptr. 851; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 267



October 29, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tehama County and from orders denying a new trial and granting probation. Curtiss E. Wetter, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with intent to commit murder.



DISPOSITION: Order granting probation reversed with directions; appeals from judgment and from other order dismissed. Order granting probation following conviction of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily harm reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of Tehama County (California) for assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily harm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily harm. Imposition of defendant's judgment and sentence was suspended and defendant was granted probation on condition that he pay a fine and serve a 90-day jail term. Defendant appealed from the judgment of conviction and from the nonappealable order denying his motion for a new trial. The court treated his appeal as one from the order granting probation and reversed the grant of probation with directions. The court held that the prosecutor was advised prior to the convening of the grand jury that defendant would claim constitutional privilege regarding any questions presented, but that the prosecutor asked questions knowingly placing defendant in a dilemma not contemplated by the law. The court held that the evidence was not limited to impeachment, but constituted an implication of guilt, and so was inadmissible under all of the cases cited by the state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of defendant's probation.

Corona Unified Hospital Dist. v. Superior Court of Riverside County


L. A. No. 27924


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 846; 395 P.2d 817; 40 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 264



October 22, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Riverside County from taking any further steps in a taxpayer's action challenging the validity of a statute authorizing use of the "lease-back" method of financing public hospital construction in certain limited situations. John Neblett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district and leasing company requested a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Riverside Superior Court (California) from taking any further steps in real party in interest taxpayer's action challenging the validity of a statute authorizing use of the "lease-back" method of financing public hospital construction in certain limited situations.


OVERVIEW: The district voted a hospital into existence; however, the bond issue to finance the building of the hospital fell short of the two-thirds approval necessary for adoption. The district then attempted to enter into a "lease-back" arrangement whereby a nonprofit corporation would build the hospital, the district would lease the facility, the project would be financed by bonds and possibly taxes, and the title to the facility would eventually vest in the district. The taxpayer filed an action against the district and the leasing company to have the lease declared void and to have the district enjoined from levying any tax to carry out the lease agreement. The district and the leasing company requested a writ of prohibition to restrain the superior court from taking any further steps in the taxpayer's action, even though the district and the leasing company prevailed at the trial level in that action. In granting the writ, the court concluded that to leave the matter for possible correction on an appeal would be too late, would render that remedy inadequate, and would result in a failure of justice.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ, finding that relief by way of prohibition was necessary to prevent the frustration of a remedial statutory purpose and the defeat of a valid project for public health and safety.
