People v. Wolff


Crim. No. 7434


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 795; 394 P.2d 959; 40 Cal. Rptr. 271; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 258



August 31, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed with directions. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder imposing life imprisonment modified and affirmed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in which the jury found that defendant was legally sane at the time he murdered his mother and convicted him of first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the murder of his mother and pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity. The jury found defendant legally sane and defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. Defendant appealed. One issue on appeal was whether the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict of sanity. The court reviewed the record to determine whether there was substantial evidence to support the jury's verdict of sanity. The court held that the conduct and declarations of defendant, which occurred within a reasonable time before and after the commission of the act, were admissible as proof of defendant's mental condition at the time of the offense. The court determined there was substantial evidence to support the verdict of sanity at the time of the murder because defendant walked steadily and calmly, spoke clearly and coherently, and appeared to be fully conscious of what he was doing at all times.


OUTCOME: The court held there was sufficient evidence to support the verdict of sanity and that the lower court gave proper insanity instructions, but modified the judgment by reducing the degree of the crime to murder in the second degree.

 

308 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


A-1 Door & Materials Co. v. Fresno Guarantee Sav. & Loan Ass'n


S. F. No. 21372


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 728; 394 P.2d 829; 40 Cal. Rptr. 85; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 251



August 25, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County. Edward L. Kellas, Judge.

Action against maker of a construction loan to recover amount claimed from loan fund by a bonded stop notice; and cross-action by defendant for a declaration of rights of all unpaid materialmen.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions. Judgment declaring that stop-notice claimants had prior equitable liens on the loan fund and that mechanic's lien claimants and lender had subordinate equitable liens and holding defendant personally liable for interest before judgment, reversed as to mechanic's lien claimants, reversed with directions as to personal liability of defendant lender for interest before judgment, and in all other respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimants brought an action against defendant lender to recover the amount claimed from a construction loan fund by a bonded stop notice. The lender filed a cross-action for a declaration of rights of all unpaid materialmen. The Superior Court of Fresno County (California) held that the claimants were entitled to recover from the lender, with equal priority and with priority over all other claims. The lender appealed.


OVERVIEW: The lender contended that the stop-notice claims should not have been allowed because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1190.1(h) provided for an equitable garnishment of construction-loan funds. On appeal, the court held that the evidence did not support the finding that the claimants were entitled to equitable liens on the loan funds. There was no evidence of any reliance on the loan funds or of any inducement to so rely. The funds earmarked for construction purposes were to be used to pay suppliers of labor and materials who filed claims under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1190.1(h) and superseded any private contract arrangements of the borrower and the lender. The claimants' right to payment arose not simply upon their giving notice of their claims, but upon compliance with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1197.1. The lender's withholding of the funds until the claimants established their claims was not in breach of its obligations but was in compliance with the statutory requirements. The lender's liability for the stop-notice claims was limited to the amount of the fund against which the claims were asserted and the lender was not personally liable for interest before judgment.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgment that the claimants had equitable liens with priority over all other claims was reversed. The judgment was affirmed in all other respects.

 

309 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Woody


Crim. No. 7788


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 716; 394 P.2d 813; 40 Cal. Rptr. 69; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 250



August 24, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Carl B. Hilliard, Judge.

Prosecution for illegal possession of peyote.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgments of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which convicted them of illegal possession of peyote in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of violating § 11500 despite the fact that they used peyote in a Navajo religious ceremony. The court reversed defendants' convictions and held that defendants' First Amendment rights had been infringed upon. Specifically, § 11500 imposed a serious burden on the free exercise of defendants' religion, given that peyote played a central role in the ceremony and practice of the Native American Church. Moreover, the State demonstrated no compelling interest that necessitated an abridgement of defendants' First Amendment rights. Contrary to the State's assertions, peyote was not used in place of medical care or given to small children. The State presented no evidence that fraudulent claims of religious immunity would preclude effective enforcement of narcotics laws. Finally, defendants' belief in peyotism was honest and in good faith. Based on the foregoing, defendants' convictions were reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions for illegal possession of peyote.

Crittenden v. Superior Court of Mendocino County


S. F. No. 21662


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 565; 393 P.2d 692; 39 Cal. Rptr. 380; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 235



July 16, 1964 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of the Real Party in Interest for a Rehearing was Denied August 12, 1964.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Mendocino County from proceeding with an action for an injunction against the highway patrol based on alleged misapplication of a Vehicle Code section. Robert L. Winslow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner highway patrol sought a writ of prohibition restraining respondent, the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), from proceeding with an action for an injunction brought by respondent business owner. In the underlying action, the business owner sought to prevent the highway patrol from issuing parking citations under Cal. Veh. Code § 22502 to customer's vehicles parked in front of his place of business.


OVERVIEW: The business owner obtained a preliminary injunction restraining the highway patrol from issuing parking citations under § 22502 against patrons of his business who had parked their northbound vehicles on the left side of the highway, in front of his place of business. The court held that although the businessman had standing to challenge the application of § 22502, the highway patrol had properly applied the section. The court explained that the owner of a business could enjoin the enforcement of an unconstitutional or void statute or ordinance that deprived him of effective access to his customers. However, the highway patrol's interpretation of § 22502 was reasonable under the circumstances. Under the businessman's interpretation, which would have allowed parallel parking on the left if parking on the right was unavailable, patrolmen and highway users would be left without clear standards for determining when right-hand parallel parking was precluded, and dangerous highway hazards could have resulted. The court recognized the weight of the expert judgment of the highway patrol in applying § 22505.


OUTCOME: The court granted the highway patrol's peremptory writ of prohibition restraining the lower court from proceeding with an action to enjoin the highway patrol from enforcing a section of the vehicle code.

In re Henderson


Crim. No. 7631


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 541; 393 P.2d 685; 39 Cal. Rptr. 373; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 230



July 9, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure reinstatement of an appeal from a misdemeanor conviction, a free transcript, and the assignment of an attorney.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant commenced a proceeding in habeas corpus to secure the reinstatement of his appeal from an order of the San Francisco Municipal Court (California), which convicted him of soliciting or engaging in lewd or dissolute conduct in public in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(a), and to obtain a free transcript and the assignment of an attorney.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of soliciting or engaging in lewd or dissolute conduct in public in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(a). His request for a reporter's transcript, which was filed with his notice of appeal, was denied by the trial court and affirmed by the appellate department. He subsequently commenced a proceeding in habeas corpus to secure the reinstatement of his appeal, and to obtain a free transcript and the assignment of an attorney. The court granted the writ. The court held that although the orderly administration of justice required defendant to raise the matter of indigence in conjunction with his request for a free transcript, to deny him relief upon the ground that he did not file an affidavit in forma pauperis when he requested the transcript would result in undue hardship, as he did not have the guidance of any statute, rule of court, or decision setting forth the such requirement. The court further held that defendant was entitled to have an attorney assigned to represent him in the further proceedings, as a conviction under § 647(a) had serious consequences.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's writ, and directed the appellate department of the superior court to recall its remittitur, to reinstate defendant's appeal, to provide him with an attorney on appeal, and to order the trial court to grant his request for a reporter's transcript without charge.

Canon v. Justice Court for Lake Valley Judicial Dist.


Sac. No. 7547


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 446; 393 P.2d 428; 39 Cal. Rptr. 228; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 215



June 25, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of El Dorado County. Robert E. Roberts, Judge.

Proceeding in prohibition to restrain the Justice Court for the Lake Valley Judicial District of El Dorado County from trying plaintiff for violation of an allegedly unconstitutional statute.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of a judgment of respondent Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), which denied the candidate's writ of prohibition.


OVERVIEW: The candidate was charged with violating Cal. Elec. Code § 12047. The justice court overruled the candidate's demurrer and denied his motion to dismiss. The candidate sought a writ of prohibition in superior court, attacking the jurisdiction of the justice court to try him under the statute. The superior court denied his petition. On review, the court reversed and remanded, and directed the superior court to issue the writ. The court ruled that the writ of prohibition must be granted because the statute was unconstitutionally discriminatory. The court ruled that the statute, which applied only to voters by virtue of its identification requirements, resulted in an arbitrary classification. The result was that the statute did not apply to non-California voters, and the identification requirement of the statute could not be accomplished if a California voter was willing to accept responsibility for an anonymous writing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and ruled in favor of the candidate. The court remanded the case and directed the superior court to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition.

Kinnear v. San Francisco

Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 341; 392 P.2d 391; 38 Cal. Rptr. 631; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 207

PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. John B. Molinari, Judge.

Action to compel reinstatement of a civil service employee discharged under an allegedly unconstitutional provision of a city and county charter requiring automatic forfeiture of the office of a city and county employee who becomes a candidate for any public office, and for damages.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment requiring reinstatement of plaintiff, awarding him damages and declaring charter provision unconstitutional, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which declared San Francisco Cal. Charter (Charter) § 5 unconstitutional and found in favor of plaintiff employee in his action seeking reinstatement to his civil service position.


OVERVIEW: The employee, who worked as a deputy for the municipality's sheriff's department, was dismissed pursuant to Charter § 5 when he declared his candidacy for sheriff. On review, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment ordering the employee to be reinstated and holding § 5 unconstitutional. In reaching its conclusion, the court relied on analogous caselaw in holding that § 5 was void for overbreadth. The court held that there was no language in the section that could be severed to limit the section's operation. Additionally, the court held that the trial court correctly awarded damages to the employee.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which found in favor of the employee in his action seeking reinstatement to his civil service position.

Fort v. Civil Service Com.


S. F. No. 21609


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 331; 392 P.2d 385; 38 Cal. Rptr. 625; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 206



May 28, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Folger Emerson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a county civil service commission to reinstate petitioner to his civil service position from which he was dismissed for violation of an allegedly unconstitutional provision in the county charter.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment granting writ and declaring provision unconstitutional, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which concluded that Alameda County, Cal. Charter § 41 was unconstitutional and directed that plaintiff civil servant be reinstated to his position. The civil servant was dismissed for violating § 41, when he became chairman of a speakers' bureau for a committee to reelect the governor.


OVERVIEW: The court found that § 41 applied alike to partisan and nonpartisan activities, not only to county elections but to all elections, was not narrowly drawn but was framed in sweeping and uncertain terms, and excepted only the right to vote and to express opinions "privately." The court concluded that in the light of the principles applicable to freedom of speech and the related First Amendment rights, no sound basis had been shown for upholding the county provision restricting the civil servant's political activity. The court found that the overbreadth and uncertainty of the part of the § 41 invoked against the civil servant could not be eliminated by the severance of language, and the only way in which a limitation of § 41 to a proper scope could be attempted was by reading into it numerous qualifications and exceptions. The court concluded that the second sentence of § 41 was void in its entirety and that the trial court correctly ordered that the civil servant be reinstated to his position.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

315 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Binns v. Hite


L. A. No. 27737


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 107; 389 P.2d 947; 37 Cal. Rptr. 323; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 183



March 11, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Walter R. Evans, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action to determine whether names of incumbent municipal court judges should appear on primary or general election ballots, if neither nomination papers for such office nor petitions for a write-in campaign have been filed, or such names should be omitted from the ballot under terms of Elec. Code, § 25304.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment declaring that § 25304 is unconstitutional and should not be applied by defendant registrar of voters, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant registrar of voters appealed a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that Cal. Elec. Code § 25304 was unconstitutional and should not be applied by the registrar in plaintiff municipal court judges' action to determine whether names of incumbent municipal court judges should appear on election ballots, if neither nomination papers for the office nor petitions for a write-in campaign were filed.


OVERVIEW: The municipal court judges asserted that § 25304 was contrary to the provision in Cal. Const. art. VI, § 11 that municipal court judges should be elected by the qualified electors of the district. The court stated that § 11 also declared that, except as such matters were otherwise provided in Cal. Const. art. VI, the legislature should provide the manner in which municipal court judges should be elected or appointed, and Cal. Elec. Code § 25304 related to the manner of the election. The fact that the procedure devised by the legislature required electors who were interested to take the initiative if the electors wished the incumbent's name to be on the ballot did not impose an unreasonable burden upon them. In view of the comparatively small number of electors' signatures required to place the incumbent's name on the ballot, it was apparent that if there was sufficient opposition to the incumbent to have any significant effect at all, there would be no difficulty in obtaining the necessary signatures. The procedures set forth in § 25304 were reasonably designed to accomplish the legitimate purposes of the statute. Thus, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which declared that the election statute was unconstitutional and should not be applied by the registrar of voters in the municipal court judges' declaratory relief action to determine whether names of incumbent municipal court judges should appear on election ballots, if neither nomination papers for the office nor petitions for a write-in campaign had been filed.

 

316 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Redevelopment Plan for Bunker Hill Urban Renewal Project 1B


L. A. No. 26751


Supreme Court of California


61 Cal. 2d 21; 389 P.2d 538; 37 Cal. Rptr. 74; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 176



February 27, 1964 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied March 25, 1964.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Philip H. Richards, Judge.

Proceeding by a city redevelopment agency to determine validity of a redevelopment plan for an urban renewal project, its authority to issue bonds to finance such project in part, and validity of bonds to be issued by the agency; and for an injunction permanently enjoining the institution by any person or organization of any action or proceeding raising any issue adjudicated or which could be adjudicated in such proceeding; and five consolidated actions by property owners within project area and taxpayers of city for declaratory relief, injunction, writ of mandate or certiorari, or for judicial review under former Health and Saf. Code, § 33746 (now § 33500).



DISPOSITION: One judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions; other judgments affirmed. Judgment in special proceeding reversed with directions as to awarding of costs against defendants and otherwise affirmed; other judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners appealed judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of defendants, city redevelopment agency, city and its council and treasurer. Plaintiffs sought declaratory and injunctive relief and judicial review under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33955 to block a redevelopment plan. Defendant development agency was plaintiff in a consolidated special proceeding.


OVERVIEW: This special proceeding by defendant city redevelopment agency was consolidated with five actions by plaintiff property owners within the project area for declaratory and injunctive relief and judicial review under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33500. The special proceeding sought to determine the validity of the plan for an urban renewal project; the agency's authority to issue bonds to finance the project; and for an injunction permanently enjoining any action raising any issue adjudicated or which could be adjudicated in the proceeding. The complaints in the five actions attacked the proceedings of defendant agency, the city, and the council leading up to the adoption of the ordinance approving the final redevelopment plan. The court concluded that the trial court correctly determined that the redevelopment plan should be upheld as not an abuse of discretion, and that the judgments to that effect should be affirmed, with the exception of the award of costs against plaintiffs. Because the actions were similar to eminent domain, costs were awarded to plaintiff property owners, although their appeal was denied.


OUTCOME: The court held the judgment in the special proceeding brought by defendant redevelopment agency as plaintiff was affirmed except for all proper trial costs and costs on appeal, which were awarded to plaintiff property owners. The other judgments appealed from were affirmed.

 

317 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


McClenny v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 27584


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 677; 388 P.2d 691; 36 Cal. Rptr. 459; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 278



January 28, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, and John F. McCarthy, Judge thereof, from hearing pending contempt proceedings.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The wife of petitioner husband obtained an order to show cause re contempt after the husband did not return their daughter after a visit. The husband filed a notice of motion and affidavit of prejudice that sought to disqualify the judge from hearing the contempt matter. The Superior Court Los Angeles County (California) denied the motion. The husband sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the judge from hearing the contempt proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied the husband's motion and affidavit of prejudice under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 that sought to disqualify the judge from hearing the pending contempt matter. The trial court denied the motion to disqualify on the ground that the contempt proceeding was a continuation of the original action. The court held that although the issues presented in the contempt proceeding were not identical in every particular to the issues previously submitted to the judge, the issue of whether the husband in fact violated the judge's orders was a matter necessarily relevant and material to the issues involved in the original action. The court held that the trial judge was in the best position to pass upon the questions involved. The court held that in domestic relations cases in particular, the contempt function was one of enforcement of the court's orders and the court could not permit a device intended for spare and protective use to be converted into a weapon of offense. The court concluded that the contempt proceeding was a continuation of the original domestic relations action, and that the trial court properly denied the husband's motion under § 170.6.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the contempt proceeding was a continuation of the original domestic relations action, and that the trial court properly denied the husband's motion. Therefore, the alternative writ was discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ was denied.

 

318 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Trummer


Crim. No. 7413


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 658; 388 P.2d 177; 36 Cal. Rptr. 281; 1964 Cal. LEXIS 275



January 14, 1964 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from parole after commitment to a state narcotic rehabilitation facility by a superior court judge without a jury trial.



DISPOSITION: Certain conditions of petitioner's parole modified; petitioner to be granted opportunity to demand a jury trial on issue of his narcotic addiction at time of commitment if he so desires; as to release from parole, order to show cause discharged and petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an application for writ of habeas corpus in which he challenged an order by the trial court (California), which committed him and placed him on parole for being a narcotics addict pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 6451.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to forgery of a narcotics prescription in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code 11715. A hearing was then held pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 6451 and defendant was adjudged a narcotics addict and committed into custody. After ten months of treatment, defendant was paroled. Defendant filed an application for writ of habeas corpus. The court denied the petition for habeas corpus. The court determined defendant was not entitled to immediate release from his parole on the ground that he was no longer an addict, where the purpose behind parole was rehabilitation and the need of further followup was necessary if a relapse occurred. The court found that certain conditions of defendant's parole that provided for the deprivation of civil rights were improper because defendant was not sentenced as a felon and had been civilly committed. The court held a provision under Cal. Penal Code § 6451 that did not allow for a defendant to demand a jury trial was invalid, but that defendant was not entitled to discharge from his parole where his remedy was an opportunity to demand a jury trial on the issue of his narcotic addiction as of the time of his commitment.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the deletion of a clause that provided for the deprivation of defendant's civil rights and also ordered the expungement of defendant's name from a register. The court granted defendant the opportunity to demand, and upon proper showing receive, a jury trial on the issue of his narcotic addiction as of the date of his commitment. The court denied defendant's petition for habeas corpus.

 

319 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Patton v. La Bree


L.A. No. 27433


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 606; 387 P.2d 398; 35 Cal. Rptr. 622; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 267



December 13, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Leon T. David, Judge.

Action for personal injuries sustained by the owner of an automobile in an accident which occurred while the owner was riding in the car as it was being driven by an employee of an automobile dealer to whom the owner had taken the car for service.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to the complaint was sustained, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff automobile owner (owner) sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained a general demurrer by defendant automobile dealer (dealer) and dismissed the owner's personal injury action as barred by Cal. Veh. Code § 17158.


OVERVIEW: The owner filed a tort action after being injured while riding as a passenger in a car she owned; the driver was an employee of the dealer to whom she had taken the car for service. When the trial court sustained the dealer's general demurrer and dismissed the owner's complaint the owner sought review. On appeal the court affirmed the judgment. The court found that the action was barred by Cal. Veh. Code § 17158, which provided that an owner who had been riding in his own car as an occupant was unable to recover for injuries resulting from the ordinary negligence of the driver. The court rejected the owner's contention that the statute did not apply to her because she had given compensation for the trip, noting that the statute's language showed no intention to except an owner who had given compensation for a ride. Neither was the court persuaded by the owner's argument that the section was unconstitutional because it made an unreasonable distinction between owners and non-owners who gave compensation, noting that legislation was presumed constitutional unless it was palpably arbitrary and beyond rational doubt erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the owner's personal injury action against the dealer.

 

320 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Benson v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 26786


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 355; 384 P.2d 649; 33 Cal. Rptr. 257; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 244



August 27, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Arnold Praeger, Judge.

Action by a deceased fireman's widow and cross-action by his former wife for declaratory relief as to their entitlement to a widow's pension from defendant city and for accruals of payments and interest allegedly wrongfully withheld by the city.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying cross-complainant any relief and awarding plaintiff interest on accrued pension benefits, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow of a deceased fireman brought an action for declaratory relief as to her entitlement to a widow's pension from defendant city, and defendant former wife brought a cross-action regarding the same pension. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) held in favor of the widow and awarded her interest on accrued pension benefits withheld by the city pending the award of the pension. The former wife appealed.


OVERVIEW: The former wife was married to the deceased city employee during the time of his employment with the city. The couple was thereafter divorced and the pension had been declared community property; however, no division was made of it. The employee remarried after retirement and died while still married to the widow. The court agreed with the trial court that the widow was the "widow" of the deceased employee within the meaning of that term as it was used in the city charter. The court rejected the former wife's attempt to analogize the situation to one involving life insurance proceeds generated from premiums that were paid for with community property. The court also concluded that it was vital for the city to retain some flexibility and control over pension rights and that such control would have been lost if third parties such as the former wife could have obtained vested interests in such pensions. The court further held that Cal. Civ. Code § 3287 supported an award of interest to the widow.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the widow and upheld the award of interest payable to her on withheld accrued pension benefits.

 

321 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Yorty v. Anderson


Sac. No. 7455


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 312; 384 P.2d 417; 33 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 241



August 20, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied September 18, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel respondents to convene as members of the Reapportionment Commission and reapportion the state senatorial districts.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters filed a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondents, lieutenant governor and others, to convene as members of the Reapportionment Commission and reapportion the state senatorial districts.


OVERVIEW: The voters sought to compel respondents to convene as the members of the Reapportionment Commission and reapportion the state senatorial districts. The voters asserted that Cal. Const. art. IV, § 6 insofar as it related to apportionment of senatorial districts, invidiously discriminated against voters living in heavily populated counties and violated the Equal Protection Clause. The court concluded that, whether or not § 6 was invalid, respondents did not have the power to reapportion the districts. The court concluded that under § 6, the power of the commission to act was expressly conditioned upon the failure of the legislature to reapportion the assembly and senatorial districts at the first regular session following each decennial federal census. The court found that the legislature did not fail to act, but that it enacted a complete reapportionment plan. The court stated that in the event that the plan was held invalid by judicial decision, the legislature, under a reasonable construction of § 6, was entitled to an opportunity to adopt a plan that met the requirements of the U.S. Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court denied the voters' petition for a peremptory writ in their action seeking reapportionment of the state senatorial districts.

 

322 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Professional Fire Fighters, Inc. v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 27007


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 276; 384 P.2d 158; 32 Cal. Rptr. 830; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 237; 53 L.R.R.M. 2976



August 13, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 11, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Leon T. David, Judge.

Action for declaration and enforcement of rights and obligations of members of a firefighters' union and of a city in respect to statutes relating to the right of firefighters to join any bona fide labor organization and prohibiting interference with that right and for an injunction to restrain defendant city from discriminating against fire department employees who are members of plaintiff local union.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment declaring statutes in question to be unconstitutional and inapplicable to defendant city by reason of its status as a chartered city, and denying plaintiffs an injunction, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, corporation and union member, brought an action in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) against defendant City of Los Angeles for declaration of rights and obligations under statutes relating to the right of firefighters to join a bona fide labor organization and for an injunction to restrain the city from discriminating against firefighters who were union members. Plaintiffs appealed from an adverse judgment.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found the statutes to be unconstitutional and inapplicable to defendant city by reason of its status as a chartered city and denied plaintiffs an injunction. The court found: (1) the plain meaning of both the Labor Code and the Government Code provisions was that unions may organize to represent their members and that an action at law on behalf of such members was one form of such representation; (2) there was a triable issue of fact as to whether there was a reasonable probability that defendant would have continued its discriminatory activities and threat of future action could only have been inferred from that which occurred in the past; (3) a statute which affected only firefighters was not in violation of the provisions of Cal. Const. art. I, § 21 as a grant of special privilege or immunity because the classification was not arbitrary and was based upon some difference that had a substantial relation to the purpose of the legislation; and (4) the Labor Code provisions and the Government Code provisions were applicable to the City of Los Angeles because the state legislature was attempting to deal with labor relations on a statewide basis.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. On the cause of action for injunction, inasmuch as it involved controverted questions of fact, the court held that the issues should be tried, if the parties so desired. On the declaratory relief cause of action, the court instructed the trial to declare the rights and obligations of the parties in a manner consistent with the views expressed by the court.

Aronoff v. Franchise Tax Board


Sac. No. 7440


Supreme Court of California


60 Cal. 2d 177; 383 P.2d 409; 32 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 230



July 18, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied August 14, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Franchise Tax Board from taking any further steps to collect income taxes assessed against petitioners.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers instituted an action seeking a writ of prohibition restraining respondents, tax board and officials, from taking further steps to collect personal income taxes assessed against the taxpayers.


OVERVIEW: After respondents disallowed deductions claimed by the taxpayers on their California personal income tax returns and assessed additional personal income taxes, the taxpayers filed a petition for a writ of prohibition to restrain respondents from taking further steps to collect the assessed taxes. The court denied the petition and ruled (1) that the issuance of a writ of prohibition to restrain the collection of personal income taxes was barred by § 19081 of the Revenue and Tax Code and Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 15, (2) that the Due Process Clause did not guarantee the right to judicial review of tax liability before payment, (3) that the taxpayers failed to exhaust their administrative remedies because they did not pay their taxes or file claims for refund and their petition for a rehearing of the tax board's assessment was still pending in the State Board of Equalization, and (4) that the writ of prohibition was limited to a threatened restraint of judicial power in excess of the jurisdiction of a tribunal exercising judicial functions and no judicial power was conferred on the tax board or State Board of Equalization.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayers' application for a writ of prohibition to restrain respondents from taking further steps to collect income taxes assessed against the taxpayers.

Zeitlin v. Arnebergh


L. A. No. 26905


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 901; 383 P.2d 152; 31 Cal. Rptr. 800; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 223; 10 A.L.R.3d 707



July 2, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 31, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Bayard Rhone, Judge.

Action by a bookseller and a prospective reader of a certain book against a city attorney to secure a declaratory judgment that the book is not matter defined as obscene by Pen. Code, § 311, and that its sale would not violate Pen. Code, § 311.2.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to complaint was sustained without leave to amend, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, bookseller and prospective reader, brought an action against defendant city attorney to secure a declaratory judgment that a certain book was not a matter defined as obscene by Cal. Penal Code § 311, and that its sale would not violate Cal. Penal Code § 311.2. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) dismissed the bookseller's and prospective reader's suit, and the bookseller and prospective reader appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court held that: (1) declaratory relief was appropriate because the bookseller and the prospective reader properly presented the issue of the book's proscription under Cal. Penal Code § 311 and alleged a genuine controversy involving the construction of the particular obscenity legislation; (2) the issue of the application of the obscenity statute to the particular book, within constitutional limitations, was a matter of law; (3) § 311 was narrowly tailored to prohibit only hard-core pornography; and (4) the book at issue did not fall within the prohibition of § 311 because it was not hard-core pornography.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court.

Los Angeles Metropolitan Transit Authority v. Public Utilities Com.


S. F. No. 21190


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 863; 382 P.2d 583; 31 Cal. Rptr. 463; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 219



June 20, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied July 17, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review an order of the Public Utilities Commission compelling a transit authority to comply with safety rules and other regulations governing the operation of passenger stage corporations and street railroad corporations.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent public utilities commission instituted an investigation into the operations of petitioner transit authority, in order to determine the extent of compliance with General Order 98. Public utilities commission entered an order compelling the transit authority to comply with safety rules governing the operation of passenger stage corporations and street railroad corporations. The transit authority sought review of the order.


OVERVIEW: The transit authority argued that the public utilities commission's actions were contrary to § 23 and related sections of article XII of the California Constitution. Moreover, the transit authority urged that article XII allowed the public utilities commission regulatory jurisdiction over private transportation utilities only, and prohibited the exercise of such jurisdiction by the commission over public transportation corporations. Accordingly, the court held that it must determine whether the California Constitution prohibited the Legislature from granting to the public utilities commission jurisdiction over the transit authority to secure compliance with the safety regulations contained in General Order 98. The court held that the Legislature has determined that the safety of operators and passengers of the transit authority could best be assured if limited, as to oppose to none, regulatory jurisdiction over safety practices was conferred upon the public utilities commission. The court concluded that the legislature lawfully granted the public utility commission jurisdiction over the transit authority's safety practices.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the public utilities commission's decision ordering the transit authority to comply with the safety rules governing the operation of passenger stage corporations and street railroad corporations.

People v. Shipp


Crim. No. 7236


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 845; 382 P.2d 577; 31 Cal. Rptr. 457; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 217



June 18, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 17, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. LeRoy Dawson, Judge.

Prosecution for robbery and for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty on the murder count, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of one count of first degree murder and two counts of robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that he was entitled to reversal of his convictions because the trial court had erred in denying production of a report prepared by an officer who testified against him, in denying his motion under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 604 for certification to the juvenile court, and in excusing prospective jurors conscientiously opposed to the death penalty. The court affirmed defendant's convictions. While the trial court had erred in denying production of the officer's report, that error was harmless. It was not reasonably probable that the jury would have reached a different verdict even in the absence of the officer's testimony. Defendant, as well as an accomplice, had both admitted that they attacked and robbed the victim. Also, the nature of the charges against defendant justified the trial court's conclusion that he was not a fit subject for consideration in the juvenile court. Furthermore, when the trial court's examination of certain prospective jurors revealed those jurors' conscientious objections to the death penalty, it was the trial court's duty to excuse those jurors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for one count of first degree murder and two counts of robbery.

 

327 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hamer v. Ross


S. F. No. 20667


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 776; 382 P.2d 375; 31 Cal. Rptr. 335; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 209



June 13, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County. Richard M. Sims, Jr., Judge.

Action to determine validity of a zoning limitation in a city ordinance and proceeding in mandamus to compel issuance of a building permit to allow a zoning variance.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for plaintiff reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which declared that certain provisions of the town's zoning ordinances were void as applied to appellee owner's property, and ordered the town to issue building permits for the erection of the garden apartments proposed by the property owner.


OVERVIEW: The property owner owned a 2.2-acre parcel of land, which she wanted to develop by building garden apartments. The property was situated in a residential area and subject to zoning restrictions, which fixed the minimum lot-size requirement at one acre, and limited the use to a single dwelling. However, most of the surrounding lots were much smaller. The town had repeatedly rejected the property owner's petitions to build the apartments. The property owner argued that because the one-acre restriction was not enforceable as to her property, the single dwelling restriction could not be either. The court held that while the ordinance that imposed a one-acre lot restriction could not properly apply to property which was virtually surrounded by parcels of lesser size, it could restrict the use of the property to a single-family dwelling because that usage was the predominant characteristic of the property in the general area. The ordinances were severable and enforceable. Because the reasonableness of the single-family restriction was fairly debatable, based on the town's interest in maintaining the character of the town, the court did not disturb the decision of the town.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for the property owner.

County of Madera v. Gendron


S. F. No. 20959


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 798; 382 P.2d 342; 31 Cal. Rptr. 302; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 211; 6 A.L.R.3d 555



June 13, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Madera County. Thomas Coakley, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action to determine constitutionality of Government Code provisions prohibiting a district attorney in a county of the 35th class from engaging in the private practice of law and to determine legality of paying the salary of a district attorney who had allegedly violated such provisions.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions. Judgment affirmed insofar as it declared the provisions constitutional and reversed with directions insofar as it determined that plaintiff county could not legally pay defendant district attorney's salary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant district attorney appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Madera County (California), which entered judgment for plaintiff county in the county's action for declaratory relief that it was not required to pay the district attorney his salary because he engaged in the private practice of law in violation of Cal. Gov't Code § 28135. The lower court also held that § 28135 was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The county sought a determination of whether it could pay the district attorney his salary in view of the fact that he allegedly violated § 28135. The district attorney sought a declaration of the unconstitutionality of § 28135. The lower court upheld the constitutionality of the statute and denied the district attorney his salary. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court affirmed the judgment that § 28135 was constitutional. The system of classification for the counties and their populations was not illusory because Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5 provided that the legislature could classify the counties by population and regulate the compensation of district attorneys. In addition, the legislature could validly restrict the private practice of law by the district attorney in the county at issue despite not imposing restrictions in other counties because the restriction went to the compensation, rather than the duties, of the office of the district attorney. The court reversed the judgment that the district attorney was not entitled to receive his salary due to his violation because the legislature did not provide for such sanction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the statute was constitutional. The court reversed the judgment that the county was not required to pay the district attorney his salary.

Citizens Utilities Co. v. Superior Court of Santa Cruz County


S. F. No. 21175


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 805; 382 P.2d 356; 31 Cal. Rptr. 316; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 212



June 13, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied July 10, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County from proceeding to trial in an eminent domain action. Charles S. Franich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A local water district sought the condemnation of petitioner utility company's property pursuant to the general eminent domain statutes, Cal. Civ. Code pt. 3. tit. 7. The Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) denied the utility company's motion to dismiss the eminent domain proceeding as unconstitutional. The utility company then sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the respondent trial court from proceeding with the condemnation.


OVERVIEW: The utility company contended that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1249 was unconstitutional as applied to public utilities and that in being so there was no method provided by the law for allowing compensation for compulsory improvements made after the date of the summons and that the trial court had no constitutional power to devise a method for such allowance. Therefore, the utility company argued that the trial court was without jurisdiction to proceed on the eminent domain action. The court found that 1) the application of § 1249 to public utility company condemnations was unconstitutional; 2) the trial court properly devised a procedure whereby the value of the utility company's property was to be valued as of the date of trial, rather than the date of summons; 3) the utility company's right to prohibition was not perfected because the company's challenge to the trial court's jurisdiction was inadequate; and 4) the utility company's interests were properly protected in the event that the water district did not have sufficient funds to adequately compensate the utility company.


OUTCOME: The court denied the utility company's writ of prohibition against the trial court.

 

330 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Koehne


Crim. No. 7286


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 646; 381 P.2d 633; 30 Cal. Rptr. 809; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 195



May 21, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody after commitment for violation of a municipal ordinance pertaining to intoxication in a public place. Harold C. Shepherd, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for a writ of habeas corpus to secure release from custody after commitment for violating Los Angeles, Cal., Los Angeles Municipal Code § 41.27-(a), which pertained to intoxication in a public place.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found lying in an intoxicated condition on a private driveway exposed to public view and was convicted of violating a city ordinance. The court granted defendant a writ of habeas corpus. The court held that it was improper to prosecute defendant under the city ordinance. The court found that the city ordinance was void because the legislature adopted a general scheme for the regulation of the criminal aspects of being intoxicated in a public place under Cal. Penal Code § 647(f), which provided that a person was guilty of a misdemeanor if found in any public place under the influence of intoxicating liquor. The court determined that it was immaterial to decide here whether a driveway was a "public place" within the meaning of § 647(f) because it was a place exposed to public view. The court also found, based on other provisions of the penal code, that the legislature by implication provided that intoxication in a place that was not a public place but was exposed to public view should not be criminal. Accordingly, the city ordinance attempting to make certain acts pertaining to intoxication in a public place criminal was in conflict with the state law and was void.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of habeas corpus for defendant.

In re Raner


Crim. No. 7255


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 635; 381 P.2d 638; 30 Cal. Rptr. 814; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 193



May 21, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody after commitment to the state narcotics rehabilitation center. William A. Munnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner brought a proceeding in habeas corpus to secure release from custody after being committed to a state narcotics rehabilitation center.


OVERVIEW: An unidentified person reported to the district attorney's office that he believed that petitioner was addicted to narcotics or was in imminent danger of becoming addicted. The State filed a petition for commitment under Cal. Penal Code § 6500, and petitioner was booked into the county jail. Three days later, he was taken to a detention facility where he remained until his hearing several days later. Petitioner was committed to the state narcotics rehabilitation center, and he brought a proceeding in habeas corpus to secure release on due process grounds. The court granted the writ and ordered petitioner dismissed, holding that the procedural safeguards were disregarded and petitioner was committed illegally and without due process. The days between petitioner's apprehension and his commitment hearing were illegal and without statutory authority, petitioner was not examined by a physician within three days before the petition for commitment was filed, and the petition for commitment was not accompanied by a physician's affidavit. Additionally, there was no evidence that notice of the time and place of the hearing was ever served on petitioner, as required by Cal. Penal Code § 6504.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's writ of habeas corpus.

 

332 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jackson


Crim. No. 7267


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 468; 381 P.2d 1; 30 Cal. Rptr. 329; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 177



April 30, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco and from an order denying a new trial. Harry J. Neubarth, Judge.

Prosecution for illegally offering to sell narcotics.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied his motion for a new trial after the jury found him guilty of illegally offering to sell narcotics, a violation of Cal. Health and Safety Code § 11501.


OVERVIEW: The record showed that defendant offered to sell heroin to an undercover narcotics agent, took the agent's money, but did not provide the drugs. On appeal of a conviction, defendant argued that, 1) the trial court erred in giving a jury instruction on specific intent; and 2) Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501 did not prohibit an offer to sell a narcotic when the drug was not delivered. The court held that delivery was not an essential element of the crime alleged; however, the trial instruction was in error because the intent to make a sale was necessary for a conviction under § 11501. Here, the jury could have reasonably concluded from the evidence that defendant had not intention of selling the drugs to the agent; thus, he was not subject to the same penalties imposed by § 11501.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction.

 

333 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mickelson


Crim. No. 7295


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 448; 380 P.2d 658; 30 Cal. Rptr. 18; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 174



April 18, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 14, 1963. McComb, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County setting aside an information. Walter R. Evans, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted defendant's motion to set aside the information charging him with two counts of committing burglaries of telephone booths, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459. Conceding that there was no evidence at the preliminary hearing to support one count, the State only sought a reversal as to the second count.


OVERVIEW: A police officer arrested defendant after locating coins in a sock while searching the car in which defendant was riding pursuant to the investigation of an area robbery that had occurred 20 minutes before the search. The car had been proceeding erratically, and the police officer thought the driver's physical characteristics and attire matched the description of the robber. The court found, contrary to the State's contention of reasonable cause for arrest and justifiable search, that the officer did not have reasonable cause to arrest the driver because there could have been more than one man in the area matching the description, the robbery occurred 20 minutes prior to the stop, the driver was driving toward the robbery scene as opposed to away from it, and the officer had no information that the robber had an automobile or a confederate. Although the officer had cause to stop the car for investigation pursuant to its erratic course, the court affirmed dismissal of the information, ruling that once a reasonable explanation was given (that the occupants were unfamiliar with the area and were lost), the officer lacked further cause to arrest the driver or search the automobile.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing the information against defendant as to both counts.

People v. Ashley


Crim. No. 7151


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 339; 379 P.2d 496; 29 Cal. Rptr. 16; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 163



March 14, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 9, 1963.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from judgments of the Superior Court of Merced County. Gregory P. Maushart, Judge.

Prosecution for kidnaping and murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Motion to take additional evidence denied. Judgments convicting defendant of kidnaping and first degree murder, determining defendant to be sane on the date the offenses were committed, and imposing the death penalty on the murder count, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an automatic appeal pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b), defendant challenged judgments from the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which determined him to be sane on the date of his commission of the charged offenses and sentenced him to death in connection with his conviction for kidnapping and first-degree murder. In addition, he filed a motion to take additional evidence.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that the evidence was sufficient to support the guilty verdict on the murder and kidnapping charges and the jury's finding of sanity, noting that the fact defendant testified he thought his acts were justified at most created a conflict in the evidence, and did not prove insanity. The court rejected as meritless defendant's speedy trial claim as well as his contention that he properly moved to disqualify the judge, finding that none of the stated reasons for disqualification was sufficient to establish the requisite bias and prejudice. Furthermore, the failure of the trial judge to appoint counsel for the self-representing defendant was not in error, as defendant had no absolute right to the services of an attorney in a purely advisory capacity. The court accordingly affirmed, holding that the trial judge had had the legal right to seek expert assistance to inform him as to the mental condition of defendant before he was required to express whether he had the "doubt" as to that condition, as that term was used in Cal. Penal Code § 1368.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to take additional evidence, and affirmed the judgments convicting defendant of kidnapping and first-degree murder, determining defendant to be sane on the date the offenses were committed, and imposing the death penalty on the murder count.

 

335 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Wollam on behalf of Culinary Workers & Bartenders Union v. Palm Springs


L. A. No. 26854


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 276; 379 P.2d 481; 29 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 160; 52 L.R.R.M. 2688



March 12, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County. Merrill Brown, Judge.

Action to determine constitutionality of a municipal ordinance prohibiting the stationary use of sound trucks.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment declaring ordinance unconstitutional, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which found that an ordinance preventing the use of stationary sound trucks was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizen brought an action on behalf of his union, attacking the constitutionality of Palm Springs, Cal., Ord. 395, art. 443, § 4430.30, which governed the use of sound trucks. The citizen claimed that § 4430.30's prohibition against the use of stationary sound trucks impinged the First Amendment right to free speech by preventing the publication of grievances against certain employers. The court held that because the use of the sound truck provided an effective means of communication, it fell within the constitutional protection of free speech. However, its use was also subject to specific regulation of its ill effects on the safety and tranquility of the public. The court found that § 4430.30's blanket prohibition against the use of a stationary sound truck exceeded its justification. The prohibition was not limited to the elimination of loud or raucous noise nor was it necessary for the prevention of traffic hazards. A complete message could not be conveyed by a moving truck. The ordinance's absolute obstruction of the use of the sound truck to convey a complete message was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court that a local ordinance prohibiting the use of a stationary sound truck was unconstitutional was affirmed.

American Civil Liberties Union v. Board of Education


L. A. No. 26949


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 224; 379 P.2d 16; 28 Cal. Rptr. 712; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 153



February 26, 1963 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 27, 1963. McComb, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Board of Education of the San Diego Unified School District to grant an application for the use of a high school auditorium for a series of public meetings.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner American Civil Liberties Union (ACLU) brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent Board of Education (San Diego, California) to grant an application for use of a high school auditorium for a series of public meetings on the constitution and civil liberties, after the board refused to grant the application because the ACLU refused to submit a questionnaire required by the board under its "revised procedure."


OVERVIEW: An ACLU chapter filed an application for use of a school auditorium for a series of meetings. The school board refused to act on the application because the ACLU failed to return a requested questionnaire, despite the fact that the court had already declared Cal. Educ. Code § 16565, on which the board had based its requirements, including the questionnaire, unconstitutional. The court found that the board's "revised procedure" simply reenacted the language of § 16565, choosing to ignore the fact that the board was limited by the appropriate provisions of the state and federal constitutions and prior case law. The court noted that the board made no attempt at all to avoid the constitutional defects held to exist in the state legislation or to recognize the limitations placed on its regulations by the court. It therefore granted the application for mandamus and ordered that a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the board to act on the application without answer to the questionnaire.


OUTCOME: The writ was granted. The court held that the board's regulations were unconstitutional, noting that it had already addressed this once. A peremptory writ of mandate issued, directing the board to act on the applications for use of the high school auditorium without requiring an answer to the questionnaire.

American Civil Liberties Union v. Board of Education


L. A. No. 26948


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 203; 379 P.2d 4; 28 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 152



February 26, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles to grant an application for the use of a high school auditorium for a series of public meetings.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner civil liberties organization filed a mandamus action against respondent school board to compel the board to grant an application for the use of a high school auditorium for a series of public meetings on the Bill of Rights.


OVERVIEW: The board denied the organization's application to use the school's auditorium on ground that the organization refused to file a statement of information as prescribed by Los Angeles, Cal., Bd. of Educ. R. 1316, which required groups using a public school's premises to assert that the property would not be used for the commission of any crime. The organization refused to sign the statement and filed suit, questioning whether the board had the legal right to make compliance with the rule a prerequisite to an otherwise lawful use of the school premises. The court denied the writ, holding that Rule 1316 was not invalid. The rule was not the type of prior restraint ordinarily referred to as an unconstitutional limitation upon freedom of speech or assembly. That the board had both the right and duty to predetermine that school premises would not be used in contravention of the law was found in the board's general power to hold, maintain, and use the property for educational purposes and in the specific provisions of the Civic Center Act, Cal. Educ. Code §§ 16551-16566. Los Angeles, Cal., Bd. of Educ. R. 1316 did not subvert the presumption of innocence or invert the burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ of mandate previously issued and denied a peremptory writ because the board's rule did not establish an unconstitutional abridgment of the rights of free speech and assembly.

Borchers Bros. v. Buckeye Incubator Co.

Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 234; 379 P.2d 1; 28 Cal. Rptr. 697; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 155



February 26, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County dismissing plaintiff's action after demurrer to complaint was sustained with leave to amend and plaintiff failed to amend. Byrl R. Salsman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff materialman sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which sustained the demurrer of defendant property owner to the materialman's foreclosure complaint with leave to amend and dismissed the materialman's action after the materialman failed to amend the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed the materialman's complaint because the complaint failed to allege that the materialman had given the prelien notice required by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1193. The materialman contended that the notice requirement of § 1193 was unconstitutional because it unfairly discriminated between laborers and materialmen in violation of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 15. The materialman further contended that § 15 was self-executing. The court affirmed the trial court's order. The court observed that § 15 was not self-executing but had to be implemented by legislation to provide enforcement procedures. The court concluded that the legislative classification was neither arbitrary nor unreasonable and, therefore, that the notice requirement of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1193 was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which dismissed the materialman's foreclosure complaint against the property owner.

Metropolitan Water Dist. v. Marquardt


L. A. No. 26662


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 159; 379 P.2d 28; 28 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 151



February 21, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the executive secretary of a metropolitan water district to take certain steps necessary to carry out its contract with the state for the delivery of water.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Metropolitan Water District of Southern California (District), requested a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent, the District's executive secretary (secretary), to take certain procedural steps necessary to carry out a contract for the delivery of water from the facilities of the State Water Resources Development System to the District.


OVERVIEW: The District sought a proceeding in mandamus to compel the secretary to take certain procedural steps necessary to carry out a contract for the delivery of water from the facilities of the State Water Resources Development System to the District, pursuant to the California Water Resources Development Bond Act, Cal. Water Code § 12930 et seq., and sections of the Water Code relating to the Central Valley Project, Cal. Water Code § 11100 et seq. The secretary contended that the contract was invalid. The court issued the mandamus and held that the contract was valid in all areas. The court held that the State of California, here the real party in interest, was capable of contracting with one of its municipalities. There was mutuality in the contract, and the court found that the bonds issued to fund the completion of the water project were lawful and did not make the contract invalid. The court concluded that the differentiation in water rates charged to customers differently situated was not in itself discriminatory, which would have invalidated the contract.


OUTCOME: The court issued the District's request for mandamus.

In re De La O


Crim. No. 7208


Supreme Court of California


59 Cal. 2d 128; 378 P.2d 793; 28 Cal. Rptr. 489; 1963 Cal. LEXIS 149; 98 A.L.R.2d 705



February 14, 1963 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody after commitment to a narcotics rehabilitation facility. William A. Munnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged and petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner detainee applied for a writ of habeas corpus, attacking the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 6450 and certain other chapters of the California Penal Code.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner detainee was found guilty of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721 by the unlawful use of and addiction to narcotics, and as an alternative to sentencing was committed to an institution under Cal. Penal Code § 6450 as a narcotic addict. Petitioner applied for a writ of habeas corpus and the court denied his request. The court found that no judgment of conviction had been entered, that petitioner was not being held under any penal sanction, and that he was being restrained involuntarily for compulsory treatment and rehabilitation. The court found that petitioner was not imprisoned as a criminal but was held as a sick person and that it was a permissible exercise of the state's power to regulate narcotic drug traffic. The court found that the facility in which petitioner was detained was not a prison. The court held that the legislature's choice of the figure of six months for the minimum period of confinement did not appear to be unreasonable and that despite the five-year maximum, the term of confinement was essentially still for an indeterminate period. The court held that petitioner's confinement was civil in nature and was not cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner detainee's application for a writ of habeas corpus that challenged the constitutionality of certain sections of the California Penal Code. The court held that the terms "addicted to" and "imminent danger of becoming addicted" in the statute regarding compulsory treatment for addiction to narcotic drugs were not technical terms of art, but had a commonly understood meaning.

People v. Stevenson


Crim. No. 7073


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 794; 376 P.2d 297; 26 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 306



November 20, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Thomas F. Fraga, Judge.

Prosecution for receiving stolen property from a person under the age of 18.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of conviction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which entered judgment of defendant's conviction for receiving stolen property from a 15-year-old boy.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial for the charge of receiving stolen property from the 15-year-old boy, the trial court instructed the jury. The instruction raised the rebuttable presumption that anyone who received or bought stolen property from an individual who was under the age of 18 was to be presumed to have done so with the knowledge that the property had been stolen; the trial court's charge was almost verbatim the last paragraph of Cal. Penal Code § 496. Defendant was subsequently convicted of the crime with which he was charged. Defendant appealed and claimed that the trial court erred in instructing the jury. Upon review, the court reversed the judgment of conviction. The court held that § 496 was unconstitutional insofar as it provided for a presumption of guilty knowledge of the part of one who received stolen property from a minor, and thus the instruction that was based on that unconstitutional presumption was prejudicial error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for receiving stolen property from a 15-year-old boy.
