Bilyeu v. State Employees' Retirement System


S. F. No. 20829


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 618; 375 P.2d 442; 25 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 296



October 25, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County allowing the State Employees' Retirement System a lien against a judgment obtained by plaintiff for personal injuries caused by negligence of a third party. Edward L. Kellas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiff state employee and claimant State Employee's Retirement System (system) appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which allowed the system a lien against a judgment obtained by the employee for personal injuries caused by the negligence of a third party. The lien was allowed for benefits paid to the employee by the system, of which the employee was a member.


OVERVIEW: The employee was injured by a third party while working as a state highway patrolman. The employee recovered damages in an action against the party responsible for his injuries. The system sought a lien against the recovery for the actuarial equivalent of the benefits that it became obligated to pay because of the employee's disability retirement that resulted from the accident. The trial court awarded the system a lien in an amount equal to the sum the system had already paid the employee pursuant to his disability retirement. Both the employee and the system appealed, and the court reversed the holding and remanded the cause. The court found that Cal. Gov't Code § 21451 allowed the system to recover from the employee an amount which was the actuarial equivalent of benefits that were provided by contribution of the state for which the system was liable because of the employee's injury. The court found that the legislation was constitutional because there was a reasonable basis for varying classifications of state employees. The court also found no reference in § 21543 indicating that the Cal. Lab. Code provisions were to be considered in determining the disposition of funds.


OUTCOME: Judgment awarding the system subrogation of amounts already paid to the employee by the system was reversed. The cause was remanded to the trial court with directions to take further evidence if necessary in order to make a finding as to the amount of the actuarial equivalent of the system's lien and for entry of an order in favor of the system based on that amount.

People v. Sidener


Crim. No. 7014


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 645; 375 P.2d 641; 25 Cal. Rptr. 697; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 298



October 25, 1962 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 21, 1962. Schauer, J., and McComb, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. John F. Aiso, Judge.

Prosecution for illegal possession of heroin.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) on an information charging him with possession of a narcotic (heroin) in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. The trial court also found to be true the charge that defendant had been convicted of violating the same section in 1955. Thus, probation was denied and defendant was sentenced to an increased term as a second offender. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the power to dismiss charges was vested exclusively in the courts by Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1 and art. III, § 1, and that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11718 was therefore invalid. The court found that the charge of a prior conviction in the present case had not been dismissed pursuant to legislative authority. The trial court found that the charge was true and it was therefore bound to impose the sentence prescribed by law. The trial court could no more dismiss this charge without statutory authority than it could dismiss a charge against any defendant convicted of murder, arson, rape or any other crime. The court determined that to dismiss the charges in the face of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11718, however, was a flagrant usurpation of legislative power. Such would have exalted form over substance to hold that broad constitutional principles of separation of powers and due process of law permitted vesting complete discretion in the prosecutor before the case began, but denied him all such discretion once the information was filed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

Chance v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L. A. No. 26756


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 275; 373 P.2d 849; 23 Cal. Rptr. 761; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 261



August 2, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition and mandamus to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County from proceeding further with a representative action. Robert H. Patton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writs discharged and peremptory writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two holders of deeds of trust, sought review by writs of prohibition and mandate of an order of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which overruled petitioner's objections to the prosecution of a class action under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 382 by plaintiffs, holders of deeds of trust, against defendants, financial institutions and related entities.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners and plaintiffs were holders of trust deeds that were individually granted to more than 2,000 individuals in an investment scheme by defendant financial institutions wherein each investor was promised a 10 percent return on his investment. The grantor of the deeds of trust went bankrupt and the notes secured by the deeds of trust were defaulted. Plaintiffs filed an action on behalf of themselves and all other owners of trust deeds to recover on the notes. Petitioners objected to plaintiff's prosecution of the class action, and the trial court overruled their objections. On review, the court granted plaintiff's demurrer and denied petitioner's writ petitions. The court ruled that plaintiff's class foreclosure action was valid under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 382 and that a community of interest existed because all notes and trust deeds were identical save for amounts, and each parcel was part of the same tract of land. The court found that the same defenses could be asserted against all trust deed holders and that petitioners were not denied due process because all members were ascertainable and would be provided an opportunity to argue for redress.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' writs challenging plaintiffs' class foreclosure action and granted plaintiffs' demurrer because the class action represented a community of interest where all the notes and deeds were identical except for amounts, each parcel was part of the same tract of land, and all holders were provided with notice.

Duskin v. State Board of Dry Cleaners, etc.


S. F. No. 20957


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 155; 373 P.2d 468; 23 Cal. Rptr. 404; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 249



July 19, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Edward F. O'Day, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the State Board of Dry Cleaners to reinstate plaintiff's licenses to operate a dry cleaning business.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant business owner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandamus compelling respondent State Board of Dry Cleaners to reinstate his dry cleaner's license despite his failure to post a bond under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 9547.


OVERVIEW: The board required the business owner under § 9547 to file a surety bond after receiving evidence that numerous liens were filed against the business owner by business creditors. When no bonds were produced, the business owner's dry cleaning license was revoked. The trial court upheld the board's decision, but the court reversed on appeal, holding that although § 9547 was not unconstitutionally vague and did not unconstitutionally delegate legislative power to the board, the regulations enacted by the board impermissibly enlarged the scope of persons protected by § 9547. Under the wording of the enabling statutes regarding the regulation of the dry cleaning industry, the purpose was to protect only customers, rather than to protect suppliers or other creditors of a licensee. The small allowable recovery indicated only customers were to be protected for the loss of clothing. Since the regulation purported to provide coverage for creditors as well as customers, it was invalid. Thus, the terms of the bond demanded by the board to benefit general creditors was invalid, and the judgment was reversed with directions to order the board to set aside its decision revoking the license.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded with instructions to the court to order the board to set aside its decision revoking the business owner's dry cleaning license.

 

347 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Lane


Crim. No. 6929


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 99; 372 P.2d 897; 22 Cal. Rptr. 857; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 243



June 28, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus to secure her release from the custody of the city police chief. The prisoner was convicted of the crime of "resorting" after a trial in the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California) on two charges of violating Los Angeles, Ca. Municipal Code § 41.07.


OVERVIEW: The evidence in support of the prisoner's convictions was that, in each case, she went from her living room to her bedroom in her own home for the purpose of having sexual intercourse with a male to whom she was not married, in violation of § 41.07. The court held that the state had adopted a general scheme for the regulation of the criminal aspects of sexual activity and determined, to the exclusion of local legislation, when sexual intercourse between persons not married to each other was criminal. The penal code sections of the state law covering this area were so extensive in their scope that they clearly showed that the legislature intended to adopt a general scheme for the regulation of this subject. The court found, therefore, that the legislature had determined by implication that the prisoner's conduct was not criminal in California. Thus, § 41.07, which attempted to make sexual intercourse between persons not married to each other criminal, was in conflict with state law and was void.


OUTCOME: The court granted the prisoner's writ of habeas corpus and ordered the prisoner discharged from custody.

Jones v. Superior Court of Nevada County


Sac. No. 7393


Supreme Court of California


58 Cal. 2d 56; 372 P.2d 919; 22 Cal. Rptr. 879; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 241; 96 A.L.R.2d 1213

PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Nevada County and Vernon Stoll, Judge thereof, from enforcing an order requiring petitioner to produce medical reports and other documentary material in a rape prosecution.

Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the order's enforcement.

The court held that the trial court's order to disclose names and addresses of witnesses and reports that will be brought to trial did not violate the privilege against self-incrimination or the attorney-client privilege. 349 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Rock Products Co. v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 26566


Supreme Court of California


57 Cal. 2d 515; 370 P.2d 342; 20 Cal. Rptr. 638; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 199



April 12, 1962 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 9, 1962. Schauer, J., and McComb, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Philip H. Richards, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief and to enjoin defendant city from enforcing a zoning restriction alleged to be unconstitutional as it applies to prevent rock and gravel operation on plaintiffs' property.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff rock and gravel excavating company filed an action against defendant City of Los Angeles in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), alleging that the City's zoning restriction on the excavating company's land was unconstitutional and void because the zoning denied the company due process and equal protection of the laws, constituted a taking without compensation, and was discriminatory in operation.


OVERVIEW: The excavating company owned a large parcel of land which was bordered by a rock and gravel excavation site on one side and residential property on others. The land was zoned for agricultural and residential use, with rock and gravel excavation being specifically excluded. The trial court found on behalf of the City. On appeal, the court determined that the trial court did not err in its findings and conclusions. The land was not useful as agricultural or residential land, because the soil was of poor quality and the land was subject to flooding. However, the exclusion of rock and gravel excavation had a basis in protecting the public health. The air pollution, traffic and other dangers resulting from the excavation would be harmful to the surrounding population, which consisted of a high number of individuals with respiratory ailments. The City's comprehensive zoning program was constitutional. An exercise of police powers such as zoning did not constitute a taking. The zoning was not discriminatory in its operation because there were significant differences in the adjoining land which did operate as a rock and gravel excavation site, and the excavation company's land.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. The adoption by the City of a comprehensive zoning plan looking toward the containment and eventual elimination of nonconforming uses accorded with recognized zoning objectives under well settled legal principles.

 

350 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Corning Hospital Dist. v. Superior Court of Tehama County


Sac. No. 7370


Supreme Court of California


57 Cal. 2d 488; 370 P.2d 325; 20 Cal. Rptr. 621; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 192



April 4, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to prevent the Superior Court of Tehama County from conducting further proceedings in an action brought against a hospital district.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hospital district sought a writ of prohibition preventing respondent superior court from conducting proceedings, contending that under 1961 Cal. Stat. ch. 1404 an action for personal injuries brought against the hospital district was barred under the doctrine of governmental immunity.


OVERVIEW: In a prior decision the court held that the doctrine of governmental immunity no longer shielded governmental entities from liability for torts for which their agents were liable. The legislature then enacted 1961 Cal. Stat. ch. 1404, which provided that the doctrine of governmental immunity from tort liability was re-enacted as a rule of decision in the state and applied to causes of action arising before and after its effective date. Section 3 of the act stated that it would expire after the 1963 legislative session. The court held that the 1961 act temporarily suspended the cause of action for personal injuries against the hospital district, but did not destroy it, and was a reasonable exercise of the police power. The prior decision had recognized a new cause of action that imposed considerable financial obligations on governmental agencies and the legislation temporarily suspended enforcement of that right pending further study and legislative action. Absent further legislation, tort claims against governmental entities would be continued and the superior court would be restrained from proceeding further.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining the superior court from proceeding in a manner inconsistent with the procedure set out in the opinion.

Burks v. Poppy Construction Co.


S. F. No. 20809


Supreme Court of California


57 Cal. 2d 463; 370 P.2d 313; 20 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 187



March 26, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Edward F. O'Day, Judge.

Action for damages and injunctive relief for alleged discrimination with respect to the sale of a house in a tract.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendants after general demurrer to complaint was sustained, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homebuyers appealed the dismissal of their complaint by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which complaint had alleged that defendants, tract developer and its employee, had violated the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51 and 52 (as amended 1959), and the Hawkins Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 35700-35741 (added in 1959), in their refusal to sell plaintiffs a home because of their race.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homebuyers were refused in their attempt to buy a home because of their race. They brought suit under the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51 and 52 (as amended 1959), and the Hawkins Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 35700 - 35741 (added in 1959). Defendants, tract developer and its employee, contended the more specific Hawkins Act was the sole measure of discrimination in real property transactions and that the Hawkins Act contravened the equal protection clause because it distinguished between publicly assisted housing and other kinds of housing. The trial court dismissed both counts, referencing defendants' brief, and the court reversed, holding that the public policy of the United States and California were strongly opposed to racial discrimination in business in general and housing in particular. This strong public policy also showed that there was a reasonable basis to distinguish publicly assisted housing.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed because the statutes on which plaintiff homebuyers based their complaint were constitutional and applied to the conduct alleged.

Kerr's Catering Service v. Department of Industrial Relations


S. F. No. 20617


Supreme Court of California


57 Cal. 2d 319; 369 P.2d 20; 19 Cal. Rptr. 492; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 175; 15 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 390; 44 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P50,466



February 19, 1962 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 21, 1962.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Cecil Mosbacher, Judge.

Action to have an order of the Industrial Welfare Commission declared unconstitutional and void.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Summary judgment for plaintiff reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Industrial Relations challenged the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which awarded summary judgment in favor of plaintiff employer. Plaintiff claimed a wage withholding regulation of defendant was unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Department of Industrial Relations sought review when the lower court held that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, § 11380 was unconstitutional and void. The court reversed the order of the lower court that entered judgment in favor of plaintiff employer. The court held that subdivisions (a) and (b) of Cal. Lab. Code § 1182 did not limit defendant's power to fix, under Cal. Lab. Code § 1182(c), only those "standard conditions of labor" which did not affect either wages or hours. The court ruled that deductions for cash shortages not due to an employee's dishonest or wilful act or culpable negligence were properly characterized as standard conditions of labor. The court held that the legislature did not intend that subdivisions (a), (b), and (c) of § 1182 be mutually exclusive. The court held that § 11380 did not violate constitutional due process or equal protection of the law.


OUTCOME: The court held that the regulation of defendant Department of Industrial Relations that prohibited deduction from workers' wages for cash shortages did not violate due process or equal protection provisions. The court reversed the order of the lower court that awarded summary judgment to plaintiff employer.

Blumenthal v. Board of Medical Examiners


L. A. No. 26565


Supreme Court of California


57 Cal. 2d 228; 368 P.2d 101; 18 Cal. Rptr. 501; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 167



January 18, 1962 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 14, 1962.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Ellsworth Meyer, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Board of Medical Examiners to register an applicant as a dispensing optician.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent board of medical examiners to register him as a dispensing optician.


OVERVIEW: An applicant was denied a license as a dispensing optician because he had not established he had at least five full years of actual experience in an establishment of a dispensing optician registered in California, or that he had been licensed as a dispensing optician for five years in another state, in accordance with Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2552(a). He submitted a second application, which was denied on the same ground and also because he had not met the requirement of good moral character in § 2552(b), having pleaded guilty to a misdemeanor of dispensing optical goods without a license. The applicant filed a petition for a writ of mandamus to compel the board of medical examiners to grant him a license, contending that § 2552(a) was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the writ. On appeal, the court reversed, and held that there was no relationship between the experience requirements of the statute and the legislative effort to correct ethical abuses in the profession. Also, § 2552(a) was invalid because it was a delegation of legislative authority to an administrative body without an ascertainable standard to guide the agency's action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded the case for issuance of a writ ordering the board of medical examiners to register the applicant as a dispensing optician.

 

354 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Di Genova v. State Board of Education


S. F. No. 20720


Supreme Court of California


57 Cal. 2d 167; 367 P.2d 865; 18 Cal. Rptr. 369; 1962 Cal. LEXIS 163



January 9, 1962 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. John B. Molinari, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the State Board of Education to reinstate plaintiff's teaching credentials and to compel the Board of Education of the City and County of San Francisco to reinstate plaintiff to his position as a teacher.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment granting writ, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher filed a petition for mandate, alleging that his dismissal from employment without notice or hearing and the revocation of his teaching credentials were invalid actions. The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) entered judgment in favor of the teacher. Defendants, State Board of Education and the Board of Education of the City and County of San Francisco (boards of education), appealed.


OVERVIEW: In 1945, the teacher was charged with a misdemeanor, "vagrancy lewd." After pleading guilty, he was sentenced and paid a fine. In late 1948 to early 1949, the teacher received a general elementary school credential and was employed as a teacher by the San Francisco school district. He acquired tenure and remained a teacher in the district until the date of his dismissal. In 1952, sections were added to the education code, which in general prohibited the employment in public schools of persons convicted of certain sex offenses. Thereafter, the teacher was dismissed and his credentials were revoked due to his 1945 misdemeanor. On appeal, the court held that the new legislation, Cal. Educ. Code §§ 13207, 13255, did not apply retrospectively to a person such as the teacher who was convicted of a sex offense prior to the enactment of the legislation. The court ruled that no statute was to be given retroactive effect unless the legislature had expressly so declared. The court found that nothing in either section pointed in the direction of retroactive application and that certainly none of its language constituted an express declaration that the statute was to operate retroactively.


OUTCOME: The judgment directing the boards of education to reinstate the teacher's credentials and to reinstate the teacher to his position in the public schools of San Francisco was affirmed.

DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiff reversed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which rendered a decision of a favor of plaintiff builder in the builder's action to have city ordinances requiring payment of sewer connection charge declared unconstitutional and to recover payments.

The builder's assignors paid the connection charges. The builder brought an action to have the ordinances declared unconstitutional and to have the payments refunded. OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which had held the ordinances unconstitutional.

Greyhound Corp. v. Superior Court of Merced County


Sac. No. 7274


Supreme Court of California


56 Cal. 2d 355; 364 P.2d 266; 15 Cal. Rptr. 90; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 302



August 3, 1961 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied August 30, 1961. Schauer, J., and McComb, J., were of the Opinion that the Application Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to prevent enforcement of an order of the Superior Court of Merced County granting inspection and copying or photographing of certain documents.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner carrier filed an alternative writ of prohibition to prevent enforcement of an order of respondent Superior Court of Merced County (California), which granted the motion of real parties in interest, accident victims who were plaintiffs below, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2031 (1957), for inspection and copying or photographing of certain documents.


OVERVIEW: In support of its application for a writ, the carrier argued that the accident victims' motion for inspection should have been denied because there was an insufficient showing of good cause, the statements of the independent witnesses were exempt from discovery by reason of privilege, and that the statements were the work product of the litigant or its attorney, and were therefore exempt. The court ruled that the trial court properly ordered the disclosure of the statements because (1) the accident victims made a sufficient showing of good cause to support the order of the trial court, (2) the mere inadmissibility of the material was not, of itself, a bar to discovery under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2016(b), (3) the carrier's privilege claim was unavailing because its action in gathering and transmitting the witnesses' statements to its attorney did not create an attorney-client privilege, (4) the carrier's work product claim was likewise unavailing because it did not show that work product was equated with privilege in the state, and it did not show that the reasons underlying the doctrine would have been applicable to the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the carrier's application for a peremptory writ.

People v. Monk


Crim. No. 6791


Supreme Court of California


56 Cal. 2d 288; 363 P.2d 865; 14 Cal. Rptr. 633; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 295



July 20, 1961 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 16, 1961.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL, automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b), from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Mark Brandler, Judge.

Prosecution for kidnaping for purpose of robbery, subjecting victims to bodily harm, and for robbery, rape and sex perversion.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction imposing the death penalty on counts charging kidnaping and bodily harm, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of kidnapping for purpose of robbery, subjecting victims to bodily harm, robbery, rape, and sex perversion and which denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, with a gun, kidnapped a woman. When he slowed the car, the woman threw herself out and was injured. A few weeks later, another woman had car problems and got out of her car. Defendant approached and kidnapped her and her son. He beat her, raped her, and stabbed her. Defendant was arrested for kidnapping for purpose of robbery, subjecting victims to bodily harm, robbery, rape, and sex perversion. He pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity, but he was convicted and given the death penalty and his motion for a new trial was denied. An automatic appeal was taken. The court affirmed, holding that the evidence as to defendant's sanity at the time he committed the crimes charged could be regarded as conflicting because medical reports indicated that he was hostile and it was difficult to obtain information from him. Defendant's wife testified that her husband underwent periodic spells of physical and sexual cruelty when he would do strange things, such as burning his arms, for which he would later apologize; but, it could not be said that the evidence failed to support the finding that defendant was legally sane when he committed the crimes and at the time of trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for kidnapping for purpose of robbery, subjecting victims to bodily harm, rape, and sex perversion.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying petition, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his request for attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: A taxpayer intervened in an action over oil and gas revenues from submerged lands. Through her attorney, the taxpayer alleged that plaintiff in that action was prosecuting a friendly suit and that he had failed to raise the question of the unlawful expenditure of dry-gas revenues and other important legal and constitutional issues. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the attorney's petition for fees.

OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for loitering. Before his trial, defendant petitioned the court and alleged that Cal. OUTCOME: Defendant's alternative writ of prohibition, which was deemed an order to show cause, and the order to show cause, were discharged. Defendant's petition for a writ of prohibition, which was deemed to be an application for habeas corpus, based on the alleged unconstitutionality of the charging statute, was denied. Defendant was remanded for a trial on the charge of loitering.

People v. Combes

Supreme Court of California


56 Cal. 2d 135; 363 P.2d 4; 14 Cal. Rptr. 4; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 279

Prosecution for murder.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. The Superior Court of Ventura County (California) entered the penalty of life imprisonment. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239, his automatic appeal was heard.


OVERVIEW: The court reviewed the various arguments by defendant and determined that (1) he was not prejudiced by any unnecessary delay in taking him before a magistrate because he was arraigned in less than two days before the magistrate who issued the warrant; (2) it was not error for the magistrate to allow the district attorney to dismiss the complaint and try and convict defendant under an indictment by the grand jury; (3) the dismissal of the complaint did not bar another prosecution on the same offense; (4) defendant was not denied a right to counsel during the lapse between the dismissal of the complaint and his arraignment on the indictment because during the lapse, defendant was not entitled to counsel; and (5) the trial judge's denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence was proper. Rather, the court determined that defendant had a fair and impartial trial in which his rights were preserved at each stage of the proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder and the imposition of a life sentence.

 

363 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Scandinavian Airlines System, Inc. v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 25637


Supreme Court of California


56 Cal. 2d 11; 363 P.2d 25; 14 Cal. Rptr. 25; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 272



May 29, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Philbrick McCoy, Judge.

Action by owner of foreign owned and registered aircraft to recover property taxes levied by both a city and county against airplanes flown exclusively in foreign commerce.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city and a county, appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of plaintiff foreign airline in its action to recover personal property taxes paid under protest on its airplanes flown exclusively in foreign commerce.


OVERVIEW: The foreign airline used one United States port several times a year for entry into the United States. Its airplanes were registered one-third each in Sweden, Norway, and Denmark, where they were taxed. The airplanes engaged exclusively in foreign commerce. The city and the county in the United States levied an apportioned personal property tax on the airplanes for the time that they were at the United States airport. The airline successfully protested and received a refund of its taxes. The court affirmed on appeal, finding no error. The court held that the exclusively federal nature of the field of international commerce required it to apply the "home-port" doctrine, and thus to hold that no jurisdiction save that of domicile had any authority to levy a personal property tax on the airplanes. It did not matter whether the home port actually levied any tax. Under the home-port doctrine, based either on the Commerce Clause or the Due Process Clause, the city and the county had no taxing authority to exercise. Airplanes were similar to ships. The court also found that a treaty with Sweden would, in any event, prevent taxation on the one-third of the airplanes registered there.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment below.

 

364 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Flores v. Los Angeles Turf Club, Inc.


L. A. No. 26202


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 736; 361 P.2d 921; 13 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 252



May 8, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Rex Hardy, Judge.

Action by a race track patron to enjoin future exclusion or forcible ejection from defendants' race track and for damages for alleged wrongful ejection and assault and battery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal on injunction count and denying plaintiff recovery on other count, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) dismissed an action by plaintiff, a race track patron, for an injunction against his future exclusion or forcible ejection by defendants, race track owners, from their race track and denied recovery on the race track patron's cause of action for statutory, general, and special damages for alleged wrongful ejection and assault and battery. The race track patron appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that: (1) because the patron had been convicted of bookmaking, the race track owners were duty-bound under Cal. Bus. & Prof. § 19561.5 to eject him; and (2) the patron had become a trespasser upon his refusal to leave the track, and no more force was used in ejecting him than was necessary. The trial court also found that the patron's exclusive remedy respecting future ejection was that provided by section 19561.5 -- an administrative hearing before the racing board. Because the patron had not pursued this remedy, the court dismissed his injunctive cause of action for lack of jurisdiction. The patron urged that section 19561.5 was unconstitutional and that the administrative remedy was inadequate. The court affirmed. The court held that: (1) section 19561.5, which barred only those convicted of crimes directly related to the activities regulated, was based upon a rational classification of persons having a substantial relation to a legitimate legislative objective and therefore was constitutional; and (2) the administrative hearing did not furnish an inadequate remedy merely because the patron could not have qualified for relief under it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Bernkrant v. Fowler


L. A. No. 25689


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 588; 360 P.2d 906; 12 Cal. Rptr. 266; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 239



April 13, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. James G. Whyte, Judge.

Action against an executrix to have an installment note secured by a trust deed cancelled and the property reconveyed to plaintiffs, and to recover amounts paid under protest to the executrix after decedent's death.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff buyers sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that entered judgment in favor of defendant executrix in an action to recover funds paid in protest, to have an installment note secured by a trust deed cancelled, and to have the property reconveyed to the buyers.


OVERVIEW: The buyers purchased an apartment complex in Nevada. By agreement of the parties, the buyers refinanced their obligations and paid a substantial part of their indebtedness to the seller in exchange for the seller's promise that he would provide in his will that upon his death all their indebtedness to him would be canceled. When the seller died in California, he did not provide for the cancellation of the buyers' balance and the buyers continued to make regular payments under protest. The buyers brought an action against the executrix to have the note cancelled and the property reconveyed to them. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the executrix. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment, reasoning the laws of Nevada applied and that the lower court improperly determined that the parties' contract was subject to the California statute of frauds.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. Protection of the buyers' rights under a valid contract precluded interpreting the general language of the statute of frauds to destroy such rights despite possible applicability of the statute arising from the movement of one or more of the parties across state lines.

Erlich v. Municipal Court of Beverly Hills Judicial Dist.


L. A. No. 26023


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 553; 360 P.2d 334; 11 Cal. Rptr. 758; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 235



March 20, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Municipal Court of the Beverly Hills Judicial District from further proceedings against petitioner on a criminal charge.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was charged in the Municipal Court of Beverly Hills with a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 383b for fraudulently selling kosher meat. Petitioner had filed a writ of prohibition that sought to prohibit respondent municipal court from further proceeding against him on that charge.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with fraudulently selling kosher meat in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 383b. Petitioner had filed a writ of prohibition that sought to prohibit the municipal court from further proceeding against him on that charge. The court noted that the term "kosher", as provided in § 383b, meant strict compliance with every Jewish law and custom. Petitioner alleged that the term "kosher", as provided in § 383b, was too vague, ambiguous, indefinite and uncertain. The court held that because § 383b was patterned on a similar New York statute it was entitled to the same construction given by the New York courts on the meaning of kosher. The court concluded that the reference to every Jewish law and custom had to be construed to include only such Jewish laws and customs that were generally recognized among the orthodox Hebrew religious requirements for the preparation of meat products. The court declined to grant the writ of prohibition because the statute upon which petitioner was charged was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of prohibition and declined to prohibit the municipal court from further proceeding against him on the charge of fraudulently selling kosher meat.

In re Newbern


Crim. No. 6661


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 500; 360 P.2d 43; 11 Cal. Rptr. 547; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 229



March 9, 1961 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed suit for habeas corpus alleging that his bail was excessive and that he was unconstitutionally denied the right to make a telephone call, in accordance with Cal. Penal Code § 851.5, after his arrests for misdemeanor intoxication charges.


OVERVIEW: A prisoner was arrested twice for intoxication. Later he appeared in court in an obviously intoxicated condition. His cases were continued, and bail was set for $ 500 per case. Thereafter the prisoner appeared for trial on a third charge of intoxication before the same judge who had sentenced him in the other two cases. He was granted a continuance to prepare a motion to disqualify the judge but shortly thereafter, was returned to jail in default of bail in the previous two cases, and bail was set at $ 100. The jailer denied the prisoner's request to call a bail bondsman. The prisoner filed a petition for habeas corpus, alleging that his bail was illegally high and that the denial of his request to call a bail bondsman violated his constitutional rights. The court held that the bail pending appeal in the previous two cases was not illegally high because its purpose was to assure a defendant would attend court when required to and also that he would be sober. However, the prisoner was unconstitutionally deprived of his rights under Cal. Penal Code § 851.5 to make a phone call to a bail bondsman, but the only remedy was that the jailer could be convicted of a misdemeanor.


OUTCOME: The prisoner's petition for habeas corpus was denied.

 

368 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Muskopf v. Corning Hospital Dist.


Sac. No. 7229


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 211; 359 P.2d 457; 11 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 204



January 27, 1961 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 21, 1961. Schauer, J., and McComb, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tehama County. Curtiss E. Wetter, Judge.

Action for damages for injuries sustained by a paying patient in a hospital operated by defendant hospital district.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant after general demurrer to complaint was sustained and plaintiff refused to amend, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, patient and her husband, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Tehama County (California), which sustained appellee hospital district's demurrer to appellants' action. Appellants claimed appellant patient sustained injury through the negligence of appellee's staff. Appellee demurred on the ground that it was immune from tort liability because it was a state agency exercising a governmental function.


OVERVIEW: Appellants claimed that the negligence of appellee's staff caused appellant patient additional injuries. Appellee demurred claiming it was immune from tort liability as it was a state agency exercising a governmental function. The trial court sustained the demurer. The court reversed. It found that the legislature had adopted a statute that allowed the state to sue and be sued and that a similar statute applied to hospital districts such as appellee. The court held that although those statutes have been construed as providing only a waiver from suit and not a waiver of substantive immunity, their continuous reenactment indicated a clear legislative purpose to remove all procedural obstacles when the state was liable. As such, the court held that the rule of governmental immunity from tort liability must be discarded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision to sustain appellee hospital district's demurrer based on immunity from tort liability. The legislative purpose behind continuously reenacting statutes that allowed the state and hospital districts to be sued was clearly to remove all procedural obstacles when the state was liable and therefore the rule of governmental immunity from tort liability was discarded.

American Civil Liberties Union v. Board of Education


L. A. No. 25788


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 167; 359 P.2d 45; 10 Cal. Rptr. 647; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 198; 94 A.L.R.2d 1259



January 24, 1961 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 21, 1961. Schauer, J., McComb, J., and White, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles to grant an application for use of certain public school buildings in which to hold public meetings.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner American Civil Liberties Union of Southern California sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles to grant their application for the use of a certain high school auditorium in which to hold a series of monthly public meetings.


OVERVIEW: The union's application was denied by the board because they had refused to furnish the "statement of information" as required by Cal. Educ. Code § 16565, which implements Cal. Educ. Code § 16564. The union refused to furnish the prescribed statements primarily on the ground that such test oath requirement abridged both the state and federal constitutional guarantees of freedom of speech and assembly under the U.S. Const. amend. I and XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 9, 10. The union sought a writ of mandate and the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the board to act upon any pending or future application by the union to be allowed to hold a public meeting or meetings at the certain junior high school auditorium without requiring a "statement of information" in the form prescribed by Cal. Educ. Code § 16565. The court held that the power of the board to refuse the use of the school to an applicant upon making the finding required by Cal. Educ. Code § 16564 amounted to a prior restraint because it foreclosed the use of the school and prevented the applicant from there holding its meeting upon a prior determination of the board.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the board to act upon any pending or future application by the union by allowing it to hold a public meeting or meetings in the junior high school without requiring a "statement of information" from the union.

Los Angeles v. Offner


L. A. No. 25841


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 103; 358 P.2d 926; 10 Cal. Rptr. 470; 1961 Cal. LEXIS 191



January 6, 1961 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied January 31, 1961. Lillie, J. Pro Tem., * Participated Therein in Place of McComb, J., Who Deemed Himself Disqualified. Gibson, C. J., Traynor, J., and Dooling, J., were of the Opinion that the Application Should be Granted.


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the secretary of a city's board of public works to post and publish notices inviting bids for sewer construction work.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city instituted a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent, the secretary of board of public works, to post and publish notices inviting bids for sewer construction work pursuant to the Improvement Act of 1911, as codified in Cal. Sts. & High. §§ 5000- 6794.


OVERVIEW: The secretary refused to post and publish notices as ordered by the city council. The court held that the ordinances providing for the special assessment and directing a local improvement were unconstitutional. Specifically, Cal. Sts. & High. § 5024(i), and the local legislation thereunder, were invalid insofar as they included the charges purportedly authorized by § 5024(i). An assessment exceeding the cost of a proposed improvement, so as to furnish revenue to the city, violated the general principle of equality stated in Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1. Nor did one of the local ordinances assume to limit or relate a charge for outlet facilities to the cost of the subject improvement. The charges were not fixed with specific reference to the costs. The improvement was not to be of local benefit to the subject assessment district. The city could not be permitted to divert special assessment funds for general taxation purposes. Thus, the city's petition for mandate was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ requested by the city.

People v. Sweeney


Crim. No. 6708


Supreme Court of California


55 Cal. 2d 27; 357 P.2d 1049; 9 Cal. Rptr. 793; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 134



December 16, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 10, 1961. White, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Bayard Rhone, Judge.

Prosecution for giving away a narcotic to a hospital patient, and for offering and giving a bribe to a deputy sheriff.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of giving away amidone, a narcotic, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500 and giving a bribe to a deputy sheriff in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 67. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denied defendant's motion for a new trial. Defendant appealed the judgment and the denial of his motion.


OVERVIEW: While visiting his alleged mistress in the hospital, defendant gave her amidone. Based on prior suspicions, a nurse contacted hospital guards and defendant was detained. After his release, he agreed to meet with a deputy sheriff, who secretly recorded their conversation where he offered the deputy a bribe. The bribe was culminated at a later time and resulted in defendant's arrest. Defendant argued, inter alia, that he was entrapped. The court held that: (1) defendant's affidavit of prejudice to disqualify the trial judge contained only conclusions and was thus insufficient; (2) impeachment evidence was properly excluded as defendant failed to lay a proper foundation for introducing a prior inconsistent statement, (3) the deputy's testimony was primary evidence and the tape recording was not the best evidence; (4) evidence of defendant's relationship with his mistress was admissible to establish bias and the limiting instruction was sufficient; (5) an improper comment by the State was harmless; and (6) defendant was not entrapped as there was substantial evidence that the criminal intent originated with defendant given that it was he who first broached the topic of a bribe.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motion for new trial and affirmed the judgment convicting him of giving away amidone and giving a bribe to a deputy sheriff.

 

372 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kusior v. Silver


L. A. No. 25304


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 2d 603; 354 P.2d 657; 7 Cal. Rptr. 129; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 193



August 12, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Brodie Ahlport, Judge.

Action to establish paternity and to provide for support of a child born nine days after entry of a final divorce judgment.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for defendant reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held in favor of respondent father in her action to establish paternity and to provide for support of a child, which was born nine days after the entry of a final decree of divorce dissolving her marriage to another man.


OVERVIEW: Mother brought suit against father to establish paternity of her child, which was born nine days after the entry of a divorce decree dissolving her marriage to another. The trial court's instructions were to the effect that a conclusive presumption, which no other evidence in the case could contradict, would apply if there were a failure to show that there was not a reasonable possibility of access to mother. The court reversed and held that the court's explanation of the term "cohabiting," with regard to legal presumptions, was error. The instructions defining the scope of the conclusive presumption were too broad in that "cohabiting," as used in Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1962(5), should properly have been construed as the living together of a man and woman ostensibly as husband and wife. The result of blood tests were not to be used to controvert the conclusive presumption of paternity, but if the conclusions of all the experts were that the alleged father was not the father, the question of paternity should have been resolved accordingly. Where the tests taken established that mother's husband could not have been the father, the rebuttable presumptions were conclusively rebutted.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court in favor of father was reversed.

Syrek v. California Unemployment Ins. Appeals Board


S. F. No. 20422


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 2d 519; 354 P.2d 625; 7 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 188



August 2, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Folger Emerson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel payment of unemployment insurance benefits to petitioner.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unemployment compensation petitioner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate to respondents, the Unemployment Compensation Insurance Appeals Board and its director of employment, to compel payment of benefits. The Supreme Court of California granted a hearing after the District Court of Appeal, First District Division One (California), reversed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant unemployment compensation petitioner refused to apply for government employment based on his conscientious objection to the loyalty oath required of government employees. A Department of Employment referee denied him benefits and respondents, the Unemployment Compensation Insurance Appeals Board and its Director of Employment, affirmed. Appellant filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondents to pay him benefits. The trial court denied the petition and appellant sought review. The court granted a hearing after the intermediate appellant court reversed. The court adopted the appellate court's opinion, which held that there was no California law that conditioned the receipt of unemployment benefits on the taking of a loyalty oath. The court also found that appellant's objection to the loyalty oath constituted "good cause" for his refusal to apply for government employment within the meaning of Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1257, both from the standpoint of the public interest and from the standpoint of the individual's rights. The court held that appellant could not have been denied benefits because he refused to take a loyalty oath where his refusal was not a sham.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant unemployment compensation petitioner's request for a writ of mandate to respondents, the Unemployment Compensation Insurance Board and its director of employment, to compel payment of benefits. The court held that appellant's conscientious objection to the loyalty oath required for government employment constituted good cause for his refusal to apply, as used in the unemployment compensation laws.

Forster Shipbuilding Co. v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 25668


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 2d 450; 353 P.2d 736; 6 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 181; 12 Oil & Gas Rep. 1213



July 8, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. George Francis, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action for taxes paid under protest.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for defendants reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessees challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that found that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107.1 was void and that the lessees were not entitled to recover from defendant county taxes paid under protest on the full cash value of their possessory interest in tax-exempt property.


OVERVIEW: The lessees held leasehold interests in certain lands and improvements owned by the city. The county assessed their leasehold interests without deducting the present worth of rentals for the unexpired terms as allowed by § 107.1 for certain holders of possessory interests on the ground that that § 107.1 was void as inconsistent with the California constitutional requirement that all property be assessed at its full cash value. The lessees paid the assessed taxes under protest and filed an action to recover the disputed amounts, and the trial court, finding that § 107.1 was unconstitutional, entered judgment for the county. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that although the first paragraph of § 107.1 was invalid because it improperly classified leasehold interests in tax-exempt property as personal property within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 14, the elimination of that paragraph in no way disturbed the scheme of taxation set forth in the remainder of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107.1. The court found that the legislature had the power to depart from assessment at full cash value in order to mitigate hardships caused by the overruling of established law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that denied the recovery of taxes paid by the lessees under protest and directed the trial court to remand the case to the county for hearing to assess the possessory interests according to the terms of the statute.

Pappa v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L. A. No. 25894


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 2d 350; 353 P.2d 311; 5 Cal. Rptr. 703; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 171



June 23, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and LeRoy Dawson, Judge thereof, from retrying the case.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, individual and physician, appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), denying defendant individual's motion to disqualify brought under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 against respondents, Superior Court of Los Angeles County and judge, to restrain the judge from retrying the case on the charges of murder and conspiracy to commit murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, individual and physician, contested the superior court decision refusing to grant their motion to disqualify, pursuant to § 170.6, the judge from retrying the case charging them both with murder and conspiracy to commit murder. The court granted an alternative writ of prohibition to entertain this appeal, but then discharged it and denied the requested peremptory writ on the merits. Based on these facts, the court held that defendant individual failed to establish under § 170.6(3) that her interests were substantially adverse to those of defendant physician as to constitute two different "sides" in their combined criminal action. The court found that defendant individual did not provide reasons for either requesting a separate trial from defendant physician or for opposing defendant physician's independent motion to change venue. Therefore, the court did not consider those matters important enough to constitute any substantially adverse interest that would warrant disqualification of the judge. The court did not conclude that defendants had adverse interests on the basis that they were represented by separate counsels because they had selected their own counsel.


OUTCOME: The court denied the requested peremptory writ of prohibition in the appeal by defendant individual and defendant physician from the superior court decision denying defendant individual's motion for disqualification. Defendant individual's interests were not substantially adverse to those of defendant physician.

People v. Harmon


Crim. No. 6532


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 2d 9; 351 P.2d 329; 4 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 139



April 8, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b)) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County. Stanley Lawson, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with malice aforethought by force likely to produce great bodily injury, committed by one undergoing a life sentence in a state prison.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of death by the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which convicted him of assault with malice aforethought by force likely to produce great bodily injury, committed by one undergoing a life sentence in a state prison pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4500. At the time that the assault was committed, the judgment made the death penalty for such offense mandatory.


OVERVIEW: While an incarcerated in a state prison, defendant stabbed another inmate and was convicted of Cal. Penal Code § 4500 and sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held: (1) contentions that defendant was denied effective representation were not supported by the record; (2) Cal. Penal Code § 4500 applied to defendant because at the time of the assault, defendant was serving a life sentence pursuant to Cal Penal Code. § 213; (3) photographic evidence was properly admitted because the photographs were not prejudicial; (4) the prosecutor did not commit misconduct in demonstrating the use of the weapon, and the opening statement was brief and free from error; (5) asking prospective jurors if they had any conscientious objection to the death penalty was proper because a judgment of death would be rendered if defendant was convicted; (6) defendant was not entitled to the benefit of an amendment of Cal. Penal Code § 4500, which mitigated the penalty, because the saving clause, Cal. Gov. Code § 9608, applied, as the amendment remained silent as to whether it was to be retroactively operated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence to death.

Messner v. Journeymen Barbers, etc.


L. A. No. 25288


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 873; 351 P.2d 347; 4 Cal. Rptr. 179; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 262; 45 L.R.R.M. 3135; 40 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P66,450



April 7, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. James C. Toothaker, Judge.

Action to enjoin defendants from picketing a barber shop.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for plaintiff reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in plaintiff's action to enjoin defendants from picketing its barber shop.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a barber. Defendants attempted to organize all the barber shops in the area. They submitted a contract to plaintiff that would have required him and his four barber employees to join defendant's organization. Defendants did not represent any of plaintiff's employees and the employees did not wish to join the union or to be represented by defendants. Plaintiff refused to sign the contract, which led to defendants' peaceful picketing in order to secure the union shop agreement. The trial court enjoined defendants from picketing plaintiff's barber shop. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court, finding that peaceful picketing for recognition by a union that does not represent a majority of the employees was not an unfair labor practice. The court found basic to the rights guaranteed to employees under 29 U.S.C.S. § 7 to form, join, or assist labor organizations is the right to engage in concerted activities to persuade other employees to join for their mutual aid and protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, finding that the peaceful picketing by defendants to persuade other employees to join a labor union was not an unfair labor practice.

In re Newbern


Crim. No. 6565


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 786; 350 P.2d 116; 3 Cal. Rptr. 364; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 252



March 8, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPLICATION for a writ of habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted; petitioner ordered discharged from custody of sheriff of county and committed to custody of chief of police of a city for further proceedings as to one misdemeanor charge, and discharged as to another.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus challenging his convictions for vagrancy and appearing in a public place in an intoxicated condition rendered against him in the Municipal Court (California).


OVERVIEW: Four days after his arraignment, petitioner was convicted of vagrancy and for appearing in a public place in an intoxicated condition. Petitioner's motions for a new trial and in arrest of judgment were denied. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus. The court granted certiorari and set aside the convictions. The mere change of the date on which the vagrancy offense was alleged to have been committed did not require the trial court to grant petitioner a continuance. However, petitioner was otherwise deprived of the effective aid of counsel in that the trial court failed to give petitioner at least five days in which to prepare for trial after his plea was entered as required by statute. Further, Cal. Penal Code § 647(11), the vagrancy statute under which petitioner was convicted, was so lacking in its definition of "common drunk" that each defendant was left to the vagaries of individual judges and juries. Thus, the statute was unconstitutional vague, uncertain, and incapable of being uniformly enforced and petitioner was entitled to a discharge from custody by reason of his conviction thereunder.


OUTCOME: The writ of certiorari was granted, and petitioner's convictions were set aside. Petitioner was ordered discharged from custody by reason of his conviction under the vagrancy statute. However, he was committed to the custody of city police for further proceedings on the other offense for which he was charged.

In re Cooper


Crim. Nos. 6570, 6571, 6572, 6573, 6574, 6575


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 772; 349 P.2d 956; 3 Cal. Rptr. 140; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 251



March 4, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Applications for a Rehearing were Denied March 30, 1960. Schauer, J., McComb, J., and Peters, J., were of the Opinion that the Applications Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDINGS in habeas corpus challenging validity of proceedings to extradite petitioners to Pennsylvania to stand trial for the distribution of obscene literature mailed from California and related offenses.



DISPOSITION: Writs denied and petitioners remanded to custody.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought writs of habeas corpus to challenge the validity of proceedings to extradite them to Pennsylvania to stand trial for the distribution of obscene literature and related offenses.


OVERVIEW: Pennsylvania sought extradition under section 6 of the Uniform Criminal Extradition Act, Cal. Penal Code § 1549.1, on the ground that in mailing obscene literature each petitioner committed an act in California intentionally resulting in a crime in Pennsylvania. The court held that states were free to extend interstate rendition beyond that required by federal law. Section 1549.1 authorized the Governor to surrender any person charged in another state upon the demand of the executive authority of that state. Cal. Penal Code § 1548.2 required the demand to be accompanied by a copy of an indictment found, an information or an affidavit. Though the affidavits did not set forth the affiants' sources of information or reasons for belief that petitioners had committed the offenses charged, the indictments established reasonable cause and the affidavits demonstrated that the indictments were based on petitioners' California activities. It must be presumed that sufficient evidence was presented to the grand jury to support the indictments. The court could not say that the invalidity of a related statute made the law under which petitioners were indicted void on its face.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writs for habeas corpus and remanded the petitioners to custody.

Santa Barbara County Water Agency v. All Persons & Parties, etc.


L. A. No. 22761


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 743; 350 P.2d 100; 3 Cal. Rptr. 348; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 248



February 29, 1960 


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County. Charles F. Blackstock, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Proceeding by a county water agency to secure a judicial determination of the legality of the establishment of such agency and its member units and the validity of a contract between the agency and the United States and member-unit contracts entered into between the agency and certain public bodies.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon remand by the United States Supreme Court, the court reviewed its judgment favoring plaintiff county water agency (agency) declaring the validity of the Santa Barbara Water Agency Act, 1945 Cal. Stat. 1501, and the existence of the agency and its member units. It had also reversed a judgment that declared valid a contract between the agency and the United States, as well as member-unit contracts between the agency and certain public bodies.


OVERVIEW: The agency sought to secure the judicial determination of the legality of the establishment of the agency and its member units, and the validity of a contract between the agency and United States and member-unit contracts entered into between agency and certain public bodies. The trial court ruled in favor of the agency. Defendants appealed, and the court affirmed the judgment as to the validity of the Santa Barbara Water Agency Act and the existence of the agency and its member units, but reversed the judgment insofar as it confirmed and declared valid the master contract and the member-unit contracts. * Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Proceedings by irrigation districts for confirmation of contracts between them and the United States for delivery of water from the Central Valley Project to the districts and for construction of a distribution system to make the water available on lands within the district.
Ivanhoe Irrigation Dist. v. All Parties & Persons, 47 Cal. 2d 597, 306 P.2d 824, 1957 Cal. LEXIS 293 (1957)



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for objecting defendants, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant irrigation districts sought review of the judgments from the Superior Court of Tulare and Madera Counties (California), which denied confirmation of contracts between the districts and the United States for the delivery of water and the construction of a distribution system.


OVERVIEW: Appellant irrigation districts, acting under Cal. On certiorari, the Supreme Court reversed the court's order affirming the judgments denying confirmation of the contracts between the districts and the federal government. Proceeding in mandamus to restrain the Municipal Court of Los Angeles County from proceeding with a trial.

OVERVIEW: The ordinance under which defendant was being prosecuted required her to register with the city as a known criminal.
