Abbott v. Los Angeles


L. A. No. 25657


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 674; 349 P.2d 974; 3 Cal. Rptr. 158; 1960 Cal. LEXIS 244; 82 A.L.R.2d 385



February 26, 1960 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 23, 1960. Tobriner, Judge Pro Tem., * Participated Therein in Place of White, J., Who Deemed Himself Disqualified.


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Newcomb Condee, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief and for an injunction.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for defendants, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a convicted criminal, challenged decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld the constitutionality of respondent city's criminal registration ordinance, Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code §§ 52.38 to 52.53 (ordinance), in his action for declaratory relief and for an injunction against respondents, a city, its police chief, and a city attorney.


OVERVIEW: The convicted criminal filed an action for declaratory relief and for an injunction to prevent enforcement of the ordinance. He alleged that the ordinance violated the Due Process Clause, the Privileges and Immunities Clause, and the Equal Protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. He also alleged the ordinance violated Cal. Const. art I, §§ 4, 11, 13 and 21 and art. IV, § 25(33). The trial court found the ordinance was constitutional, both on its face and as applied to the convicted criminal. On appeal, the court found the rules related to municipalities' police power under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 11 demonstrated the city was without power to enact the ordinance. The court found that the ordinance conflicted with state law. The state preempted the field of criminal registration by passing sex offender registration statutes that were less extreme than the city's, by passing laws for habitual offenders, and by clearing criminal records. The overall requirement of criminal registration for more than sex offenders placed the ordinance within a field that the state preempted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the finding upholding the constitutionality of the city's criminal registration ordinance and remanded.

 

384 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Vallerga v. Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control


S. F. No. 20285


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 313; 347 P.2d 909; 1 Cal. Rptr. 494; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 349



December 23, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 20, 1960. Dooling, J. Pro Tem., * Participated Therein in Place of Peters, J., Who Deemed Himself Disqualified.


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Donald K. Quayle, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to set aside its revocation of a liquor license.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment denying writ, reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensees challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that denied their application for mandamus to compel respondent Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to set aside its revocation of their liquor license. The license was revoked for a violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24200(e).


OVERVIEW: A hearing officer found that the licensees' premises were established as a resort for lesbians and homosexuals and determined that grounds were therefore established to revoke the license under § 24200(e) and also that to continue the license would have been contrary to public welfare and morals within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22. The court reversed the judgment with directions. Caselaw interpreting other statutes had held that mere proof of patronage by homosexuals, without proof of the commission of illegal or immoral acts on the premises, was not sufficient to justify revocation of a license. The language of § 24200(e), however, unambiguously made such conduct grounds for revocation and could not be interpreted otherwise. It was therefore unconstitutional. The record did not justify the conclusion that the license's revocation could be justified on constitutional grounds. Although the record included evidence of public displays that manifested sexual desires by patrons of the premises, the revocation was not grounded on that evidence but on the finding that the premises were a resort for homosexuals. That reason was insufficient for revocation of the license.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment that denied the licensees' application for mandamus and directed the trial court to grant a peremptory writ of mandate directing the department to set aside its order revoking the license.

 

385 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dittus v. Cranston


Sac. No. 7096


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 284; 347 P.2d 671; 1 Cal. Rptr. 327; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 344



December 18, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the State Controller to honor a claim by the secretary of the State Board of Control.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Secretary of the California State Board of Control, filed a petition in mandamus, seeking to compel respondent, California State Controller, to honor her claim for a sum of money that had been appropriated to the Secretary by the California Legislature.


OVERVIEW: The legislature appropriated money to the Secretary for the payment of claims of fishermen and fish processing companies arising out of the enactment of two statutes in 1957. One statute, the anti-netting bill, closed certain waters to commercial fishing for shad and salmon, and the other statute, 1957 Cal. Stat. ch. 2099, declared that it was the public policy of the state to reimburse, where possible, persons whose boats, nets, and other fishing equipment were rendered valueless by the anti-netting bill. The court issued the writ, holding that because the appropriation promoted the purposes of the Fish and Game Preservation Fund, which were public in character, there was no violation of Cal. Const. art. IV, § 31, which prohibited a gift of public funds. Although the Board was not given the express authority to examine and adjust claims after an appropriation had been made, the court found that it was clearly implied.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandamus.

 

386 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


San Francisco v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S. F. No. 20333


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 236; 347 P.2d 294; 1 Cal. Rptr. 158; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 338



December 14, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of the Respondents and the Real Parties in Interest for a Rehearing was Denied January 13, 1960.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco and Orla St. Clair, Judge, thereof, from further proceedings in an action to compel a city board of permit appeals to set aside their order overruling an order of the city planning commission denying an application to erect an apartment house building.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the city and county of San Fransico and property owner, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Fransico, which overruled an action to compel a city board of permit appeals to set aside their order.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, the city and county of San Francisco and a property owner therein, sought prohibition to restrain the court from further proceedings in an action. The sole ultimate question was whether the superior court's order was an act in excess of jurisdiction, insofar as that term was used to indicate that those acts may be restrained by prohibition or annulled on certiorari. The court concluded under the pertinent statutes of the state, applicable charter provisions and ordinances of the city and county of San Francisco, and established decisional law, the superior court exceeded and proposed to exceed its jurisdiction and that petitioners were entitled to the relief sought. The court held that the standards administered by the board of permit appeals were, as a matter of law, precisely the same standards which were administered at the primary level by the planning commission, and that such standards were lawful and adequate in the premises.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining the superior court from any further proceedings in the action and vacated the temporary restraining order.

 

387 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Allied Properties v. Dept. of Alcoholic Beverage Control


S. F. No. 20236


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 141; 346 P.2d 737; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 329



November 30, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Albert C. Wollenberg, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to vacate a liquor license suspension.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment granting writ, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of alcoholic beverage control challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held that §§ 55.5, 55.6, 55.65 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, 1935 Cal. Stat. ch. 330, p. 1123, and Cal. Admin. Code tit. 4, § 99(a), (b), (f), were unconstitutional. Appellee licensee's mandamus proceeding challenged the department's suspension of its liquor license.


OVERVIEW: The liquor licensee filed a proceeding in mandamus to challenge a temporary suspension of its license by the department. The trial court held that Cal. Admin. Code tit. 4, § 99(a), (b), (f), and §§ 55.5, 55.6, 55.65 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act (act), 1935 Cal. Stat. ch. 330, p. 1123, which were codified at 1937 Cal. Stat. ch. 758, p. 2173, 1947 Cal. Stat. ch. 657, p. 1698, 1949 Cal. Stat. ch. 574, p. 1064, were unconstitutional. The department appealed, and the court reversed. The act's purposes, which were to prevent price cutting in the sale of alcoholic beverages and to promote temperance, were proper legislative objectives. The means of effectuating the act's purposes, by having each producer or wholesaler establish the retail price of its own brand, were reasonable, and the classification made by regulating retail, but not wholesale, prices was reasonable because the legislature could reasonably conclude that the larger number of retailers made price stabilization more necessary than for wholesalers. There was no unlawful delegation of legislative power because private contracts were not legislative in character. The provisions were therefore not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment, which had held certain provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act and the California Administrative Code unconstitutional, in a licensee's mandamus proceeding challenging the department's suspension of his liquor license.

 

388 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Benford


Crim. No. 6522


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 2d 1; 345 P.2d 928; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 314



November 6, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Edwin L. Jefferson, Judge.

Prosecution for unlawfully selling, furnishing and giving away marijuana.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed; application to take additional testimony on appeal, denied. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A criminal defendant appealed from his conviction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for unlawfully selling, furnishing, and giving away marijuana, a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. Defendant, who had previously been convicted of this same offense, asserted that the testimony of a police officer established entrapment and on appeal sought the introduction of additional evidence to establish that defense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for unlawfully selling, furnishing, and giving away marijuana, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500, an offense for which he had previously been convicted. The court affirmed the lower court's decision, denying defendant's application requesting that the testimony of a police officer be admitted on appeal in order to establish the defense of entrapment. In reaching this decision, the court refused to decide whether an appellate court in a criminal case could request the addition of evidence, but found that defendant had failed to establish that the requested evidence was unavailable at trial or would be anything other than cumulative. The court noted that under state law, evidence that a defendant had previously committed similar crimes or had a reputation of engaging in such crimes was not admissible on the issue of entrapment. It therefore found that the prosecution's inability to prove defendant's prior criminality was of little significance. The court also found that an officer's actions in allowing defendant the opportunity to commit a crime did not constitute entrapment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

 

389 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Jensen v. Traders & General Ins. Co.


S. F. No. 20212


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 786; 345 P.2d 1; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 249



October 23, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Cecil Mosbacher, Judge.

Action to recover on an automobile liability insurance policy.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judgment holders sought review from a judgment that was entered in favor of defendant insurance company after a jury trial in the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) in an action to recover damages for personal injuries sustained in an auto accident.


OVERVIEW: The judgment holders filed an action against the insurance company to collect a judgment from the insurance company's insured for injuries that arose from an automobile accident. The judgment holders contended that the trial court erred when it instructed the jury that if the insurance company had given a cancellation notice to its insured in accordance with its policy language, the policy ceased to remain in effect. The judgment holders contended that the insurance company's cancellation clause was ambiguous and contrary to the state's public policy. They also contended that the insurance company's failure to return or tender the unearned premium rendered the cancellation ineffective. The court disagreed with the judgment holders' contentions. The court concluded that the language of the cancellation clause was plain and unambiguous and it did not violate the state's public policy as expressed through the legislature. The court also pointed out that policy language made return of the unearned premium a consequence of cancellation rather than a condition precedent thereto.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

390 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Konigsberg v. State Bar


L. A. No. 23266


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 769; 344 P.2d 777; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 248



October 16, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied November 10, 1959. Gibson, C. J., Deeming Himself Disqualified, did not Participate Therein. Draper, J., Sat Pro Tempore * in Place of the Chief Justice. Traynor, Acting C. J., and Peters, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


* Assigned by Acting Chairman of Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review action of the Committee of Bar Examiners in refusing to certify petitioner for admission to practice law and application to the Supreme Court for admission to practice.



DISPOSITION: Petition for review and application to Supreme Court denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bar applicant sought review of the decision of the Committee of Bar Examiners (California), which refused to certify the applicant for admission to practice law under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6064. The applicant's application to join the bar had been previously denied, affirmed without opinion, and then reversed and remanded by the United States Supreme Court. The court remanded it to the bar examiners.


OVERVIEW: After passing the bar exam, the applicant was denied admission to the bar when he would not answer questions related to his involvement with the Communist party. On appeal, the court affirmed. Under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6046, the bar examiners reviewed applications and certified them for admission and the court admitted the certified applicants, § 6064. No one who advocated the overthrow of the government should be certified under § 6064.1. On his first application, the bar examiners had determined that the applicant had not shown he had good moral character as required by § 6060(c) or that he did not advocate the overthrow of the government. In its reversal, the United States Supreme Court held that there was no evidence in the record justifying the bar examiners' findings and noted that he was not denied admission for refusing to answer questions. On remand, the bar examiners refused to certify the applicant for his refusal to answer questions it found to be material. The court found that because the refusal to certify was based upon the applicant's refusal to answer questions about his activities, and not upon those activities themselves, it was proper.


OUTCOME: The court denied the applicant's petition for review of the bar examiners' decision and denied his direct application for admission.

People v. Merkouris


Crim. No. 6403


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 672; 344 P.2d 1; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 238



September 16, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 15, 1959. Gibson, C. J., Traynor, J., and Peters, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (d)) from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Lewis Drucker, Judge.

Prosecution for murder.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of first degree murder imposing the death penalty, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(d), defendant automatically appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of first-degree murder and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence to convict him, and that the trial court should have held that it entertained "doubt" as to his sanity at time of trial, under Cal. Penal Code § 1368. The court found the evidence sufficient, because reasonable inferences of guilt could have been drawn therefrom. The court also found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in concluding that reports from the institution where defendant had been confined that he was sane were more persuasive than outward manifestations that he was not. The court also found that hearsay statements of the victims, testimony regarding defendant's indictment for sending threatening letters through the mail, and testimony regarding statements of defendant's brother were properly admitted because these was relevant to intent and not prejudicial and because defendant had not objected in the courts below.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that there was sufficient evidence to support guilty verdicts, that the trial court was correct in not holding as a matter of law that it entertained "doubt" as to defendant's sanity, and that the trial court properly admitted certain testimony into evidence.

 

392 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Union Interchange, Inc. v. Savage


L. A. No. 24992


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 601; 342 P.2d 249; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 231



August 10, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County denying motion to vacate a preliminary injunction. Bayard Rhone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant officials appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), denying a motion to vacate a preliminary injunction granted to plaintiff businesses to stay enforcement of certain amendments to the California Business and Professions Code pending a decision on the merits in a declaratory judgment action.


OVERVIEW: The businesses filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief, seeking a declaration that their business operations were outside the scope of legislation relating to the regulation of real estate and business opportunities transactions or, in the alternative, that the legislation was unconstitutional. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction to the businesses. No appeal was taken. Two years later, the officials presented a motion to vacate the preliminary injunction. The trial court denied the motion and the court affirmed. The court held that the denial of the motion to vacate was not an abuse of discretion and that the trial court was not required to make a specific reservation of jurisdiction in its order granting the preliminary injunction. The court held that the trial court had an inherent power to modify or vacate the injunction upon a showing that there had been a change in the controlling facts upon which the injunction rested, that the law had been changed, modified or extended, or where there the ends of justice would be served by modification. The court found no change of circumstances since the injunction was originally issued.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order denying the officials' motion to vacate the preliminary injunction.

People v. Chessman


Crim. No. 5006


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 467; 341 P.2d 679; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 223



July 7, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 5, 1959.
Writ of certiorari denied Chessman v. California, 361 U.S. 925, 80 S. Ct. 296, 4 L. Ed. 2d 241, 1959 U.S. LEXIS 21 (1959)
Writ of certiorari denied Chessman v. Dickson, 362 U.S. 965, 80 S. Ct. 883, 4 L. Ed. 2d 879, 1960 U.S. LEXIS 1297 (1960)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (automatically taken under Pen. Code, § 1239, subd. (b)) from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Charles W. Fricke, Judge.

Prosecution for first degree robbery, grand theft, kidnaping for purpose of robbery, and other offenses.
People v. Chessman, 38 Cal. 2d 166, 238 P.2d 1001, 1951 Cal. LEXIS 198 (1951)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him on 17 felonies, denied his motion for a new trial, and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant on 17 felonies, denied his motion for a new trial, and sentenced him to death. On appeal, defendant contended that the transcript did not present a reasonably accurate and complete record of the evidence and other oral proceedings but was a fabrication of falsities constructed through the fraudulent and conspiratorial collaboration of the trial judge, the prosecuting attorney, and a substitute court reporter. The court concluded that the corrected report's transcript was substantially accurate and sufficiently complete in every respect to permit a fair review of the appeal from the judgments of conviction. The court rejected defendant's arguments that the court's acceptance of such transcript denied him constitutional rights. Further, the court held that defendant was not surprised or hindered in his defense by ensuing police testimony concerning his self-incriminating statements. The court held that the State had not imposed unconstitutionally cruel or unusual punishment on defendant. At no time in any proceeding during the entire history of the case had the State denied defendant the right to counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.

 

394 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


State Board of Education v. Levit


Sac. No. 7057


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 441; 343 P.2d 8; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 222



July 2, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 29, 1959.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in mandamus to compel the Director of Finance to comply with orders of the State Board of Education to print "Science for Work and Play" and "Science for Here and Now" which the board adopted for use as part of a uniform series of science textbooks for the elementary grades.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State Board of Education (board) brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent State Director of Finance (director) to comply with the board's orders to print certain science textbooks, which the board adopted for use as part of a uniform series of science textbooks for the elementary grades.


OVERVIEW: The board claimed that the director violated the statutory duty imposed on him by Cal. Gov't Code § 13570 and Cal. Educ. Code §§ 4801, 11221, in refusing to order the printing of the books. The director contended that he had no duty to execute the printing orders on the ground that the printing contract did not become effective because he did not approve it and that no funds had been appropriated for the textbooks due to a restrictive budget provision. The board argued that the legislature could not validly interfere with the ultimate selection of textbooks made by the board by a restrictive provision in the budget. The court held that the restrictive budget provision violated the prohibitions of Cal. Const. art. IX, § 7 because it was not merely a curtailment of curriculum but was a prohibition against the particular books named and the legislature could not take away the board's right of ultimate selection. Because the director originally took the position that he would honor the printing requisitions if the restrictive provision were held invalid, the court limited the writ to a consideration of the requisition without regard to or consideration of the budget restriction.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ ordering the director to consider the board's printing orders without regard to the ineffective restrictive budget provision.

Stephenson v. City of Palm Springs


L. A. No. 24645


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 407; 340 P.2d 1009; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 216; 44 L.R.R.M. 2371; 37 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P65,596



June 26, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County. Hilton H. McCabe, Judge.

Action to enjoin a municipality and its chief of police from enforcing a "right-to-work" ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiffs affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city and its chief of police, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California). The trial court permanently enjoined them from enforcing a right-to-work ordinance enacted by a city council after plaintiff union members challenged the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: A city council passed an ordinance that declared as illegal all closed and union shop agreements. A union member, who brought an action individually and on behalf of the union and its members, alleged that union members were threatened with criminal prosecutions for having entered into a collective bargaining agreement that required union membership as a condition of employment. The union members sought preliminary injunctive relief. The trial court permanently enjoined the city and the chief of police from enforcing the ordinance. The court affirmed. Under Cal. Lab. Code §§ 920- 923, 1115-1122, and 1126, closed and union shop agreements were encouraged and protected. The city council thus had improperly enacted a police measure that conflicted with state laws. The ordinance's stated intention in a severability provision to avoid forbidden conflict was contravened by the state laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that permanently enjoined a city and a chief of police from enforcing a right-to-work ordinance.

 

396 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Katzev v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. No. 25012


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 360; 341 P.2d 310; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 211



June 24, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 22, 1959.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. John K. Otis, Judge pro tem. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Action for declaratory relief attacking as unconstitutional a county ordinance prohibiting the sale or circulation of crime comic books to persons under 18.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment adverse to plaintiffs, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, book and magazine dealers, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held against them in their action for declaratory relief attacking as unconstitutional a county ordinance prohibiting the sale or circulation of crime comic books to persons under 18.


OVERVIEW: A county ordinance prohibited the sale of so-called "crime comic books." The superior court upheld the ordinance. The court reversed. The ordinance was an unjustifiable abridgement of the right to freedom of the press because no showing was made of a clear and present danger of a substantive evil justifying the suppression of the comics. The record failed to disclose a close, causal connection between the substantive evil claimed, juvenile delinquency, and the circulation of crime comic books in general. Further, the ordinance was too broad because it failed to draw any distinction between various accounts of crime. As written, Bugs Bunny, Woody Woodpecker, and Treasure Island accounts would have been forbidden by the ordinance. In addition, the ordinance denied the dealers equal protection because it established arbitrary and unreasonable exemptions. Finally, the ordinance failed to establish a clearly denied standard of guilt and, therefore, violated standards of due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the adverse judgment against the dealers.

 

397 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Chavez v. Sargent


S. F. No. 19863


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 162; 339 P.2d 801; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 195; 44 L.R.R.M. 2134; 37 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P65,476



May 19, 1959 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Benito County. Stanley Lawson, Judge. *


* Assigned by Chairman of Judicial Council.

Employer's action for injunctive relief from the activities of a labor union and its secretary, designed to induce the employer to enter into a union shop agreement with the union allegedly in violation of a county "right-to-work" ordinance.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after sustaining a general demurrer to the complaint and refusal to amend, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employer filed a complaint against defendant union and its secretary seeking to enjoin the union from demanding that the employer enter into a union shop agreement that allegedly violated a county "right-to-work" ordinance. The employer appealed from a judgment of dismissal entered by the Superior Court of San Benito County (California) after a general demurrer to his complaint was sustained and he had declined to amend.


OVERVIEW: The employer, supported by a majority of his employees, desired to enter into a union shop agreement with the union, but a local ordinance prohibited such agreements. The employer sought injunctive relief after the union demanded that it sign the agreement. The court rejected the union's argument that the ordinance was invalid because the field of labor relations had been preempted by federal laws and concluded that the state's regulation of the field was not so complete as to preclude local regulations. However, the court found that the ordinance was invalid because it contained unseverable provisions which conflicted with the legislatively declared general labor policy of the state supporting the full freedom of employees for self-organization and for voluntarily selecting, and negotiating agreements through, their own employee committees or agencies. Therefore, the court concluded that the employer did not have a cause of action under the ordinance. Furthermore, the court found that the proposed agreement was a lawfully negotiated union shop contract and that the means by which the union sought to reach its objective were lawful.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision dismissing the employer's request for injunctive relief.

SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

399 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Texas Co. v. County of Los Angeles


L. A. Nos. 24968, 25102


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 2d 55; 338 P.2d 440; 1959 Cal. LEXIS 180; 10 Oil & Gas Rep. 714



April 24, 1959 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied May 20, 1959. McComb, J., was of the Opinion that the Petitions Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. H. Eugene Breitenbach and John F. Aiso, Judges.

Actions to recover taxes on possessory interests in tax-exempt lands owned by a city.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments for defendants after their demurrers were sustained, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporations challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which had ruled that the county board of equalization erred in sustaining assessments against the corporations based on the present worth of the use of land for the unexpired term of a lease.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporations brought these actions to recover taxes on possessory interests in tax-exempt lands owned by the city. Judgments for defendants were entered after their demurrers were sustained, and plaintiffs appealed. The issue was whether the value of a possessory interest in tax-exempt land under the capitalization of income method of valuation was the present worth of the use of the land for the unexpired term of the lease or that worth less the present worth of rentals to become due. Plaintiffs contended that the county board of equalization erred in sustaining assessments based on the present worth of the use of the land for the unexpired term of the lease. The court affirmed. No distinction could have been made between rent and the cost of improvements that reverted to the lessor, for both were part of the purchase price of the leasehold, and no deduction could be made for that purchase price. The court held that a leasehold is not less valuable because it has not been paid for in advance, and to draw a distinction between rent paid and rent to be paid confuses the equity the lessee has in the leasehold with its value.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the trial court, which had denied the corporations relief in their attempt to recover taxes on possessory interests in tax-exempt lands owned by the city.

 

400 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Petersen


Crim. No. 6215


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 2d 177; 331 P.2d 24; 1958 Cal. LEXIS 220; 77 A.L.R.2d 1291



October 31, 1958 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 26, 1958.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from custody following his arrest for violation of San Francisco, Cal., Police Code §§ 1119 and 1156.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was arrested after parking his taxicab in a stand designated for another's exclusive use, in violation of San Francisco, Cal., Police Code §§ 1119 and 1156, which established exclusive stands and prohibited others from standing therein. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus, challenging the ordinances' constitutionality. The court rejected respondent municipality's argument that the remedy was improper where petitioner was released on bail, as petitioner remained a constructive prisoner subject to restraint by penal authorities. However, the court discharged petitioner's writ by finding that the ordinances were a proper exercise of police power. The ordinances were rationally related to facilitating police supervision and preventing disorderly and aggressive passenger solicitation by competing companies. Furthermore, granting stands to one permittee was not invalidly discriminatory. Since respondent could deny use of its streets to all but one carrier, it could validly direct that each of several taxicab companies use separate stands.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's writ was discharged, as ordinances providing for exclusive taxicab stands and prohibiting their use by others was a valid exercise of respondent municipality's police powers.
